Credit Markets
For Virgin Islands, Trouble in Paradise --- U.S. territory faces rising debt and pension obligations, and big budget gap

By Heather Gillers 
728 words
27 January 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B10
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A U.S. territory famed for its white-sand beaches and azure waters is in a precarious financial position. This time, it isn't just Puerto Rico.

The U.S. Virgin Islands shares many of the same fiscal problems as its Caribbean neighbor 80 miles to the west: high levels of debt, mounting pension obligations and a declining population.

Local legislators on Tuesday are expected to consider a new round of taxes as a way to shore up the Virgin Islands' $110 million budget deficit. That follows an unsuccessful attempt this month to sell about $220 million in bonds, the second time in two months the territory has called off a planned bond sale.

Virgin Islands Gov. Kenneth Mapp said the territory, with a population of some 105,000 people, has no problems making payments on its $2.2 billion in debt obligations. But he predicted widespread service cuts and layoffs if new bonds can't be sold.

Moody's Investors Service on Tuesday downgraded certain Virgin Islands bonds by three notches further into junk and cited "an increased possibility that the government may be forced to restructure its debt to address its financial problems."

"I think this is a critical juncture," said Nikolao Pula, director of the Office of Insular Affairs, which coordinates federal policy for several U.S. territories as part of the Interior Department.

Any failure by the Virgin Islands to pay back money owed to investors could intensify competition for limited government dollars inside the territory.

Virgin Islands bonds have traditionally appealed to mainland bondholders and U.S. mutual funds because they are exempt from state taxes. U.S. mutual funds hold nearly $1 billion in Virgin Islands bonds, according to research firm Morningstar Inc.

The mutual fund with the highest percentage of its portfolio invested in Virgin Islands debt is made up largely of Wisconsin investors. Wells Fargo Asset Management, which oversees the Wells Fargo Wisconsin Tax Free C fund, said it is protecting itself from the Virgin Islands' financial situation by holding debt that is either insured or coming due within the next 18 months. More than 8% of its holdings is debt issued by the U.S. territory.

At Western Asset Management, based in Pasadena, Calif., which also holds Virgin Islands bonds, senior analyst Fred Poon said he expects the Virgin Islands legislature will eventually enact a "sin tax" on cigarettes and alcohol, as well as other revenue-raising measures. "Who wants to argue against taxes on sin?" Mr. Poon said.

Including the islands of St. Thomas, St. John and St. Croix, the Virgin Islands has long struggled with budget deficits, but its problems accelerated after the 2008 economic downturn.

First, the economy, long dependent on tourism, took a hit. Annual expenditures by visitors fell 19% in the period from 2007 to 2013, a drop of $280 million. The territory's population shrank by almost 9% over that period.

Another blow came in 2012 when one of the territory's biggest employers shut down. The Hovensa oil refinery on the island of St. Croix employed 1,200 workers and 960 contractors, according to a report by Fitch Ratings at the time.

The closing was expected to deprive the government of an estimated $100 million in annual revenue, according to a 2012 report from the then-governor.

With less revenue, the territory has relied increasingly on bond proceeds to pay operating costs while contributing less to pension plans. That borrowing has increased its debt to a level similar to that of Puerto Rico, on a per capita basis.

The pension plan has only 27% of what it needs to pay future benefits, according to 2015 financial statements.

Some analysts expect the territory to eventually turn to Washington, D.C., for help, as Puerto Rico did in 2016. Congress last year passed legislation that provides Puerto Rico with a stay against creditor litigation in exchange for oversight from a seven-member board that controls the territory's finances and approves any court-supervised debt restructuring.

A similar solution for the Virgin Islands "is totally possible within the next five years," said Matt Fabian, a partner with research firm Municipal Market Analytics.

COMPANIES
Job losses in compliance loom as banks harness power of automation; Financials

By Martin Arnold, Banking Editor 
501 words
26 January 2017
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Thousands of jobs will be put at risk as the biggest banks harness artificial intelligence systems to the wave of roles created in recent years to meet ever-growing regulatory demands, industry experts have warned.

New technologies mean that banks could make vast savings in compliance, according to Richard Lumb , head of financial services at Accenture, who estimated that "thousands of roles" in the banks' internal policing could be replaced by automated systems.

"We are seeing work with clients today which is very much around big data and robotic process automation, where in compliance - take anti-money laundering - you can take out thousands of roles," Mr Lumb told the Financial Times. "That is coming quite quickly now and that will sweep across the industry."

He said that many of the jobs created by banks for compiling and checking data on customers and transactions had already been moved offshore to lower-cost countries. In the next wave of automation they will simply disappear.

"Companies have really thrown bodies at this to deal with the demands of the regulators," he said. "But now we are shifting from a revolution of labour arbitrage and offshore to a revolution of automation around this."

Citigroup estimates that the biggest banks have doubled the number of people they employ to handle compliance and regulation. This now costs the banking industry $270bn a year and accounts for 10 per cent of operating costs.

In a report called "digital disruption" sent to clients this week, Citi said there was a "huge cost take-out opportunity for financial institutions" from regulatory technology, or regtech .

Ronit Ghose, a banks analyst at Citi and co-author of the report, said: "So much stuff in banks is still paper-based. That is just archaic and with the advent of artificial intelligence and biometric identification it has to change."

Spanish bank BBVA recently estimated that, on average, financial institutions have 10 per cent to 15 per cent of their staff dedicated to this area. This heavy investment has been necessary in response to the crackdown by regulators that followed the financial crisis. European and US banks have paid more than $150bn in litigation and conduct charges since 2011, Citi estimated.

The US bank said that, ultimately, banks would benefit from pooling their resources in compliance. "Over the longer term, a nationwide [know your customer] utility could be beneficial to the whole of society, and many regulators and governments are working towards this ideal," it said.

However, progress has been slow on persuading big US and European banks to sign up to the private sector projects offering to provide a customer identity-checking utility service to the sector.

Lance Uggla, president of IHS Markit, a financial data provider that is offering a KYC utility service, said he was "disappointed" at the lack of progress but the concept remained "very strong" and the expectation of success was "intact".

Markets
Welcome to the Virgin Islands, One of the Most Indebted Places in the U.S. The U.S. territory is running out of options as it faces rising debt and pension obligations, a declining population and tepid response to proposed new bond offerings

By Heather Gillers 
1,130 words
26 January 2017
20:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A U.S. territory famed for its white-sand beaches and azure waters is in a precarious financial position. This time, it isn't just Puerto Rico.

The U.S. Virgin Islands shares many of the same fiscal problems as its Caribbean neighbor 80 miles to the west: high levels of debt, mounting pension obligations and a declining population.

Local legislators on Tuesday are expected to consider a new round of taxes as a way to shore up the Virgin Islands' $110 million budget deficit. That follows an unsuccessful attempt this month to sell about $220 million in bonds, the second time in two months the territory has called off a planned bond sale.

Virgin Islands Gov. Kenneth Mapp said the territory, with a population of some 105,000 people, has no problems making payments on its $2.2 billion in debt obligations. But he predicted widespread service cuts and layoffs if new bonds can't be sold.

Moody's Investors Service on Tuesday downgraded certain Virgin Islands bonds by three notches further into junk and cited "an increased possibility that the government may be forced to restructure its debt to address its financial problems."

"I think this is a critical juncture," said Nikolao Pula, director of the Office of Insular Affairs, which coordinates federal policy for several U.S. territories as part of the Interior Department. "We just hope whatever decision they make will be good moving forward."

Any failure by the Virgin Islands to pay back money owed to investors could intensify competition for limited government dollars inside the territory.

Virgin Islands bonds have traditionally appealed to mainland bondholders and U.S. mutual funds because they are exempt from state taxes. U.S. mutual funds hold nearly $1 billion in Virgin Islands bonds, according to research firm Morningstar Inc.

The mutual fund with the highest percentage of its portfolio invested in Virgin Islands debt is made up largely of Wisconsin investors. Wells Fargo Asset Management, which oversees the Wells Fargo Wisconsin Tax Free C fund, said it is protecting itself from the Virgin Islands' financial situation by holding debt that is either insured or coming due within the next 18 months. More than 8% of its holdings is debt issued by the U.S. territory.

At Western Asset Management, based in Pasadena, Calif., which also holds Virgin Islands bonds, senior analyst Fred Poon said he expects the Virgin Islands legislature will eventually enact a "sin tax" on cigarettes and alcohol, as well as other revenue-raising measures.

"Who wants to argue against taxes on sin?" Mr. Poon said.

Including the islands of St. Thomas, St. John and St. Croix, the Virgin Islands has long struggled with budget deficits, but its problems accelerated after the 2008 economic downturn.

First, the economy, long dependent on tourism, took a hit. Annual expenditures by visitors fell 19% in the period from 2007 to 2013, a drop of $280 million. The territory's population shrank by almost 9% over that period.

Another blow came in 2012 when one of the territory's biggest employers shut down. The Hovensa oil refinery on the island of St. Croix employed 1,200 workers and 960 contractors, according to a report by Fitch Ratings at the time. The closing was expected to deprive the government of an estimated $100 million in annual revenue, according to a 2012 report from the then-governor.

With less revenue, the territory has relied increasingly on bond proceeds to pay operating costs while contributing less to pension plans. That borrowing has increased its debt to a level similar to that of Puerto Rico, on a per capita basis.

The lower pension contributions widened the funding gap for a retirement system that covers 9,303 current workers and 8,465 retirees and past workers, according to the fund's 2015 financial reports.

That pension plan has only 27% of what it needs to pay future benefits, according to 2015 financial statements; a 2015 analysis by Segal Consulting predicted the fund would run out of money by 2024.

The governor said he plans to unveil proposed pension fixes this year and hold hearings on the topic. "We need to come up with a strategy," Mr. Mapp said.

Another strain is that U.S. territories, including the Virgin Islands, typically receive less federal money than U.S. states, according to Rep. Stacey Plaskett, the nonvoting U.S. congresswoman representing the Virgin Islands. For example, the federal government caps the amount of Medicaid payments the territories receive. "Our disparity of treatment is I believe what has caused these financial issues to occur," Rep. Plaskett said.

Some analysts expect the territory to eventually turn to Washington, D.C., for help, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/puerto-ricos-drastic-population-loss-deepens-its-economic-crisis-1467219467". Congress last year passed "http://www.wsj.com/articles/president-obama-signs-puerto-rico-debt-relief-bill-1467321998"in exchange for oversight from a seven-member board that controls the territory's finances and approves any court-supervised debt restructuring.

A similar solution for the Virgin Islands "is totally possible within the next five years," said Matt Fabian, a partner with research firm Municipal Market Analytics.

But a cash crunch could come much sooner. Ken Kurtz of Moody's predicts the territory will need to sell bonds within the next few months to keep from running out of operating cash.

Interest in Virgin Islands bonds has been scarce ever since Congress authorized debt-relief legislation for Puerto Rico last June. Bondholders showed lukewarm interest in a Jan. 11 offering, even after the governor requested that federal funds backing the bonds be wired directly to an escrow agent, to alleviate concerns that the Virgin Islands would divert the money if the financial situation worsens.

The Virgin Islands Public Finance Authority received orders for slightly more than 60% of the bonds after offering an interest rate "in the 6% range," according to Mr. Mapp. The authority opted to cancel the deal rather than reduce its size, he said. A spokesman for the authority said it is no longer trying to sell bonds and will assess whether to re-enter the market "when appropriate credit and acceptable interest rates are present."

About $55 million of the $219 million offering would have been used to close a gap in this year's $1.35 billion budget, the governor said. If the Virgin Islands can't close that gap, "this is not sustainable," Mr. Mapp said. "We can't continue doing this."

Pro Private Markets
Chris Lynch Is Leaving Accomplice VC; The Boston venture firm, which was created out of Atlas Venture, raised a $200 million fund in 2015

By Yuliya Chernova 
200 words
26 January 2017
22:20
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Chris Lynch, a general partner at Accomplice VC, is leaving the firm, according to a person familiar with the situation.

The Cambridge, Mass.-based venture firm, which was created out of Atlas Venture, raised a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120150316eb3go1729" in 2015. Ryan Moore and Jeff Fagnan are the other general partners at the firm. Jon Karlen had left Accomplice last year.

Representatives of the firm didn't respond to a request for comment.

Mr. Lynch was focused on enterprise software, big data and security. Some of his companies included DataRobot, a data-science startup that raised a "http://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFVW00120160211ec2bb0w5m" in 2015, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/funding-snapshot-cloud-security-startup-threat-stack-raises-15-3m-series-b-1461160206" startup Threat Stack, as well as DataCamp, Acetti Software and Squirrl.

He joined Atlas Venture in 2012. He has served as president and chief executive of data storage startup Vertica Systems, acquired by HP, as well as of Acopia Networks, a file virtualization startup, acquired by F5 Networks.

Accomplice's portfolio companies include DraftKings, Carbon Black, PillPack, Hopper and others.

Equities: Goldman Set Pace for Dow Climb --- Industrial giants gave a final shove to 20000 after a long stock rally led by bank's advance

By John Carney 
607 words
26 January 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B11
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

When it came to Dow 20000, Goldman Sachs Group Inc. drove the index to the five-yard line. But International Business Machines Corp. and Boeing Co. got it over the goal line.

In the immediate aftermath of the presidential election, Goldman Sachs powered the Dow Jones Industrial Average higher. It was the biggest contributor to its historic drive to the 20000 level, contributing 21.9% of the gain the index notched since Nov. 8.

Of the Dow's 1735.77-point rise since the election, Goldman accounted for 378.91 points.

Goldman's rally -- the shares are up 30.4% since the election -- came as financial stocks overall rose sharply on hopes Donald Trump's administration would loosen regulatory shackles on banks, cut taxes and pursue pro-growth policies. And, importantly, all this would hopefully lead to higher interest rates, which help improve bank profitability.

Indeed, gains for the KBW Nasdaq Bank index since Election Day are more than double those of the Dow.

Mirroring investors' optimistic view, Bank of America Corp. Chief Executive Brian Moynihan said Wednesdaythat the U.S. economy was "on very sound footing," and that consumer and business confidence had jumped since the election.

"In less than a week you're seeing things happen," Mr. Moynihan said, referring to initiatives by Mr. Trump. That could bolster consumer and business confidence, although "now we will have to see how that will play out," Mr. Moynihan added in remarks to the Council on Foreign Relations in New York.

Mr. Moynihan has good reason to be cheery. Bank of America has gained even more ground than Goldman. Its 35% rise since the election is the best of any big-bank stock.

But Bank of America isn't in the Dow. And as Bank of America's rally gained further ground in mid-January on the back of its fourth-quarter results, Goldman lost steam. While the bank reported fourth-quarter earnings that beat analyst expectations, investors seemed to have already priced into financial stocks many of the benefits they expect to see in 2017.

At that point, IBM and Boeing stepped in to keep the Dow's momentum going. Those stocks were the second- and third-biggest contributors, respectively, to the Dow's rise since Election Day.

Much of their gains came this month, though. They contributed more than 80 points to the Dow's nearly 300-point rise so far in 2017.

Boeing gained 4.2% on Wednesday alone after the aerospace giant reported earnings that topped analyst forecasts. IBM is up more than 7% since the start of the year as the company attempts a transformation of its cloud-computing and artificial-intelligence businesses.

Although it flagged into the finish, Goldman remained the only Dow component to account for more than 10% of its increase since the election.

The ascent of Goldman's shares has helped lift the value of its executives' stock-based compensation plans. It has left the stock within striking distance of its all-time closing high of$247.92. That was hit in October 2007.

But Goldman isn't likely to dwell on its Dow victory. Speaking on the bank's earnings call earlier this month, finance chief Harvey Schwartz said he is "always rooting for the share price." But that, he added, isn't going to lead to Goldman "thinking about changing how we run the company."

---

Liz Hoffman contributed to this article.

Pro Private Markets
Accel's Steve Loughlin on Breaking Through the Noise in SaaS; RelateIQ's co-founder shares his outlook on investing, Salesforce and artificial intelligence 

By Patience Haggin 
537 words
26 January 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Steve Loughlin co-founded RelateIQ Inc., which Salesforce.com acquired for $390 million in 2014. Last year he left Salesforce to become a partner at Accel Partners, which was an early backer of RelateIQ. WSJ Pro Venture Capital spoke with Mr. Loughlin about bringing both his startup and corporate experience to investing in artificial intelligence-enabled software-as-a-service.

WSJ: You recently invested in Split Software. You worked with them at RelateIQ when they were developing the capability that later turned into their current product. What drew you to get involved as an investor?

I knew that team has been working on this technically, before it was a problem they really wanted to solve. It's about how can you deploy software and continue to play software in a really high-quality manner but also a manner in which it's responsive at the speed that customers and users expect. Having to respond to competitors' speed is actually just an incredible asset to have.

What's your investment thesis for sales and marketing investments?

There's this whole concept around how you scale these businesses, so you not only have the product, but you also have to build a sales and marketing engine around that. Where are you going to get your first $5 million in recurring revenue? And then $20 million, and then $100 million. Being precise about that is important because if you try and focus on every customer, you're not going to give a great experience.

With Salesforce, you've been a leader of their AI initiative. What special advantage is that give you as an investor?

There are certain products and problems that are more relevant to Salesforce, so it's having worked on the strategy with the team and knowing the places are that are going to be a high priority in the future. You're looking for things that are pretty early but are going to have high prospects in the future. The advantage is that I have a deep understanding of what's happening inside one of the larger companies, and at the same time kind of know what these emerging companies can do to find the white space in a very large category.

What areas of AI-enabled sales and marketing technology may be overhyped?

I wouldn't say there are any areas that are overhyped because I think there is a lot of opportunity. I would say one of the key things that startups and emerging companies need to think about is the value that they are creating for the customer and also where they sit in the priority of the customers' buying patterns. There are a lot of companies that have great features, but haven't connected to becoming great businesses. The challenge is: Are you a feature, a product, or company? The challenge is most startups start out as a feature, and then you have to have a plan to actually go from feature to product to company.

Write to Patience Haggin at "mailto:patience.haggin@wsj.com"

Heard on the Street
For U.S. Banks, U.K. Offers a Model

By Aaron Back 
504 words
26 January 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

Just what banking regulation will be like under President Donald Trump remains unclear. But recent comments by Treasury Secretary nominee Steven Mnuchin suggest he might be looking across the Atlantic to the U.K. for inspiration.

That isn't necessarily good news for investors in the biggest U.S. lenders.

In his Senate testimony last week and in written responses to senators this week, Mr. Mnuchin repeatedly raised the specter of some kind of "21st century Glass-Steagall," without giving any details.

Mr. Mnuchin clearly doesn't favor a simple return to the Great Depression-era law. In his written comments, Mr. Mnuchin said that "a bright line between commercial and investment banking. . .may inhibit the necessary lending and capital markets activities to support a robust economy."

So what does Mr. Mnuchin want? So far, he isn't saying. But one possibility would be a separation of retail bank operations such as that being put in place in the U.K., says Karen Petrou, analyst at Federal Financial Analytics.

Under this system, U.K. banks like Barclays and HSBC Holdings must establish separate subsidiaries that contain their U.K. operations serving individuals and small businesses, funded by government-guaranteed deposits. Their global investment-banking businesses stand outside this "ringfence" with their own capital, allowing them in theory to be wound down if they run into trouble without endangering the retail bank or necessitating a bailout.

Legislation along these lines could conceivably garner support from both parties.

Such a plan also could help Mr. Mnuchin square his seemingly contradictory comments on the Volcker rule. He has said banks with federally insured deposits shouldn't be taking trading risks with their own money, but also that trading restrictions on those banks have harmed market liquidity.

For universal banks like J.P. Morgan Chase, Citigroup and Bank of America, the potential implications are huge. They would have to decide if it is worth incurring the costs of undergoing a ringfencing process, including potentially higher overall capital requirements, or if it would be more efficient to just break up, potentially losing some synergies between units.

Either way, it will be a cumbersome process.

Another question is how the investment-banking units would be funded. It would be problematic if they were overly dependent on short-term wholesale finance, as Bear Stearns and Lehman Brothers Holdings were. One possible source of funding would be corporate deposits, which aren't insured.

U.S. banks don't necessarily want another regulatory revamp that forces further rethinking of fundamental business models, after years of adapting to current rules. Tweaks to the existing system, such as looser Volcker-rule enforcement, would suit them just fine.

But in today's political scene, big banks can't bet on getting exactly what they want. Until the regulatory outlook is clearer, investors should be mindful of the risks.

Banks  AI plans threaten thousands of jobs

Martin Arnold, Banking Editor 
563 words
25 January 2017
05:31
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Thousands of jobs will be put at risk as the world s biggest banks harness "https://www.ft.com/artificial-intelligence-robotics" systems to the wave of roles created in recent years to meet ever-growing regulatory demands, industry experts have warned.

New technologies mean that banks could make vast savings in compliance, according to Richard Lumb, head of financial services at Accenture, who estimated that  thousands of roles  in the banks  internal policing could be replaced by automated systems.

 We are seeing work with clients today which is very much around big data and robotic process automation, where in compliance   take anti-money laundering   you can take out thousands of roles,  Mr Lumb told the Financial Times at last week s World Economic Forum in Davos.  That is coming quite quickly now and that will sweep across the industry. 

He said many of the jobs created by banks in recent years for compiling and checking data on customers and transactions had already been moved offshore to lower-cost countries. In the next wave of automation they will simply disappear.

 Companies have really thrown bodies at this to deal with the demands of the regulators. They have had no option,  he said.  But now we are shifting from a revolution of labour arbitrage and offshore to a revolution of automation around this. 

Citigroup estimates that the biggest banks, including JPMorgan and HSBC, have doubled the number of people they employ to handle compliance and regulation. This now costs the banking industry $270bn a year and accounts for 10 per cent of operating costs.

In a report called  digital disruption  sent to clients this week, Citi said there was a  huge cost take-out opportunity for financial institutions  from the fast-growing area of regulatory technology, or regtech.

Ronit Ghose, banks analyst at Citi and co-author of the report, said:  So much stuff in banks is still paper-based. We are forever being asked to sign things. That is just archaic and with the advent of artificial intelligence and biometric identification it has to change. 

Spanish bank BBVA recently estimated that, on average, financial institutions have 10 to 15 per cent of their staff dedicated to this area. This heavy investment has been necessary in response to the crackdown by regulators that followed the financial crisis. European and US banks have paid more than $150bn in litigation and conduct charges since 2011, Citi estimated.

The US bank said that, ultimately, banks would benefit from pooling their resources in compliance.  Over the longer term, a nationwide [know your customer] utility could be beneficial to the whole of society, and many regulators and governments are working towards this ideal,  it said.

However, progress has been slow on persuading big US and European banks to sign up to the private sector projects offering to provide a customer identity-checking utility service to the sector.

Lance Uggla, president of IHS Markit, a financial data provider that is offering a KYC utility service, said he was  disappointed  at the lack of progress but the concept remained  very strong  and the expectation of success was  intact .

 The concept of a utility for KYC is very well embraced by financial market participants today as well as many other shared services they would like to create to reduce their cost structures,  he told the FT.

Heard on the Street
Markets
Under Trump, U.S. Banks Could Get a British Makeover; Recent comments by Steven Mnuchin suggest the U.K. could be a model for banking regulation under President Donald Trump

By Aaron Back 
538 words
25 January 2017
18:23
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Just what banking regulation will be like under President Donald Trump remains unclear. But recent comments by Treasury Secretary nominee Steven Mnuchin suggest he might be looking across the Atlantic to the U.K. for inspiration.

That isn't necessarily good news for investors in the biggest U.S. lenders.

In "http://www.wsj.com/articles/mnuchin-hearing-does-little-to-clear-trump-fog-of-uncertainty-1484861753"and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/treasury-nominee-steven-mnuchin-says-bank-regulation-should-be-tailored-to-activity-1485258397", Mr. Mnuchin repeatedly raised the specter of some kind of "21st Century Glass-Steagall," without giving any details.

Mr. Mnuchin clearly doesn't favor a simple return to the Great Depression-era law. In his written comments, Mr. Mnuchin said that "a bright line between commercial and investment banking &may inhibit the necessary lending and capital markets activities to support a robust economy."

So what does Mr. Mnuchin want? So far, he isn't saying. But one possibility would be a "ringfencing" of retail bank operations such as that being put in place in the U.K., says Karen Petrou, analyst at Federal Financial Analytics.

Under this system, U.K. banks like Barclays and HSBC"http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-k-banks-start-to-ring-in-the-changes-heard-on-the-street-1421769384" that contain their local U.K. operations serving individuals and small businesses, funded by government-guaranteed deposits. Their global investment-banking businesses stand outside this ringfence with their own capital, allowing them in theory to be wound down if they run into trouble without endangering the retail bank or necessitating a government bailout.

Legislation along these lines could conceivably garner support from both parties, avoiding a Democratic filibuster in the Senate.

Such a scheme also could help Mr. Mnuchin square his seemingly contradictory comments on the Volcker rule. He has said banks with federally insured deposits shouldn't be taking trading risks with their own money, but also that trading restrictions on those banks have harmed market liquidity.

For universal banks like J.P. Morgan Chase, Citigroup and Bank of America, the potential implications are huge. They would have to decide if it is worth incurring the costs of undergoing a ringfencing process, including"http://www.wsj.com/articles/how-the-u-ks-version-of-glass-steagall-will-hit-big-banks-1444913519", or if it would be more efficient to just break up, potentially losing some synergies between units. Either way, it will be a cumbersome process.

Another question is how the hived-off investment-banking units would be funded. It would be problematic if they were overly dependent on short-term wholesale finance, as Bear Stearns and Lehman Brothers were. One possible source of funding would be corporate deposits, which aren't insured.

U.S. banks don't necessarily want another regulatory revamp that forces further rethinking of fundamental business models, after years of adapting to current rules. Tweaks to the existing system, such as looser Volcker-rule enforcement, would suit them just fine.

But in today's political scene, big banks can't bet on getting exactly what they want. Until the regulatory outlook is clearer, investors should be mindful of the risks.

Pro Private Markets
EquipmentShare Builds Case for Peer-to-Peer Construction Gear; The company gets $26 million to expand its peer-to-peer marketplace for construction equipment

By Cat Zakrzewski 
427 words
24 January 2017
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A Missouri company that is bringing the on-demand marketplace business model to the construction industry has raised $26 million.

Insight Venture Partners and Romulus Growth, a Romulus Capital fund, led the round in EquipmentShare. Previous investor Y Combinator also participated.

EquipmentShare operates as a marketplace like AirBnB where contractors can lend or rent construction equipment ranging from forklifts to concrete buggies.

The round comes as venture capital interest in marketplaces and on-demand startups has softened, following a frenzy of funding rounds in 2014 and 2015. EquipmentShare Chief Executive William Schlacks said he thinks the company was somewhat insulated from the changing funding environment because it is a business-to-business, not consumer, service.

"We're about solving real problems, not playing some VC game," Mr. Schlacks said.

The investment in EquipmentShare highlights the increase of industries that are being transformed by software. Late last year, construction software provider "http://www.wsj.com/articles/procore-technologies-joins-billion-dollar-startup-club-1481230801" raised funding at about a $1 billion valuation.

Mr. Schlacks said when evaluating investors, he knew he wouldn't find many with experience in construction, but did look marketplace expertise.

Romulus has invested in another marketplace startup that allows medical professionals to lend and rent equipment.

"There really is no investor who spends 60 hours a week in this space," Mr. Schlacks said.

EquipmentShare offers rentals in Columbia and St. Louis, Missouri, as well as Dallas, Jacksonville, Fla., and Auckland, New Zealand. The company will use the funding to expand to new markets in the South and Midwest, including Austin.

In addition to expanding its rental service, EquipmentShare will offer ES Track, a telematics platform. The software allows businesses nationwide to track their fleet and it provides analytics about the equipment.

The tracking software launches as a federal mandate is set to go into effect at the end of the year that would require contractors to keep electronic logs on the hours of services of vehicles.

Neil Chheda, Romulus Capital co-founder and general partner, said this software could be "the backbone" of automated fleets of construction equipment in the future. But he noted that autonomous construction equipment is likely 5 to 10 years away.

He said there are many other problems EquipmentShare can focus on today just in helping contractors better manage their equipment, adding, "There's a lot of low-hanging fruit."

Tracy's Take: At Washington March, Not Much Wall Street Rhetoric

CFPB Chief Presses On With Regulatory Agenda

Former SEC Enforcement Chief Ceresney to Return to Private Practice

Treasury Nominee Steven Mnuchin Says Bank Regulation Should Be Tailored to Activity

At Wells Fargo, Bank Branches Were Tipped Off to Inspections

At Washington March, Not Much Wall Street Rhetoric

On Saturday, I brought my notepad to the National Mall as one several Wall Street Journal reporters monitoring the throngs "http://www.wsj.com/articles/demonstrators-gather-for-womens-march-a-day-after-trump-inauguration-1485006572" the day after Inauguration Day. What I saw reminded me of the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052970203735304577167214156233978", in that the crowd represented a litany of different people advocating for litany of different causes.

One "Occupy" element that was missing: Very few people were ranting about Wall Street.

The marchers showed up to advocate for women and to show their displeasure with the election results. It was no surprise to see signs demanding equal pay and abortion rights alongside jabs at President Donald Trump.

Chants, flyers and posters also addressed many other issues, including immigration, climate change, the minimum wage, Obamacare, the Patriot Act, socialism, racism, gay rights and public education. In five hours, I saw one sign about financial regulation, about halfway through: "Regulate Banks, Not Bodies!"

It would be a leap to conclude the public is no longer angry at Wall Street. I suspect many marchers still harbor negative views of the financial industry, a premise confirmed anecdotally when I walked past Bank of America Corp.'s branch across from the Treasury Department and heard a handful of young men shouting their opinions of the firm. Suffice it to say it's not appropriate to repeat them here.

Recent polling by "http://www.wsj.com/public/resources/documents/161300NBCWSJDecember216PollFinal1.pdf", as well as Gallup, suggests hating Wall Street has fallen out of favor somewhat, with banking and finance receiving substantially higher marks from the public at the end of 2016 than around 2010, the last time there was substantial shift in Washington's orientation toward the industry.

Perhaps that helps explain why Mr. Trump has felt free to do what was unthinkable only months ago, and appoint several Goldman Sachs Group Inc. alumni to key administration posts. If memories of the crisis weren't fading, it would be hard to imagine someone like Steven Mnuchin, the Goldman veteran turned leader of a bank that critics called a "foreclosure machine," sailing to confirmation as Treasury secretary, as Mr. Mnuchin appears to be doing.

How big banks fare in the Trump era remains to be seen, and surely the industry is wary of the fact that populism appears to be a powerful political force. Mr. Trump won't want to be painted as a puppet of Wall Street power, and lawmakers including Sens. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.) and Bernie Sanders (I., Vt.) won't shy away from branding him that way if they see what they perceive as lax regulatory policies.

What the march suggests is that for now, other perceived evils have the public's attention.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

CFPB Chief Presses On With Regulatory Agenda

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTON On the first full business day of the Trump administration, Richard Cordray, the head of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, made his presence known by alleging that Citigroup Inc.'s mortgage-servicing units mistreated struggling borrowers.

Monday's settlement with Citigroup the bureau's 15th enforcement action in the past two months underscores Mr. Cordray's intention to keep pursuing a busy regulatory agenda, unfazed by the intensifying heat from the Republican administration to rein in his agency and remove him from its helm.

The consumer watchdog created in the aftermath of the financial crisis is on the front line of President Donald Trump's war on Obama-era financial regulations and Mr. Cordray has emerged as a major target of the Republican assault. (Mr. Cordray will discuss his stance at a WSJ Pro Financial Regulation event Tuesday morning, his first public appearance since Friday's inauguration.)

Congressional Republicans are stepping up their attack on the CFPB, which they and the financial industry have long criticized as a symbol of regulatory overreach. They are preparing legislation to rein in its budget, weaken its leadership structure and narrow its authority. They are looking for ways that would allow President Donald Trump to fire Mr. Cordray despite legal hurdles.

"This was a duplicative agency that didn't need to be there," Rep. Blaine Luetkemeyer (R., Mo), who heads a House subcommittee with oversight of the CFPB, said in an interview earlier this month. "It's been a total failure, and in my mind it needs to go away."

Mr. Trump hasn't made any specific remarks about the CFPB but some of his aides said they wanted to see the agency constrained. Steven Mnuchin, Mr. Trump's pick for Treasury secretary, said in his confirmation hearing last week that the CFPB should be preserved but Congress should have control over its budget. Asked about the future of Mr. Cordray at a press conference Monday, White House spokesman Sean Spicer said "no decision has been made."

A CFPB spokeswoman has said Congress has confirmed Mr. Cordray to serve a term until July 2018 and he had no plans to step down.

The CFPB is plowing ahead with a spree of enforcement actions. In January alone, the regulator sued Navient Corp., the nation's largest servicer of federal and private student loans, over its handling of loan repayments. It fined two big credit-reporting agencies, alleging deceptive marketing of credit reports, and it expanded a crackdown on overdraft fees by suing a regional bank.

Navient and TCF denied wrongdoing. The two credit-reporting bureaus settled without admitting or denying the allegations.

"When banks deliberately design ways to pull the wool over the eyes of their customers, the consumer bureau is watching carefully," Mr. Cordray told reporters after filing the lawsuit over TCF National Bank's overdraft program on Jan. 19. "We will not hesitate to take action against any entity that we find to be deceiving or abusing consumers. "

The aggressive stance has drawn sharp reactions from some of the companies targeted.

Navient Chief Executive Jack Remondi said in an interview Monday that the CFPB was acting with a political agenda, adding that an official from the agency's enforcement team made what Mr. Remondi characterized as an ultimatum weeks after the presidential election. That came after the two sides held regular talks over the case since 2015, the CEO said. "Settle now or be sued," Mr. Remondi said in the interview. "  Settle before inauguration day' was their quote, turning this into a politically driven solution."

"This case is about standing up for student-loan borrowers, plain and simple," said Seth Frotman, the CFPB's student-loan ombudsman. "For years, Navient has systematically and illegally failed borrowers at every stage of repayment."

A Navient spokeswoman said the company "welcomes a strong central consumer regulator who publishes clear rules for all to follow, and doesn't have a position on the future of the CFPB or its director."

People familiar with the transition process said the administration might start its push to revamp the agency with Mr. Trump urging Mr. Cordray to step down voluntarily. If the CFPB director won't oblige, the president could fire him for cause or by citing a federal court ruling that declared the bureau's single-director structure unconstitutional. The CFPB has appealed the decision.

In a sign that the new administration may be looking to name a new director soon after the inauguration, Mr. Trump earlier this month met with Randy Neugebauer, a recently retired congressman who is seen as a top candidate for the job. The Texas Republican had led his party's attack on the CFPB as a senior member of the House Financial Services Committee.

Mr. Cordray's departure could scuttle the bureau's continuing efforts to roll out new rules on payday lending, forced arbitration in consumer financial contracts and prepaid cards.

The CFPB was established with the help of Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass), a firebrand popular with liberal voters, to tip the balance of power toward consumers from banks after millions lost their homes and savings during the financial crisis. Since then, it has grown into a major regulator with 1,600 employees that has written rules to rein in companies' activities in markets including mortgages, credit cards and student loans.

The bureau's enforcement actions against banks, payday lenders, debt collectors and others have resulted in a total of $12 billion returned to some 27 million consumers the regulator said were harmed by alleged corporate misconduct.

Democrats and consumer advocates have vowed to protect the agency and urged Mr. Trump to keep Mr. Cordray in the job or be ready for a prolonged fight.

"Republicans, working with special interests in the industry, are trying to make us forget that we had the biggest financial crisis since the Great Depression just eight years ago," said Ed Mierzwinski, consumer program Director for Federation of State Public Interest Research Groups, known as U.S. PIRG, a consumer group that supports the CFPB.

To guarantee the CFPB's independence, the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act, which created the agency, states the president can remove its director only for "inefficiency, neglect of duty or malfeasance in office."

To allow Mr. Trump to fire the CFPB chief for cause, lawyers working with Republicans have been looking for missteps during Mr. Cordray's career. House Republicans last week issued a report laying out one such scenario, saying Mr. Cordray may have violated federal law regarding 2015 rules on auto lending.

 Josh Mitchell contributed to this article.

Former SEC Enforcement Chief Ceresney to Return to Private Practice

By Dave Michaels

WASHINGTON Andrew Ceresney, a top white-collar lawyer who led the Securities and Exchange Commission's enforcement division for the past three years, is returning to his former legal home at Debevoise & Plimpton LLP.

Mr. Ceresney, 45 years old, will resume a practice he handled for Debevoise before becoming Wall Street's top civil enforcer. He returns with an added rank, joining as co-chair of the firm's litigation practice, a title once held by his mentor, former SEC Chairman Mary Jo White. Ms. White, who took over the SEC in 2013 and stepped down Friday at the end of Barack Obama's presidency, brought Mr. Ceresney with her as enforcement director.

At Debevoise, Mr. Ceresney will defend some of the same types of companies and executives that he investigated at the SEC, where he and Ms. White were known for seeking higher corporate penalties in legal settlements. The move from probing Wall Street to defending it from regulators is a common switch for SEC lawyers, although the practice frustrates many advocates of strict regulation who say the "revolving door" promotes coziness.

Like many former regulators, Mr. Ceresney is a supporter of moving between the two worlds, saying government lawyers are better equipped to grapple with Wall Street if they know where to apply the pressure. Under government ethics rules, he is forbidden from directly dealing with the SEC for a year, but can interact with officials at other federal or state agencies.

"When I worked for the government, I represented my client, the American people," Mr. Ceresney said. "It was extremely valuable in my prior role to have the private-practice experience and to know what kind of leverage the government had."

The law firm is expected to announce the hiring Tuesday, and Mr. Ceresney will resume work from its New York office in early March.

Unlike some prior SEC enforcement chiefs, Mr. Ceresney didn't carry out an extended courtship after leaving in December. A predecessor, Robert Khuzami, benefited from a bidding war for his services when he left the government in 2013. Mr. Khuzami eventually landed at Kirkland & Ellis, where he was paid more than $5 million a year.

Mr. Ceresney said he "explored a bit" in terms of other opportunities, but quickly decided he wanted to return to Debevoise, a firm he first joined in 2003. He earned about $245,000 at the SEC; he declined to comment on his pay at Debevoise.

Debevoise is one of the few white-shoe law firms that uses a "lockstep" system to compensate partners based on seniority, rather than rewarding them for how many clients they bring in. Ms. White reported earning $2.4 million in 2012 when she had the title that Mr. Ceresney will take.

In recent months, Debevoise has recruited other former government lawyers, including David O'Neil, who was acting head of the U.S. Justice Department's criminal division, and Julie Riewe, who worked for Mr. Ceresney and led the SEC's asset-management unit.

Ms. White hasn't announced what she intends to do next. Mr. Ceresney has worked with Ms. White for nearly two decades, having joined the U.S. attorney's office in Manhattan in 1998 when she was its top federal prosecutor.

As an ex-prosecutor, Ms. White was an unusual choice to run the SEC, a bureaucracy faulted by critics for not punishing enough banks that sold the toxic mortgage securities that nearly toppled the financial system in 2008. Mr. Ceresney said early on that he would restore the SEC's "swagger" and take "bolder action" against Wall Street. During his term as director, the SEC pursued a record number of enforcement cases using a strategy that targeted small infractions as well as bigger frauds.

The commission's next likely leader is corporate attorney Jay Clayton of Sullivan & Cromwell LLP, who like past SEC chairmen appointed before the financial crisis would come directly from Wall Street.

Mr. Ceresney said he doesn't think he'll lack for work in his new job. "Enforcement is a bipartisan issue," he said. "I don't expect the new administration to change that."

Treasury Nominee Steven Mnuchin Says Bank Regulation Should Be Tailored to Activity

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON Treasury Secretary nominee Steven Mnuchin said banks should be regulated based on their "complexity and activity, not simply size" and addressed other regulatory issues in written answers submitted to members of the Senate Finance Committee after his confirmation hearing last week.

"I believe in a regulatory framework that is determined by complexity and activity, not simply size," he wrote, addressing a question about the $50 billion asset threshold at which banks face tougher regulation under the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul law. Many lawmakers and regulators want to change the line.

Mr. Mnuchin also endorsed a "comprehensive review" of the powers and institutional processes of the U.S. Financial Stability Oversight Council, a group of senior regulators created by Dodd-Frank, signaling he is open to discussing changing that aspect of the law as well.

Many of Mr. Mnuchin's answers repeated or elaborated on statements he made in the hearing. Addressing whether he supported a return to the Glass-Steagall law forcing banks to split the business of traditional lending from investment banking, he said the Trump administration wanted to study the issue.

"A bright line between commercial and investment banking, although less complicated, may inhibit the necessary lending and capital markets activities to support a robust economy," Mr. Mnuchin said. "This is why we have suggested a 21st Century Glass-Steagall may be appropriate."

At Wells Fargo, Bank Branches Were Tipped Off to Inspections

By Emily Glazer

As Wells Fargo & Co.'s sales-tactics scandal unfolded, investors, regulators and politicians asked how improper practices could have persisted for so long. One possible reason: bank branches were given a heads up before Wells Fargo's internal monitors landed for inspections.

Managers and employees at the bank's roughly 6,000 branches across the U.S. typically had at least 24 hours' warning about annual reviews conducted by risk employees, current and former Wells Fargo employees and executives said. That gave many employees time to cover up improper practices, such as opening accounts or signing customers up for products without their knowledge.

More than a dozen current and former employees of the bank across California, Arizona and New Jersey, for instance, said they forged or saw colleagues forge signatures on documents or shred papers that could have indicated accounts were opened without authorization.

Often, managers would call for all hands on deck at a branch to stay late into the evening or sometimes all night to shred documents or forge signatures if they weren't there, some current and former managers said.

For instance, they would go through desks to find signature cards that hadn't been approved, make sure wire forms had been filled out properly and that documents in a "control binder" like cash or teller audits were filled out, said Ivan Rodriguez, a former branch banker at Wells Fargo for about six years until 2013.

Some branches that opened accounts for customers without the customer present would cut and paste a signature the bank had on file for the customer and add it to the required signature card, Mr. Rodriguez said.

"You became numb to it," he added. "It became pretty normal."

A Wells Fargo spokeswoman said the bank has boosted oversight, monitoring and accountability so unethical practices don't happen again. That includes investing millions in staffing, mystery shops by a third party, unannounced branch inspections on employee sales behavior and an increase in branch visits by its internal auditors.

The bank has been under fire since September when it entered a $185 million settlement and enforcement action with regulators and a city official over opening as many as 2.1 million accounts using fictitious or unauthorized information. Wells Fargo still faces a spate of state and federal investigations, including from the Justice Department and the Securities and Exchange Commission.

In the scandal's wake, Wells Fargo has been changing procedures and trying to tighten internal checks, said Vic Albrecht, who has led risk management for the retail bank since September. Last fall, it piloted a surprise sales-practices inspection that previously didn't exist to ensure there isn't undue sales pressure and customers get appropriate products, among other checks, he said.

Around 100 of these "Conduct Risk Reviews" have been completed based on possible risks or complaints. Top retail-banking executives who typically oversee hundreds of branches, risk executives and company lawyers are aware of the results, Mr. Albrecht said.

But the other system, now known as "Branch Control Review," still gives a 24-hour notice so branch managers can staff appropriately and it doesn't interrupt customer service, he said, though advance notice and other parts of the review could change in the future. It measures "operational integrity," according to an internal bank document reviewed by The Wall Street Journal. That system, alongside the bank's "Quality of Sales Report Card" that checks signatures, procedures and funding, are part of a new branch "risk score." This will be factored into employees' compensation under a new plan rolled out to staff this month, according to plan documents.

Bank-branch audits are common in the industry, especially to try to spot risks related to large transactions, paperwork logs and cash shipments. Other big banks, such as Bank of America Corp., Citigroup Inc. and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. typically have surprise branch audits, or checks, at least once a year, said people familiar with the banks, who added that branches didn't get notice ahead of time.

"They need to audit business as usual, not to see somebody put on their Sunday best clothes," said Jeffrey Sonnenfeld, professor and associate dean for leadership studies at Yale School of Management. "Mystery shoppers...don't give advance notice to the stores that they're coming."

Banks are expected to have three lines of defense to spot irregularities or problems: its business executives, risk-management professionals and internal auditors. The board of directors is supposed to add another layer for checks and balances. It appears some of these were lacking at Wells Fargo, according to regulators.

"Had these structure elements been functioning properly, they would have prevented the type of abuses we have witnessed at Wells Fargo," Thomas Curry, head of the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency, said at one of two congressional hearings about the bank's sales practices.

At Wells Fargo, retail-bank risk executives some branch employees called them auditors typically traveled district by district to check on branches, some current and former employees said. But retail-bank executives would usually receive anywhere from 24 to 72 hours' notice of the inspections, and branch managers typically got one day's heads up, these people said.

Some of those bank managers would meet with operations staff in their districts to discuss issues auditors were finding so they could fix those in advance of another audit, one of these people said.

Internally, the advance notice known to some within the bank as an open-book test was considered standard, these people said. Only in recent years did much of the operational-control checks move to electronic forms auditors would gather in advance, making it harder for branch employees to fake records.

The inspectors would examine, for instance, how many accounts were funded, whether signature cards existed for accounts opened in the last 90 days, and if employees knew the opening and closing procedures of the branch, among other checks, these people said.

"The volume of business you did was humongous, so the dotting of Is and the crossing of Ts was not that great," one of these people said. "Growing the business was primary: the more successful you were, the higher the goals were. So you had to keep up."

WSJ Pro: Ex-SEC Chief Thinks She Could Have Accomplished Much More

By Dave Michaels

SAN DIEGO Speaking publicly for the first time since she left the Securities and Exchange Commission, former chairman Mary Jo White on Monday said postelection politics prevented her from finishing several regulations that were drafted and ready to advance.

Ms. White, whose tenure ended on Friday, said she couldn't muster a commitment from other commissioners to vote on rules governing derivatives trading and electronic delivery of mutual-fund shareholder reports.

SEC rules require that at least three of its sitting commissioners be present for the commission to vote on new regulations effectively granting dissenting commissioners veto power over any measure they oppose when the SEC has two open seats on its five-member board. By simply not showing up to a vote, a dissenting commissioner can block the SEC from acting altogether.

Ms. White, an appointee of former President Barack Obama, didn't name the commissioners who objected to moving forward on particular rules. But she evinced frustration with her inability to corral enough votes to finish items that were high on her priority list.

"Several were ripe for final commission action last year, but were stalled by the postelection absence of a quorum to act," Ms. White told Northwestern University's Securities Regulation Institute in Coronado, Calif.

Ms. White said some rules could advance under her anointed successor corporate attorney Jay Clayton of Sullivan & Cromwell LLP but she acknowledged that others are unlikely to move forward because Republicans oppose them. The list of endangered regulatory proposals includes measures required by the 2010 Dodd Frank Act, such as rules to oversee trading platforms for security-based swaps and new executive-compensation disclosures.

"It remains to be seen what the new Commission may do with these initiatives, and when," she said.

Ms. White said she expects the SEC will in the future continue her focus on revising corporate disclosures, with an eye toward reducing duplication and complexity. She said regulators also would likely expand their oversight of the bond market and high-speed traders. The SEC under Ms. White proposed requiring all active proprietary traders to register with the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority. It also sought to have Finra establish a repository for trades in U.S. Treasurys.

WSJ Pro: Republicans on House Financial Services Huddle at Agenda Retreat

By Gabriel T. Rubin

Republican members of the House Financial Services Committee huddled at a resort outside of Washington on Sunday and Monday to strategize about top priorities for the new Congress.

The meeting, which took place at the Gaylord National Resort in Maryland, focused on the top agenda items of the panel's chairman, Rep. Jeb Hensarling of Texas, and other senior committee members, chief among them being a rollback of the Dodd-Frank Act.

Their agenda focused on the "big priorities for the year: Choice Act, flood insurance and housing finance," a congressional aide said, referring to Mr. Hensarling's bill that would provide regulatory relief to banks that hold more capital, among other changes.

Subcommittee chairmen, several of whom are new to their positions, also made presentations at the retreat, which was held in private.

Congressional Republicans will meet in Philadelphia later this week at a corporate-backed policy retreat to address overall priorities for the Congress, which besides a financial regulatory overhaul include replacing the Affordable Care Act and making changes to the corporate tax code.

The committee is expected to move forward with the Choice Act sometime next month. The bill, initially introduced in the last Congress, will be revamped slightly to account for new political realities, including Donald Trump taking office as president last week.

Still, it is unclear how far the legislation can go given the threat of a Democratic filibuster in the Senate.

"We doubt the bill can pass the Senate and testimony last week by Treasury Secretary-designate [Steven] Mnuchin casts a lot of doubt on whether the Trump administration will spend political capital trying to pass the Choice Act in the Senate," said Brian Gardner, an analyst for the investment banking firm Keefe, Bruyette & Woods.

 Rachel Witkowski contributed to this article

Fugitive in Credit-Card Case Who Fled to Pakistan Is Arrested

By Kate King

Federal authorities have arrested a fugitive who fled to Pakistan after he was indicted four years ago in a $200 million credit-card-fraud scam, the U.S. attorney's office in New Jersey said Monday.

Habib Chaudhry, 49, has been living in Pakistan but decided to return to the U.S. and turn himself in to federal officials, according to Jerome Ballarotto, who was appointed by the court Monday to serve as Mr. Chaudhry's attorney.

"It's not easy, living in the world today with something like this hanging over your head," Mr. Ballarotto said. "It's not like the old days when you could just disappear. He decided he didn't want to live that way, so he came here to clear things up."

Federal officials charged Mr. Chaudhry in February 2013 with participating in an effort to create 7,000 false identities and open thousands of credit cards, which were then used to charge hundreds of millions of dollars. Nineteen people have pleaded guilty in connection with the fraud case and two remain at large, according to the U.S. attorney's office.

Mr. Chaudhry, who is an American citizen, appeared in federal court in Newark, N.J., on Monday but didn't enter a plea. He was released on a $250,000 bail secured by the deed to a house he and his wife own in Valley Stream, N.Y. He surrendered his passport and will live under house arrest and wear an electronic monitor, his lawyer said.

Federal officials said the credit-card fraud was one of the largest ever prosecuted by the Justice Department. The 2013 criminal complaint outlines a 10-year scheme, spanning eight countries and 28 states, which involved creating fake identities and sham companies to open credit cards and process transactions. Federal officials accused Mr. Chaudhry and others of using the proceeds to buy luxury cars, electronics, spa treatments, clothing and gold.

Yahoo Among Many Disclosing Data Breaches to SEC

By Vipal Monga

Yahoo is part of a growing list of companies disclosing past data breaches to the Securities and Exchange Commission, as hackers become more successful in penetrating company firewalls to steal sensitive data.

The internet company reported in September that hackers had stolen data from 500 million users in 2014, making it the 102nd company to disclose such a theft to the SEC, according to data from Audit Analytics. Overall, 17 companies reported breaches to the regulator last year, down from 18 in 2015 and 34 in 2014.

Yahoo also disclosed in December a second breach that occurred in 2013.

The Wall Street Journal reported on Monday that the SEC has opened an investigation into whether Yahoo should have reported the data theft to investors earlier. The SEC requires companies to disclose cybersecurity risks as as soon as they are determined to have a material effect on their businesses.

But, as CFO Journal reported in September, company executives must exercise judgement when deciding whether any hacking is material. Many hesitate to do so, because offering too much or too little information to investors can hurt companies.

On the one hand, investors may feel management or boards tried to hide a problem or failed to disclose a breach in a timely way. On the other, too much disclosure could cause investors to bid down a company's stock price, even if a later investigation reveals little impact on the business, according to experts.

The SEC has yet to bring a case against any company that failed to disclose a breach. At a conference last February, Stephanie Avakian, the SEC's deputy director of enforcement, said the disclosure failure would need to be "significant" to warrant such a move.

Poland's Pitch to Brexit Bankers: You Might as Well Move Somewhere Cheap

By Max Colchester

When the U.K. quits the European Union, many banks will shuffle operations to Paris or Frankfurt. But London-on-Seine or London-on-Main might not be as compelling as London-on-Oder.

Wroclaw, Poland, is one of a clutch of cities in the Eastern European country wooing big banks' business. The pitch: If you're going to move a bunch of stuff, you might as well go somewhere cheap.

J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. executives recently visited Wroclaw to scope it out; the giant U.S. bank is weighing hiring roughly 2,000 people in Poland, according to people familiar with the matter. Credit Suisse Group AG already has 4,000 staff in Wroclaw, a medieval city with a picturesque market square. Bank of New York Mellon Corp. has about 1,000.

New York, London, Paris and Frankfurt will still be the locales of choice for fancy bankers who pitch megaclients. But banks have tens of thousands of operational and support roles. Bank executives say Brexit will accelerate a well-establish trend to move them to lower-cost locations.

"Brexit will encourage people to look at outsourcing simply because there is more pressure on costs," says Isabelle Jenkins, a partner at consulting firm PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP.

Poland which is in the EU is marketing itself as an inexpensive place for profit-strapped banks to put their European back-office workers. Even before Brexit, Credit Suisse, BNY Mellon and UBS Group AG were expanding their operations in the country. Goldman Sachs Group Inc. is doubling staff numbers this year and eyeing Poland as a potential hub to service its enlarged EU operations after Brexit, according to people familiar with the matter. A spokeswoman for J.P. Morgan said its review "has nothing to do with London jobs or Brexit."

The Association of Business Leaders in Poland estimates up to 20,000 financial-services-related jobs could be created in the country this year alone. As Brexit unfolds, more roles will come, says Polish Deputy Prime Minister Mateusz Morawiecki. "We are now seeing a definite interest by some of these and other financial institutions in relocating all or part of their European head-office activities to Poland," he says.

Last November Mr. Morawiecki, a former banker, traveled to London to make his pitch. Polish cities offer a different draw from Paris or Frankfurt: a cheap, educated European workforce that can process transactions and help manage functions like human resources.

Razor-thin margins were pushing Europe-based banks to move jobs out of London well before Brexit. Credit Suisse, for instance, said it is aiming to reduce head count in London to 5,000 by the end of 2017 from 9,000 in 2016. The bank's website currently advertises for 250 jobs in Poland, compared with just 37 in the U.K.

Now finance companies in the U.K. are again combing through their businesses to work out what needs to be shifted where to ensure they can continue selling to EU-based clients. If they are forced to spend heavily to recreate subsidiaries in the European Union to service clients, then costs may have to be cut elsewhere.

Poland isn't alone in trying to lure operational roles. Ireland and several other Eastern European countries are making similar pitches. Poland also has its drawbacks. EU authorities have criticized its conservative government for cracking down on judicial and press freedoms. The country's leader is a euroskeptic. Mr. Morawiecki, meanwhile, has led a campaign to "re-Polonize" the country's domestic financial sector, raising questions about how foreign businesses may be treated.

There are also practical considerations. The operations centers have to manage a high turnover of staff because employees regularly move looking for better pay. There are also some unpractical Polish labor laws including one that prevents pregnant women from sitting at computer screens for more than four hours.

Having a back-office in the country offers several advantages, bankers say. Poland offers a large and young workforce. State university tuition is free, so many have a higher education. The time zone and the fact it is in the EU's single market mean that it is easy for managers to visit from other European hubs without facing visa issues. Currently 212,000 people work in service centers in Poland, with the majority in financial services, according to the Polish business association.

Other Polish cities are also capitalizing. Credit Suisse last year opened a new office in Warsaw. Goldman Sachs recently moved into a skyscraper in the capital and took an option to extend office space for an extra 750 people.

U.K. Supreme Court Rules Theresa May Must Consult Parliament Before Starting Brexit

By Jenny Gross

LONDON Britain's Supreme Court on Tuesday ruled Prime Minister Theresa May can't start the Brexit process without approval from Parliament, a decision that could potentially complicate her path toward a clear break from the European Union.

The government said the ruling wouldn't affect Mrs. May's plans to trigger talks to leave the EU by the end of March and the opposition Labour Party said after the judgment it wouldn't seek to stop Brexit from happening.

But Labour said it would try to amend any bill introduced by Mrs. May to kick off the Brexit process, possibly influencing how the U.K.'s new relationship with the EU will look.

While a majority of lawmakers voted to stay in the EU, many have said they won't seek to block Article 50, which formally starts Britain's exit from the EU, given the popular vote, in which 52% voted to leave.

"The British people voted to leave the EU, and the government will deliver on their verdict triggering Article 50, as planned, by the end of March," a U.K. government spokesman said. "Today's ruling does nothing to change that."

Eight justices voted against the government and three voted in favor of it, in a decision that was widely expected.

Lord David Neuberger said any change in the law to put Brexit into effect must be made by an act of Parliament. "To proceed otherwise would be a breach of settled constitutional principles stretching back many centuries," he said.

He said the Supreme Court justices were ruling on the process of legally bringing the result into effect, and that the ruling had nothing to do with whether the U.K. should exit from the EU or the timetable.

The case has been one of the most politically charged in decades. After the High Court ruled against the government in November, pro-Brexit activists called the decision an attempt to overturn the will of Britons who chose to break away from the bloc in a June referendum. The Daily Mail newspaper said the three High Court judges who ruled on the case were "enemies of the people."

But the landscape has shifted since then. In December, Mrs. May won lawmakers' backing to trigger the start of Brexit by the end of March after promising to give Parliament, the majority of whom backed staying in the EU, an opportunity to scrutinize her plan first.

Mrs. May has outlined a plan for a definitive break from the EU, saying she intends to take the country out of the EU's single market for goods and services. Leaving the single market will create uncertainties for U.K. businesses that rely on trade with Europe, particularly financial markets, auto makers and aerospace.

Asked in an interview with a German newspaper this month whether the U.K. was seeking to become a tax haven with low levels of corporate tax, U.K. Treasury Chief Philip Hammond said the U.K. could change its economic model if it isn't granted access to trade in the EU after it leaves the bloc.

Jeremy Corbyn, Labour leader, said in a statement after the ruling that Labour "will not frustrate" the process for trigger the U.K.'s exit.

"However, Labour will seek to amend the Article 50 bill to prevent the Conservatives using Brexit to turn Britain into a bargain basement tax haven off the coast of Europe," Mr. Corbyn said. "Labour will seek to build in the principles of full, tariff-free access to the single market and maintenance of workers' rights and social and environmental protections."

He added that the party would demand that the U.K. government lays out a plan for negotiations so parliamentarians could hold it to account.

Kelvin Hopkins, a Labour member of Parliament, urged his party not to seek to complicate the process of leaving.

"My colleagues in the House of Commons need to realize that if we are seen to frustrate the will of the British people, by opposing or delaying Brexit we could find ourselves in a position where we will never see a Labour government again," Mr. Hopkins said in a statement.

Over four days of hearings before the Supreme Court last month, the government said it had the right to trigger Brexit because of the so-called royal prerogative, in which executive authority is given to ministers so they can govern on the monarch's behalf.

The case was brought by a group of British citizens opposed to Brexit with the help of some of the U.K.'s top constitutional lawyers.

Spearheading the legal challenge were British businesswoman Gina Miller and hairdresser Deir Dos Santos. Grahame Pigney, a France-based expatriate who used crowdfunding from more than 4,000 people to pay for lawyers, joined the suit as a co-party.

 Nicholas Winning contributed to this article.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

White Sees Corporate Disclosure as SEC Priority

Mary Jo White, who stepped down as SEC chairman Friday, predicts changes to corporate disclosures will be on her successor's front burner. White says the SEC would likely seek public comment on how it should update the statistical disclosures required by bank holding companies. Other changes will probably have to wait for the SEC to be back to full strength with five commissioners, she says. Those measures could include further relief for smaller companies seeking to raise money from private investors, she says. "I would expect the next commission to do so, looking for ways to provide flexibility where appropriate to facilitate the ability of private markets to provide liquidity to small companies to grow and to adjust the incentives for companies to go public and remain public," she tells the Securities Regulation Institute sponsored by Northwestern University's Pritzker School of Law. (dave.michaelswsj.com; @davidamichaels)

Bailout Prevention Rule Publishing Despite Trump Freeze

The Federal Reserve's "total loss-absorbing capacity" rule designed to prevent bailouts of big banks is set to be published in the Federal Register Tuesday, according to the register's website. President Donald Trump's chief of staff had issued a moratorium on new regulations Friday, asking executive agencies to withdraw any rules that had been submitted for official publication. The Fed board had voted to adopt the so-called TLAC rule on December 15. It is not clear whether the Fed and other regulators will be following the White House regulatory freeze going forward. Financial regulators are generally considered independent of the White House, although they may choose to follow the order so as to comply with its spirit. (Ryan.Tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

State AGs Support CFPB in Lawsuit Vs PHH

Democratic state attorneys general from 16 states and Washington DC came to the aid of the CFPB in a high-profile lawsuit, in which a panel of judges last year declared the CFPB's single-director structure unconstitutional and granted the president power to fire the CFPB chief at will. In a motion filed with the federal appeals court in Washington, the AGs asked for permission to intervene in the case, PHH v. CFPB, to support the CFPB's ongoing appeal process, saying that the bureau's interest may not be represented adequately under the new administration. After the earlier ruling, the CFPB has requested a rehearing of the case. "The current ruling, if permitted to stand, will undermine the power of the State Attorneys General to effectively protect consumers against abuse in the consumer finance industry," wrote the AGs. The move comes as Congressional Republicans seek the removal of CFPB Director Richard Cordray by President Donald Trump. (yuka.hayashi@wsj.com; @TokyoWoods)
