Michaels's Take: Catching the CAT is Next Step in Finra's Evolution

GOP Lawmakers Get Quick Start on Financial Deregulation Push

How a Financial Council Republicans Loathe Could Work in Their Favor

ECB Survey Suggests Easing Credit Conditions in Eurozone

U.K. Leader to Seek Clean Break From EU

Catching the CAT is Next Step in Finra's Evolution

If everything goes according to plan, a committee of stock and options exchanges will announce Tuesday who will build the government's latest supercomputer: the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/sec-to-vote-on-consolidated-audit-trail-to-detect-market-manipulation-1479240411".

Of the three finalists to build the CAT, one is the clear favorite: the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority. Finra, Wall Street's self-overseer, is the frontline regulator of U.S. brokerages. It has strived in recent years to automate its surveillance of equity and options markets, consulting a range of technology experts to help build and operate the CAT.

The Securities and Exchange Commission ordered the CAT's construction after regulators struggled to explain the causes of the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052748704029304575526390131916792", when the Dow Jones Industrial Average plunged nearly 1,000 points before quickly rebounding. Simply put, the SEC in 2010 lacked the information needed to quickly analyze the chaotic trading session. In response, it proposed a massive supercomputer to track the lifecycle of every stock and options order, including data on the identity of brokers' customers.

Finra is well positioned to operate the CAT, given its history as a quasi-governmental market regulator that reports to the SEC. While Finra began as a voluntary association of over-the-counter brokers, it has become a sort of deputy for the SEC in recent decades. It proposes and enforces regulations, fines wrongdoers and monitors markets for insider trading.

The other bidders SunGard Data Systems Inc. and Thesys Technologies LLC are private companies. To be sure, they are on the cutting edge of trading technology. But the exchanges that will pick the winning bid are, like Finra, self-regulatory organizations. In recent years they have contracted with Finra to handle nearly all of their regulatory duties. Setting aside variation in the cost of bids and assuming they are all technically feasible, it seems likely that exchanges will once again pick Finra, this time to build the most comprehensive repository of stock and options orders.

Finra has experience dealing with and learning from the risks inherent in big technology projects. Two years ago, it backed away from an ambitious project known as Cards, for Comprehensive Automated Risk Data System. Cards was supposed to automate Finra's hunt for broker practices that can be harmful to investors, but the project was dropped after some lawmakers and many brokers complained it would become a target for hackers. The brokerage industry has raised the same concerns about the CAT, which is more expansive than Cards would have been.

Tuesday's announcement is important for Finra's future. Winning the bid and operating the CAT would cement Finra's indispensability to the U.S. regulatory system. In fact, if Wall Street's self-regulator doesn't win the award, it's fair to ask what went wrong with its bid.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

WSJ Pro: GOP Lawmakers Get Quick Start on Financial Deregulation Push

By Gabriel T. Rubin and Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON Congressional Republicans are moving quickly to lay the groundwork for an aggressive financial deregulation agenda, passing a half-dozen related measures in the first two weeks of the year as they look ahead to launching a broader overhaul next month.

Following President-elect Donald Trump's pledge to unwind Obama-era financial laws, the House has opened the congressional session that began Jan. 3 by passing bills that would create new hurdles in rulemaking for the Commodity Futures Trading Commission and the Securities and Exchange Commission. The House also passed a more significant bill called the Reins Act that would require congressional approval of major federal rulemaking. That move is teeing up a much larger GOP strategy to repeal regulations like the landmark Dodd-Frank Act of 2010.

Congressional review of rules needs to happen "early and often," Rep. Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas) told WSJ Pro Financial Regulation in a brief interview Friday. Mr. Hensarling, who heads the House Financial Services Committee, said he sees the Reins Act as the first step toward passing his own Dodd-Frank replacement bill "and it needs to be done as early as possible."

While Republican congressional leaders are for now focused on higher-priority goals like repealing President Barack Obama's health law, Mr. Hensarling and others are moving equally aggressively on financial deregulation, hoping to lay the groundwork for that as well.

In an interview with Fox News radio, Mr. Hensarling said that "Dodd-Frank is to household finances what Obamacare is to household health care."

Republican control of both the White House and Congress means the deregulatory measures passed this month by the House have a greater chance of becoming law than they did in previous years, now that there's a president sharing GOP lawmakers' objectives. But it's unclear when, or if, the Senate might take up the measures, or what the prospects would be in that chamber, given the requirement there for at least some Democratic votes to pass bills.

The bills chosen so far in the House were ones that had passed the chamber in earlier years, so it was easier to move them out the gate with the new Congress. Under one, the CFTC would have to obtain a subpoena before it could collect computer source code from algorithmic trading firms, and the SEC would have to do additional cost-benefit analysis before completing new regulations.

The House also passed a larger bill last week that would require agencies to adopt the lowest-cost versions of rules and to publicly post detailed information on rule proposals for extended periods, among other changes.

All of the GOP moves precede a coming effort to roll back "midnight regulations" put in place by the Obama administration. Under the 1996 Congressional Review Act, only simple majorities are required to block rules that have been promulgated within 60 congressional "session days." That puts any rule finalized since May in the crosshairs of Republican leaders. House Majority Leader Kevin McCarthy said that under a timetable fixed by legislative procedures, lawmakers could start voting to repeal specific rules as soon as Jan. 30.

Following that effort, Republicans are expected to move forward on more ambitious legislation to repeal and replace the main financial regulations enacted after the 2008 financial crisis.

The Financial Services Committee will consider an updated version of Mr. Hensarling's Financial Choice Act, which first passed the House last year. The legislation would make major changes to the Dodd-Frank Act, including revamping the structure of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau.

Rep. Blaine Luetkemeyer (R., Mo.), who was recently named to lead the Financial Institutions and Consumer Credit Subcommittee, said the full committee is expected to mark up the Choice Act by mid-February and the bill could hit the House floor in the second quarter.

The Choice Act is "a priority of leadership to get it to the floor as quickly as possible, and the new administration wants it out of the House and out of the Senate as quickly as possible," Mr. Luetkemeyer said in an interview with WSJ Pro Financial Regulation.

The sweeping changes to the legislative policy and financial laws face Democratic opposition in the Senate, and it is unclear whether they would be able to win enough Democratic votes to overcome a filibuster delay strategy that could block a vote.

Rep. John Conyers of Michigan, the top Democrat on the House Judiciary Committee, blasted the GOP deregulation push in a statement, saying the changes to a regulatory process would "seriously undermine critical protections across every regulated industry, including consumers' health and product safety, environmental protections, workplace safety and consumer financial protections."

 Yuka Hayashi contributed to this article.

How a Financial Council Republicans Loathe Could Work in Their Favor

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON Republicans have for years trashed a powerful regulatory committee born after the financial crisis as too political, opaque, and overreaching. But once a GOP administration takes control of the panel, they may find its broad remit useful in quickly scaling back Obama-era policies.

The Financial Stability Oversight Council, a group of 15 state and federal officials, was created by the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul law as a single entity watching the health of the full financial system something some felt was missing in the crisis.

It is chaired by the Treasury secretary. The two men who have held that post under President Barack Obama have used its authority to expand oversight of big non-banks from insurers to investment funds.

President-elect Donald Trump's choice to fill the job, former Goldman Sachs Group Inc. executive Steven Mnuchin, has criticized Dodd-Frank, and could reverse the committee's agenda.

"FSOC up to now has focused on where regulation could be strengthened and increased, but could easily focus on where regulation should be streamlined," said David Portilla, a partner at Debevoise & Plimpton LLP who worked for FSOC.

Mr. Trump's team has talked about a broad regulatory rollback. Much of that agenda will be time-consuming and require congressional approval. But some changes can be done quickly and unilaterally, once Mr. Trump has his regulatory team in place. These include freezing not-yet-completed rules, relaxing enforcement of existing ones and repurposing FSOC.

Neither Mr. Trump nor Mr. Mnuchin, whose Senate confirmation hearing is scheduled for Thursday, have spoken publicly specifically about FSOC. But Mr. Trump has populated his "landing team" in charge of overseeing the transition of power at FSOC with some of its loudest critics.

Alex Pollock, a fellow at the conservative think tank R Street Institute, has said FSOC lacks credibility, saying it has not sounded enough alarm on risks from government-sponsored financial market players like housing finance giants Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, or the Federal Reserve.

Kyle Hauptmann, another FSOC landing team member, is executive director of the Main Street Growth Project, an advocacy group whose website calls FSOC a "rogue regulator" and asks readers to "Sock Back at FSOC" by telling Congress to curtail its power or abolish it.

Republicans have proposed repealing some of FSOC's authority, including in a bill from House Financial Services Committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling (R., Tex.) that is expected to serve as a starting point for a debate on changing Dodd-Frank this year.

Yet, Republicans could instead maintain FSOC's authority and use it for different aims. Consider the powers it grants the Treasury secretary:

  As chair of FSOC whose members include the chairs of the Federal Reserve and Securities and Exchange Commission Mr. Mnuchin can convene a meeting of the top financial regulators at any time, and set the agenda. The Treasury controls a staff of about 20, known as the Secretariat, which drives the regular meetings of FSOC and its five subcommittees. Financial regulators are independent from the Treasury, but by placing an item on the agenda of an FSOC meeting, the Secretariat can effectively force staff at financial regulators to focus on it.

 FSOC can make public statements or recommendations that have a name-and-shame effect. Mr. Obama's first Treasury secretary, Timothy Geithner, successfully pushed for FSOC to draft recommendations for the SEC to more tightly regulate money market mutual funds. Under his successor, Jacob Lew, FSOC conducted a yearslong investigation of the asset-management industry. In both cases, the SEC acted in response.

Mr. Mnuchin's FSOC could issue reports or recommendations on the need for cost-benefit analysis of regulations, the impact of regulations on the economy, or the future of Fannie and Freddie.

FSOC doesn't have "the most powerful tools," Mr. Lew said in a recent interview, but it can convene regulators "to have sensible coordination and conversation."

  FSOC's greatest power is to designate "systemically important" financial firms outside the banking system for stricter federal oversight. It also has the power to remove that label once a firm has been designated -- a step it already took with GE Capital, the financing arm of General Electric Co., after that firm shrunk significantly. Mr. Mnuchin's FSOC could rescind the designations of two current "systemic" insurance firms, Prudential Financial Inc. and American International Group Inc. and drop an effort in the courts to reinstate the label on a third, MetLife, Inc.

Mr. Mnuchin could also push FSOC to rewrite its policies in a way that would make it easier for companies to avoid "systemic" designations in the future.

 If Mr. Mnuchin sympathizes with FSOC detractors, he could call fewer meetings with lighter agendas, essentially letting the council die on the vine. By law, FSOC must meet at least quarterly. During the Obama administration, FSOC has met roughly every six weeks, while staff subcommittees generally meet every two weeks.

Appointment of Trump's Economic Policy Team Faces Delays

By Nick Timiraos

Several of President-elect Donald Trump's top economic policy jobs may go unfilled for days or even weeks after he is sworn in Friday, potentially slowing his pursuit of an ambitious domestic policy agenda that includes an overhaul of the tax code, repeal of the Affordable Care Act, infrastructure spending and broad deregulation.

Mr. Trump faces delays in the confirmation of the Treasury, commerce and labor secretaries and the budget director. The hearing for Steven Mnuchin, Mr. Trump's intended nominee for Treasury secretary, is set for Thursday, the day before inauguration.

Committees delayed hearings set last week for Wilbur Ross Jr., the intended nominee for commerce secretary, until Wednesday, and for Andrew Puzder, Mr. Trump's pick for labor secretary, until February. Hearings for Rep. Mick Mulvaney (R., S.C.), tapped as the budget director, haven't been set.

Other selections still haven't been announced. Mr. Trump hasn't said who he will name as agriculture secretary and the new administration hasn't tapped anyone to chair the Council of Economic Advisers. Transition officials considered CNBC commentator Lawrence Kudlow for the job, but he appears unlikely to receive the post because he has been critical of Mr. Trump's trade positions, according to a person familiar with the discussion.

"It's going to be a challenge. People are going to have to get up to speed very quickly," said Jacob Lew, the outgoing Treasury secretary, in an interview last week.

The delays are due in part to routine vetting by congressional committees. Democratic opposition hasn't proven to be a setback yet, though partisan roadblocks could further slow the process. Senate committees began confirmation proceedings for seven of Mr. Trump's cabinet nominees last week, including his picks for attorney general and the secretaries of state and defense.

Such delays aren't unprecedented. In 2009, Congress delayed the hearing of Treasury Secretary Timothy Geithner until the day after President Barack Obama's inauguration, when the Senate Finance Committee's review of Mr. Geithner's tax returns showed he had failed to pay more than $34,000 in federal taxes over several years. Mr. Geithner said those had been innocent mistakes, and he was confirmed less than a week after inauguration of that year.

Mr. Geithner later saw top picks for key Treasury posts withdraw from consideration, prompting Paul Volcker, the former Federal Reserve chairman advising Mr. Obama on economic issues, to call the lack of staff "shameful."

Mr. Obama's first two picks for commerce secretary also withdrew from consideration, and the position remained vacant until late March. Tax issues also led Mr. Obama's pick to head the Department of Health and Human Services, former Sen. Tom Daschle, to withdraw from consideration.

Until Mr. Geithner's delay, Treasury secretaries for incoming administrations had been confirmed on Jan. 20 or Jan. 21, extending back through the Carter administration. Likewise, the budget director has traditionally been confirmed within one week of the inauguration.

Any confirmation delay could be especially important shaping the initial proposals advanced by the administration given Mr. Mnuchin's desire for the Treasury to serve as the locus of economic policy-making. The same goes for regulatory policy and Mr. Mulvaney, given the central role the Office of Management and Budget plays in finalizing new regulations and unraveling old ones.

Mr. Trump has appointed several economic advisers to West Wing positions that don't require Senate confirmation, including former Goldman Sachs President Gary Cohn as the director of the National Economic Council and economist Peter Navarro to lead a new National Trade Council. If it takes longer for cabinet nominees to be seated, those West Wing advisers and their deputies could play an even greater role in shepherding policy.

Trump transition officials didn't respond to requests for comment.

While the U.S. economy doesn't face the same crisis that Mr. Obama inherited upon his inauguration eight years ago, the Treasury Department will assume responsibility for a series of international financial challenges that have required constant attention in recent months, including capital flight from China, the still-shaky state of European banks and potential economic turbulence in Brazil and Mexico.

Other tasks could become pressing. Officials will have to prepare a budget proposal by February laying out their plans for the domestic economy and may have to take special measures to avoid breaching the federal debt ceiling after mid-March, when a suspension of that ceiling expires.

The Treasury will also have to prepare the semiannual foreign-exchange report due in April, in which Mr. Trump has promised to take issue with what he has said is China's effort to gain an unfair trade advantage through currency intervention.

"You do not know what is going to happen in the world, particularly in the post-9/11 world, where economics should be seen as a part of national security," said Stephen Myrow, a former Treasury official in the George W. Bush administration who is now managing partner of Beacon Policy Advisers LLC.

If Mr. Mnuchin, the Treasury secretary-designate, isn't confirmed by Friday, another Treasury official would become acting secretary. That role is likely to be taken by Adam Szubin, the acting undersecretary for terrorism and financial intelligence, according to people familiar with the matter.

Confirmation delays could reflect the intricate finances of the businessmen selected by Mr. Trump, requiring more preparation for Senate aides tasked with combing through financial disclosures.

"You've never had this many people with these complicated financial disclosures all at once," said Mr. Myrow. Delays could also arise if the Trump transition team announced nominees before having cabinet picks submit extensive paperwork required, including tax returns, for congressional committees to review, he said.

WSJ Pro: ECB Survey Suggests Easing Credit Conditions in Eurozone

By Todd Buell

FRANKFURT Eurozone banks expect credit standards to ease in the current quarter, while demand for funding is also expected to pick up, a quarterly survey showed Tuesday, offering policy makers signs of economic vitality as they meet here this week.

An overwhelming majority said that standards remained broadly unchanged, the European Central Bank's latest Bank Lending Survey found.

For the current quarter, banks expect a net easing of credit standards for all categories, according to the survey. This includes corporate loans, housing loans and consumer credit.

Analysts said the results showed a steady firming of growth in the eurozone, while revealing a worrying low level of borrowing to fund new projects.

"For now, business loan demand is failing to pick up steam," said Teunis Brosens an economist at ING Bank.

The results will still probably be welcome news for the ECB's policy-setting Governing Council as it gathers here for its meeting. The Council is expected to leave policy unchanged after agreeing in December to extend its asset-purchase program until the end of this year, albeit at a lower volume from April. The ECB will announce its policy decision on Thursday.

The eurozone economy is generally more reliant on bank lending than the U.S., meaning that weak bank health and reluctance to lend can be a significant hindrance to growth in the currency bloc. The ECB has tried to set policy since the onset of the economic crisis in a way to ease conditions for banks to allow them to lend to households and firms.

The ECB survey, which polls lenders across the currency bloc, did show that in aggregate terms more banks said they had tightened standards on loans to companies in the fourth quarter than eased.

This was the first tightening of standards for business loans since the fourth quarter of 2013, though the shift was "driven mainly by developments in the Netherlands," the ECB said.

The ECB survey expresses its results in terms of "net percentage", which means the difference between the share of banks that report a tightening of standards versus those that report an easing.

The survey showed that a bigger share of banks reported an increase in demand for business loans in the fourth quarter than a decrease, though still about 70% reported no change.

Banks expect this positive trend to continue in the early parts of this year. Net demand for housing loans and for consumer credit was up significantly in the fourth quarter.

"For the first quarter of 2017, banks expected a continuing increase in net demand for housing loans and consumer credit," the survey said.

U.K. Leader to Seek Clean Break From EU

By Jenny Gross

LONDON Prime Minister Theresa May is set to declare Tuesday that the U.K. wants a clean break from the European Union, in a closely watched speech in which she is expected to lay out her plans for the divorce.

Mrs. May is expected to say that Britain doesn't want "partial membership" in the EU "or anything that leaves us half-in, half-out," according to excerpts of a speech released by her office on Monday.

Previous such comments, interpreted to mean that Britain is heading toward a looser relationship with the EU and could lose access to the bloc's common market, sent the pound tumbling. The currency dropped to three-month lows against the dollar in European markets Monday.

"We do not seek to hold on to bits of membership as we leave," Mrs. May's prepared remarks said. Instead, Britain will seek a new type of relationship with the EU, according to the remarks. The prime minister is to deliver the address to a group of diplomats and other officials in London on Tuesday.

The prime minister has repeatedly said London wants to control immigration. EU leaders have said the U.K. can't impose restrictions on EU citizens' ability to live and work in the U.K. and retain its existing economic relationship, which includes unfettered access to the EU's market of 440 million consumers.

The speech excerpts released by her office made no reference to the single market.

Politicians who campaigned for Brexit said the U.K. will be better off once it is outside the single market and can negotiate its own trade deals with countries elsewhere. They say the U.K. will thrive if it is no longer bound to EU regulations and required to abide by the bloc's free movement of people principle.

Tim Farron, leader of the pro-EU Liberal Democrats, said that Mrs. May is delivering "a destructive, hard Brexit and the consequences will be felt by millions of people through higher prices, greater instability and rising fuel costs."

In recent weeks, Mrs. May has come under increasing pressure to spell out details of her vision for Britain's exit as the country prepares to give formal notice at the end of March that it will leave.

Mrs. May has broadly outlined her priorities. In addition to immigration, she has said she wants to remove the U.K. from the jurisdiction of the European Court of Justice, while maintaining good trading terms with Europe. She hasn't said whether she wants to stay in Europe's single market.

"We want to buy your goods, sell you ours, trade with you as freely as possible, and work with one another to make sure we are all safer, more secure and more prosperous through continued friendship," Mrs. May will say, according to the excerpts. She will also say she wants the U.K. post-Brexit to be "a magnet for international talent."

"I want us to be a truly global Britain the best friend and neighbor to our European partners, but a country that reaches beyond the borders of Europe too," she is expected to say.

Negotiations between Britain and the remaining 27 EU governments are expected to be lengthy and contentious.

In a boost for Brexit supporters, President-elect Donald Trump said in a weekend interview with the Times of London that the U.S. would work hard to come to a quick trade deal with the U.K. Downing Street welcomed those comments on Monday, saying a U.K.-U.S. trade deal is one of the opportunities of Britain's exit from the EU.

Past trade deals have taken years to complete, and some officials have cast doubt on whether the U.K. can quickly hammer out a quick trade deal with the U.S.

 Jason Douglas contributed to this article.

WSJ Pro: Chinese Insurers Scale Back Risky Products as New Rules Bite

By Chao Deng

China's clampdown on high-yielding insurance products is taking a bite out of one high-profile issuer and the network of domestic banks that has helped to peddle them.

Analysts say the withdrawal of a property insurance product by Anbang Insurance Group Co. is evidence that recent regulations by the insurance regulator are having an impact on the industry.

A person familiar with the situation said the firm had by Jan. 5 stopped selling "Anbang Win-Win #3," a three-year product that yielded more than 5% annually and had been distributed by China's largest banks. Industrial and Commercial Bank of China, China Construction Bank, Industrial Bank Co. and China Merchants Bank are among the banks that have stopped selling the policy, according to a local media report by Caixin.

China's insurance regulator has been trying to rein in sales of wealth-management products that insurers have masked as universal life products. Authorities view the products as risky because insurers have taken the proceeds to pump into stocks and property as well as investment overseas. Such activity, analysts say, puts the nation's financial system at risk if investments go under or insurers abuse their newfound power in listed firms.

While the policy that Anbang pulled is a property policy, it typifies the kind of high-yielding products regulators are trying to curb and shows the firm is falling in line with the spirit of the law, according to analysts.

The insurance regulator said in late December that it will bar insurers from opening new subsidiaries within one year if premiums on their short-term savings products account for more than 50% of their total premium income in the current quarter. This followed a tightening round in September, in which insurers were given a grace period until 2019 to lower their share of short- and medium-term products to below 50% of total premiums.

"I think [the rules] are very strong incentive for insurers to stop selling these short-term universal life products," said Sally Yim, senior vice president at Moody's Investors Service in Hong Kong. "Insurers will have to expand less quickly and if they still want to open new branches, they have to meet the requirement for that 50% rule."

The person familiar with the matter said that the pulling of Win-Win #3 was a regular part of Anbang's product renewal cycle and that the deadline was communicated to users of Anbang's mobile app.

Moody's Agrees to Settle Financial Crisis-Era Claims for $864 Million

By Aruna Viswanatha and Josh Beckerman

Moody's Corp. said Friday it agreed to pay $864 million to the U.S. Department of Justice and several states in connection with bond grades it issued before the 2008 housing-market collapse.

The agreement marks what is expected to be the last in a series of deals in the waning days of President Barack Obama's administration closing investigations into the financial crisis.

Late last month, the Justice Department struck multibillion-dollar settlements with Deutsche Bank AG and Credit Suisse Group AG over toxic securities and separately filed a lawsuit against Barclays PLC alleging misconduct in billions of dollars in mortgage securities sold in the mid-2000s.

Barclays said it would seek to dismiss the case and said it believed the claims were "disconnected from the facts."

Moody's had said in October it expected the Justice Department to sue the company in connection with its mortgage-bond ratings, but many companies and government officials have scrambled to close out long-running investigations in advance of President-elect Donald Trump taking office next week.

"Moody's failed to adhere to its own credit rating standards and fell short on its pledge of transparency in the run-up to the Great Recession," said Bill Baer, the Justice Department's No. 3 official.

Moody's said in a statement it determined the agreement removed "significant legacy legal risk" and avoided "costs and uncertainty associated with continued investigations and litigation." The firm said it "stands behind" the integrity of its ratings and methodologies.

The firm said in the agreement that it, at times, deviated from methodologies it said it would use to rate mortgage bonds and used a more lenient standard on some complicated bonds than it had disclosed.

Moody's didn't admit in the out-of-court settlement that the conduct violated the law.

"Moody's stands behind the integrity of its ratings, methodologies and processes, and the settlement contains no finding of any violation of law, nor any admission of liability," it said.

Nearly every top global bank has paid tens of billions of dollars to resolve claims that they packaged risky mortgages into securities and marketed them to investors as safer than they knew them to be. Ratings firms, which gave many of the bonds top grades, had initially eluded liability and argued their analysis was protected as free speech under the First Amendment.

After fighting a Justice Department lawsuit for two years, Moody's rival, S&P Global Ratings, agreed in 2015 to pay $1.5 billion to resolve similar claims.

The Moody's settlement includes a $437.5 million civil penalty to the Justice Department and $426.3 million to 21 states and the District of Columbia.

Moody's said it would record the financial impact of the payments in its fourth-quarter 2016 results. It estimated the impact of an after-tax charge of about $702 million, or about $3.62 a share.

Under the pact, Moody's agreed to maintain multiple policy changes, including separating its commercial and credit-rating functions and exclude analysts from any commercial discussions.

Zohar CLO Funds Target Lynn Tilton in $1 Billion Lawsuit

By Peg Brickley

The Zohar investment funds at the heart of Lynn Tilton's $2.5 billion distressed-debt empire sued their founder Monday, accusing Ms. Tilton of pillaging more than $1 billion from investors and the troubled companies she manages.

Through a "toxic mix of fraud, theft and mismanagement," Ms. Tilton stole money from the Zohar funds and from the troubled companies, siphoning hundreds of millions of dollars in fees and assets from a souring loan portfolio and failing businesses, according to the lawsuit filed in federal court in New York.

The Zohar funds are collateralized loan obligations created by Ms. Tilton and packed with loans to troubled companies managed by her and her New York-based Patriarch Partners investment firm. Their lawsuit claims that Patriarch Partners was essentially a black box, and only Ms. Tilton knew what was inside. Once the Zohar financial machinery began to falter, Ms. Tilton stepped away and new managers got a look at the books.

Ms. Tilton, under the guise of multiple companies that the lawsuit calls "the Patriarch Enterprise," was running a racket, not a turnaround empire, the funds allege.

"The Patriarch Enterprise's repeated, continuous, and flagrant violations of law constitute a pattern of racketeering activity' in violation of civil racketeering laws, according to the lawsuit. "Their racketeering activities, both individually and collectively, have been ongoing since at least 2006 and pose a serious threat of continuing criminal conduct into the foreseeable future."

A Tilton spokesman said Monday he was not immediately able to comment on the lawsuit. Ms. Tilton in the past has defended her handling of the investment funds and the companies, denying any wrongdoing or improper fees.

Fraud allegations have been mounting for months against the flashy financier with an Ivy League education and a Wall Street background. She is already battling fraud charges from the Securities and Exchange Commission, and a lawsuit from a German bank Norddeutsche Landesbank Girozentrale, or Nord.

The SEC and the German bank each accuse her of mishandling the Zohar funds, collateralized loan obligation vehicles Ms. Tilton created to attract investor dollars into the risky business of lending to troubled companies. A decision is awaited on the SEC charges, which she denies, and on Ms. Tilton's bid to squash the Nord suit.

Ms. Tilton created the first Zohar fund in 2003 and ran all three until March 2016, when she resigned. She has said she has put more than $440 million of her own funds into the Zohar funds and the Patriarch collection of troubled companies.

The first Zohar fund defaulted in 2015, a second is due to default this month, and new managers of the funds that took over when Ms. Tilton resigned, Alvarez & Marsal, want her to give back hundreds of millions of dollars she allegedly pilfered and to relinquish her claims on the companies. Investors believe there's significant value in the portfolio companies, and have sued to oust her from control so they can sell or restructure the businesses.

"While Ms. Tilton has made herself famously wealthy, she has left nothing but losses in her wake for the Zohar Fund investors who trusted in her integrity and promises," according to the lawsuit.

The new suit may be the toughest test yet for Ms. Tilton, who kept the financial details of her turnaround operation under wraps for years. From their post as fund managers, Alvarez & Marsal got an inside look at Ms. Tilton's Patriarch Partners investment and management firm, an array of at least 20 different units, the workings of which had long been kept from public view.

Ms. Tilton wooed ratings firms and investors, based on her talent for spotting value in well known but financially troubled companies like Rand McNally and Spiegel. Using Zohar funds, she gained control of them, and worked to bring them back to financial health. Interviewed by Barbara Walters, pictured with furs and helicopters for magazine and newspaper profiles some glowing, some damning Ms. Tilton was the bejeweled, leather-clad, smiling public face of what was for over a decade a very private business.

Not even investors knew exactly what was in the Zohar funds, multiple sources told The Wall Street Journal. In 2015, SEC charges of fraud opened a crack in the facade, and lawyers for Nord, a German bank that lost money on its Zohar investments, began piecing together information and found what appeared to be equity in funds that were supposed to be composed of less-risky debt investments.

Ms. Tilton has said investors banked on her talent for spotting turnaround candidates and restoring them to financial health. That took time, money and commitment, she said. In defending herself in the SEC case, she argued she was entitled to amend loan terms to give distressed businesses more time to recover. That discretion, she said, benefited the Zohar investors as well as the companies.

There were successes in what was generally known as the Patriarch portfolio of businesses, and they paid dividends. But the Zohar funds never saw those dividends, according to new fund manager Alvarez & Marsal. Instead, Ms. Tilton claimed the dividends as owner of the companies, according to the new Zohar suit.

Who owns the companies in the Patriarch portfolio is a question that's already being fought over in courts in New York, where Nord sued, and Delaware, where Alvarez & Marsal sued. Nord says it was bilked into investing in equity in troubled companies, as opposed to debt, which carries less risk in a workout situation. Ms. Tilton says the Zohar investors knew full well they were getting equity as part of their investment. She has moved to have the Nord case thrown out.

In Delaware, Alvarez & Marsal has moved to oust Ms. Tilton from control of some of the more valuable companies, including game-controller maker PDP, on the grounds that the Zohar funds own the businesses. In response to Alvarez & Marsal, Ms. Tilton insists she owns the businesses, and has the irrevocable right to control them.

Who owns the companies is an important point, since warding off a default in the last Zohar fund, which comes due in 2019, likely means selling the businesses, or taking other action to refurbish them. Ms. Tilton has said she put more than $440 million into the companies and Zohar funds, but was not willing to continue financing the portfolio companies once she had relinquished control of the loan funds.

However, for months after Ms. Tilton handed over control of the Zohar funds to Alvarez & Marsal, Patriarch-run businesses allegedly refused to accept calls from their lender. Executives of several portfolio companies directed all inquiries to Patriarch, a lawyer for the Zohar funds told a Delaware judge last year.

The judge ordered Ms. Tilton and Patriarch to start handing over documents, a decision she's still appealing.

Corrections & Amplifications:

An earlier version of this article incorrectly said the Zohar complaint alleges some $100 million of dividends were diverted. No figure was cited in the lawsuit.

Supreme Court to Review Time Limits on SEC  Disgorgements'

By Brent Kendall and Jess Bravin

WASHINGTON The Supreme Court said Friday it would decide whether employers can prohibit groups of workers from suing over labor disputes by requiring each employee to submit individual claims through private arbitration.

The case was one of 13 new matters the justices added to their docket, breaking with a monthslong pattern in which the court operating with an open seat that leaves it more vulnerable to tie votes has generally accepted few new cases for review. Among the other new cases is one examining the powers of the Securities and Exchange Commission to relax time limits for requiring money managers and others to forfeit ill-gotten gains from fraudulent conduct.

The arbitration case amounts to a dispute over which of two federal laws takes precedence: the National Labor Relations Act, which authorizes employees to take collective action for "mutual aid or protection," or the Federal Arbitration Act, which provides that arbitration clauses generally are "valid, irrevocable and enforceable."

The Supreme Court in a series of pro-business rulings has given wide sweep to arbitration clauses, and companies have been expanding use of the device to funnel consumer and employee disputes into private arbitration and out of court.

Businesses say arbitration is cheaper and more efficient than suits in court. Consumer and labor groups say the private forum is stacked against them and prevents public disclosure of alleged corporate misconduct. The groups also say it effectively eliminates the opportunity to bring class-actions where a large number of individuals sue collectively.

The high court took up three different disputes that examine the use of arbitration in employment agreements and will consider them in one consolidated case. Lower courts are divided on the enforceability of arbitration clauses in the labor context.

One dispute involves an appeal by the National Labor Relations Board, which said an arbitration agreement imposed by Murphy USA Inc. violated the rights of gas station workers who wanted to sue over alleged labor violations. A federal appeals court in New Orleans disagreed and sided with the company.

A different appeals court sided with workers at accounting firm Ernst & Young who wanted to bring collective claims for overtime in court instead of being forced into arbitration. The third case involves an appeal by Epic Systems Corp., a health-care software company, after a lower court ruled its arbitration clause couldn't prevent workers from joining together to bring wage-and-hour claims in court.

The Supreme Court could hear oral arguments in the case as soon as this spring.

Among the other new cases, the court:

 Will rule on whether a five-year statute of limitations in federal law applies to SEC orders that require financial firms and individuals to "disgorge" orders in which defendants in SEC civil cases must give back ill-gotten gains such as salary or stock. The Supreme Court ruled in 2013 that the five-year statute of limitations does apply to SEC claims for monetary penalties but didn't say whether it applies to disgorgement. Federal appellate courts are split on the issue.

The case revolves around an appeal by New Mexico-based investment adviser Charles Kokesh, who is seeking to avoid paying $34.9 million in disgorgement sought by the SEC. The SEC in 2009 charged that Mr. Kokesh misappropriated money from several business-development companies he ran to pay for expenses such as salaries and bonuses, according to the agency's complaint.

 Will consider how strictly filing deadlines in certain shareholder class actions should be enforced. The issue involves class actions that are filed before the deadline, typically a three-year or five-year period, that later are dismissed. Lower courts disagree over whether other investors can pursue the claims if they can demonstrate they would have qualified for the original plaintiff class. The case before the court involves an appeal by California's public employees' pension fund, which is seeking to bring claims related to the collapse of Lehman Brothers in 2008.

 Will decide whether challenges to a controversial Obama administration clean water regulation should have been heard directly in an appeals court or whether the lawsuits should have been considered by trial courts first. An appeals court already has put the regulation on hold during the litigation, but some challengers say it would have been fairer for federal district judges to review the cases first. They say the confusion has caused needless delay. President-elect Donald Trump has called for the water regulation to be repealed.

  Andrew Ackerman contributed to this article.

EU, U.S. Reach Trans-Atlantic Agreement Over Insurance Rules

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine and Jacob M. Schlesinger

The U.S. and European Union have reached a trans-Atlantic agreement to smooth the ability of insurance companies to operate in the two regions, officials said Friday.

The agreement, reached after more than 20 years of discussions and almost a year of formal negotiations, aims to remove regulatory uncertainty for American insurers that do business in Europe. Meanwhile, EU reinsurers will benefit from changes to collateral requirements that could save them hundreds of millions of dollars.

American International Group Inc. and MetLife Inc. are among the biggest U.S. insurers with European operations. AIG declined to comment. MetLife didn't have an immediate comment.

"This is a very welcome development for U.S. insurers" as it "will enable them to write business in the EU insurance market and will alleviate their long-standing concern that lack of equivalence would restrict their ability to be globally competitive in an ever-increasingly competitive insurance marketplace," said Howard Mills, global insurance regulatory leader for consultancy Deloitte Services LP and a former New York state insurance commissioner.

Trade associations representing reinsurers and insurance companies across the U.S.  the American Insurance Association, the American Council of Life Insurers and the Reinsurance Association of America in a joint statement said they welcome the deal, saying it "seeks to resolve significant insurance and reinsurance regulatory issues for companies doing business in both jurisdictions."

Negotiators worked to close out an agreement in the final days of President Barack Obama's administration, in part because the U.S. political transition could have led to further delays as new officials take over for the incoming Trump administration.

Under the agreement, American insurers won't be subject to new EU insurance regulations, known as Solvency II, that went into effect a year ago.

Europe's regime seeks to ensure insurers have enough capital to weather severe losses and to establish unified requirements across the bloc. The rules apply only to European firms, but EU representatives had called for "equivalent" regulatory oversight of non-European insurers operating in EU countries.

EU reinsurers could save about $400 million a year in collateral costs in the U.S., according to the European Commission, the EU's executive arm, because collateral requirements for reinsurers operating overseas will be eliminated.

A third pillar of the deal, which isn't binding, is a set of principles outlining how American and European insurance supervisors should exchange information with one another to mitigate risks in the sector.

EU national governments and the European Parliament have to approve the agreement. The commission hopes to expedite the process by requesting to sign on behalf of member states, which shortens the process.

The Office of the U.S. Trade Representative and the Treasury Department, which also joined in the negotiations, sent letters to Congress notifying members of the deal, kicking off the 90-day scrutiny period before U.S. authorities can sign the agreement.

"This agreement will provide opportunities for U.S. insurers and reinsurers doing business in the EU while continuing to ensure a high standard of protection for U.S. and EU consumers," U.S. Trade Representative Michael Froman said in a statement.

Citadel to Pay Over $22 Million for Misleading Clients

By Dave Michaels and Alexander Osipovich

U.S. regulators made Citadel Securities LLC pay $22.6 million for failing to fill customer orders at the best price, nabbing their first offender in the lucrative side market for executing retail stock orders.

Citadel Securities, which handles more retail trades than any other brokerage, agreed to settle claims that it didn't always obtain the best price for orders despite giving assurances that it would. The trading firm, owned by billionaire investor Kenneth Griffin, handles more than one in three retail orders that once would have gone straight to stock exchanges, promising better prices and speedier executions.

The practice in which Citadel and other high-tech "wholesalers" pay retail brokers to execute mom-and-pop orders has drawn increased scrutiny in recent years. At a Senate hearing in June 2014, Sen. Carl Levin (D., Mich.) questioned TD Ameritrade Holding Corp. about whether such deals simply reap windfalls for retail brokers instead of ensuring the best price and trade execution for their clients.

Joe Saluzzi, a co-founder of brokerage Themis Trading LLC, said Friday that "this is all about a system that's been rigged and broken for many, many years."

Firms such as Citadel, which handles 35% of the average daily volume for retail shares, noted the SEC has approved the practice, known as payment for order flow. It results in better prices for retail clients than trading on exchanges, these firms say.

Zia Ahmed, a Citadel spokesman, said the firm takes "very seriously our obligations to comply fully with all laws and regulations" and is "committed to providing superior service and execution quality to our clients."

The Securities and Exchange Commission's civil probe targeted two specific algorithms that Citadel used to execute about 2.6% of retail orders from 2008 to 2010. The algorithms decided how Citadel would fill an order when prices from a slower feed differed from prices on a richer and faster view that is sold by exchanges. When the two sources diverged, an algorithm known as FastFill used the price shown on the slower feed, which resulted in a worse price for "a substantial number of smaller orders," the SEC's order said.

Because of the way FastFill worked, some orders for larger numbers of shares actually received a better price, the SEC said.

Another algorithm, called SmartProvide, delayed the immediate execution of retail orders when the slower feed showed a better price than the faster one, the SEC said. In those cases, Citadel sent the order to the broader market, which sometimes netted clients a better price. In other cases, some orders that came back to Citadel got a worse price than if they had been immediately filled at the quote shown on the slow tape, the SEC's order said.

The Securities and Exchange Commission's action faulted Citadel because its agreements with retail broker-dealers didn't fully explain how the algorithms worked, and only said Citadel would get the best available price for clients. Under the agreement, Citadel will pay a $16 million penalty and forfeit $5.2 million in profits and $1.4 million in interest. Citadel didn't admit or deny the allegations.

"The penalty reflects that it's a substantial amount for serious conduct," Robert Cohen, co-chief of the SEC's market abuse unit, said on a conference call with reporters. "It's a difficult area to penetrate if you are an investor or even a regulator, and it affected a lot of orders."

Retail brokers who supply Citadel with orders include Charles Schwab, Ameritrade, Scottrade Financial Services and E*Trade Financial Corp. Retail brokers shouldn't be faulted because, on average, their customers benefit from trading with market makers such as Citadel, said David Weisberger, head of trading analytics at IHS Markit Ltd.

"The size of the fine has nothing to do with the impact on investors and everything to do with disclosure," Mr. Weisberger said.

Critics such as IEX Group Inc. and T. Rowe Price Group Inc. have complained that speedy trading firms could exploit arbitrage opportunities created by the gap between the slower feed, known as the consolidated tape, and faster feeds sold by the stock exchanges. That gap has narrowed in recent years as exchanges, under pressure from regulators and money managers, committed to upgrade the slower tape's technology.

Corrections & Amplifications:

The Securities and Exchange Commission found that Citadel Securities LLC didn't use algorithms that would have found clients the best prices. A previous version of this article incorrectly stated that Citadel did use the algorithms and referred to the SEC as the "Security and Exchange Commission." Also, the name of the earlier article's contributing reporter is Imani Moise. The first name was misspelled as "Imami." (Jan. 13, 2017)

Fiduciary Rule Guidance Unveiled as Implementation Deadline Looms

By Lisa Beilfuss

The Labor Department published two more batches of guidance aimed at investors and the wealth-adviser industry over the new rule on retirement-savings advice, a move that keeps the so-called fiduciary rule's April implementation deadline on track amid mounting Republican opposition.

The fiduciary rule, unveiled last spring, requires brokers and advisers who work with tax-advantaged retirement savings to work in the best interest of their clients and generally avoid conflicts of interest, which can arise with commission-based compensation. Previously, professionals were held to a less-rigorous standard that required only suitable advice.

In one list of frequently asked questions released Friday, the Labor Department focuses on investor rights, the fate of commission-generating accounts and advisers' responsibilities under the fiduciary rule. The FAQs are meant to help the brokerage industry be in compliance with the stricter standards by April 10 and to continue informing investors of the coming changes.

The department highlights in its FAQs that the rule doesn't require advisers move clients from commission-based accounts to fee-based accounts. Advisers act as fiduciaries when they recommend the proper type of account, the department said, nodding to concerns that smaller investors who do few annual trades will wind up paying more in fees than they might in commissions.

Under the rule, a fiduciary is anyone paid for making an investment recommendation specific to a client's needs about retirement accounts. Covered recommendations include advice about buying, holding or selling securities or other investment property in retirement accounts, including recommendations to roll over an account, buy an annuity or take a distribution from a 401(k) plan or IRA.

When it comes to defining "best interest," the Labor Department said advisers' obligation isn't perfection, but rather that he or she "make recommendations that adhere to a professional standard of care and that are based on your financial interest, without regard to his or her own competing financial interests." It doesn't require that advisers "somehow scour the market and identify the single best investment product for you out of all the investments in that vast market."

The second list of FAQs released Friday focuses on technical compliance issues. With that set of guidance, the Labor Department clarifies the types of investment recommendations and communications bound by the rule and discusses how the rule treats third-party administrators.

The publication of the guidance comes as speculation swirls that the rule will be delayed and either killed or watered down. While President-elect Donald Trump hasn't publicly addressed the fiduciary rule, he has appointed high-level officials who are vocal opponents of the rule.

Meanwhile, Republican control of Congress makes delay more likely and potential dismantling easier. Last week, Rep. Joe Wilson (R., S.C.) introduced a bill to delay the rule's implementation date by two years, a move he says is meant to give Mr. Trump "adequate time to re-evaluate" the regulation. Mr. Wilson said he expects to get his bill to the House floor before April 10.

Despite uncertainty over the rule's fate, many brokers are moving forward with efforts to comply with the more rigorous standard come April. As such, the industry has been anticipating the FAQs issued by the Labor Department for more clarity on the rule's reach, the terms of its exemptions and investors' potential recourse if they feel their best interest wasn't served.

How a Growth Push Derailed CAN Capital, a Big Lender to Small Businesses

By Peter Rudegeair

An ill-fated growth push has tripped up one of the oldest, most-successful online lenders to small businesses.

CAN Capital Inc. was forced to stop making new loans late last year after it discovered problems in how the company reported borrower delinquencies, according to people familiar with the matter. The errors were significant enough, the people said, to cause the company to breach covenants with its big-bank creditors such as Wells Fargo & Co. and push out three top executives.

To help turn the business around, the firm recently hired restructuring firm Realization Services Inc. for assistance in negotiating with creditors and brought on investment bank Jefferies Group LLC for advice on strategic alternatives, The Wall Street Journal reported Friday.

Acting Chief Executive Parris Sanz, a lawyer who took over the top spot at the company in November, said in an interview earlier this month that the problem affected a tiny amount of the company's loans.

"We hit a bump in the road and blew out a tire," said Mr. Sanz. "We just need to change out the tire, and we'll be back on the road."

CAN Capital's struggles are the latest setback for the fast-growing financial-technology sector, particularly online lending, in which lenders use algorithms to automate credit decisions and fund loans with investor money rather than customer deposits.

Resurgent competition from banks and cooling investor demand for many online loans have hurt companies such as LendingClub Corp., Prosper Marketplace Inc. and On Deck Capital Inc. LendingClub, the largest online consumer lender by historical loan-origination volume, replaced its CEO after loan-disclosure problems last May.

Ultimately, CAN Capital's problems can be traced in large part to a decision made around five years ago to expand rapidly into new markets without putting in place proper internal controls. "It became clear that our business has grown and evolved faster than some of our internal processes," a spokeswoman said at the time of the executive changes.

What makes CAN Capital's's situation particularly painful is its long track record. Over its nearly two-decade history, it has issued about $6.5 billion in loans and merchant cash advances, in which the company agrees to fund a small business in exchange for a percentage for its future sales, the company has said.

Some advocacy groups criticized the cash advances as having high fees, but CAN Capital grew, signing up big banks as partners. Wells Fargo has referred customers who were declined for a loan from its merchant-services division to CAN Capital for the past six years.

Around five years ago, CAN Capital started to shift its approach to resemble its startup competitors. The company named a new CEO, Daniel DeMeo, and moved its headquarters in the New York suburb of Scarsdale, N.Y., to Manhattan's trendy Meatpacking District. Venture-capital firms including Accel Partners and Meritech Capital Partners invested $63 million into the lender, which one investor called the "best-kept secret in the emerging financial innovation boom."

The early signs from the changes were encouraging. In 2015, CAN announced it had attracted $650 million in debt backing from Wells Fargo, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., Morgan Stanley and other large banks The same year, the company had around $29 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, up from about $19 million in 2014, according to people familiar with the matter.

But as the firm shifted to compete with online lenders, CAN's loan systems allowed some employees to grant borrowers a few extra days to get payments made beyond what was stated in loan agreements, people familiar with the situation said. Such "grace days" were more common in cash advances, and typically don't happen in the loan business unless there is a formal modification. Otherwise, the loans need to be declared delinquent.

Last summer, top CAN executives learned the issue was widespread enough to cause the firm to misstate delinquencies to investors and creditors. In November, the board of directors put Mr. DeMeo, along with the company's chief financial officer and chief risk officer, on leave. They aren't expected to return, the people say.

Around that time, banks involved in CAN Capital's credit facility, led by Wells Fargo, said CAN's issues put it in violation of covenants governing how much it was permitted to borrow, according to people familiar with the matter. As part of a December letter notifying CAN's less-senior creditors about the breach, the banks said certain debt payments starting in March could be at risk, these people said.

Mr. Sanz says CAN officials are in talks with Wells Fargo and other banks to fix the issues and that only 3% of the loans funded with the credit facility were considered ineligible. CAN is looking to sell more than $100 million in loans and assets to strengthen its financial position and has laid off about 250 staffers in Georgia and Costa Rica, or 55% of its overall workforce, according to people familiar with the matter.

Mr. Sanz said the asset sale and other steps are part of plans to raise additional liquidity for lenders and "position us for success in 2017."

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

CSRC Chairman's Saturday Meeting Spooks Local Investors

The Shanghai Composite closes down 0.3% Monday after dropping as much as 2.2% in afternoon trade following rumors of tighter regulation on margin financing, say analysts at three brokerages in China. They did not wish to be named. Nearly 10 initial public share sales are expected to hit the market this week, to raise a combined (approximate) 4 billion yuan ($579.91 million), threatening to drain liquidity from other stocks. Also, market regulator China Securities Regulatory Commission held a meeting on Saturday on stock refinancing, backdoor listing and a new listing board, say analysts. "There was some speculators suggesting this might signal another round of tighter regulation, however this is purely just a rumor at this stage," said David Millhouse, head of China research at Forsyth Barr Asia. There was a big amount of large cap buying coming in late in the day when the Shanghai Composite got below 3050, he said. (kenan.machado@wsj.com)

China Regulator: Insurer Eavesdropped on Inspectors During Visit, Xinhua Reports

A Chinese insurance regulator said a state insurance firm used an iPhone and a voice recorder to eavesdrop on inspectors, according to the official Xinhua News Service. The eavesdropping devices were found taped under chairs at the Weifang office of Yingda Taihe Property Insurance, which was being inspected, according to a branch of the China Insurance Regulatory Commission based in northeastern Shandong province. Yingda said four employees had been removed from their posts and were under investigation, according to Xinhua. "In a modern society when everyone is called upon to learn and obey the law, executives and employees of a state-owned insurance company adopted such gravely improper means to confront inspection," the regulator said in a statement, Xinhua reported. "They need to rectify their work ethic, morals and legal consciousness." (mark.magnier@wsj.com; @MarkMagnier)

Australia Regulator Focusing on Lending Standards

Australia's prudential regulator appears to be more interested in addressing systemic risks for banks through a focus on lending standards rather than further building up buffers, Deutsche Bank says. The regulator opted to maintain the "countercyclical capital buffer" at zero, despite what Deutsche Bank says was a risk it would set a small buffer to reflect housing growth running ahead of nominal GDP growth, still high household indebtedness and similar moves to lift the capital buffer in other countries. The decision is a small positive for Aussie banks, and a sensible approach by the regulator, Deutsche reckons. (robb.stewart@wsj.com; Twitter: @RobbMStewart)

Fitch Turns Negative on Australian Banks

Rising macroeconomic risks and pressure on profit growth prompts Fitch to cut its 2017 outlook on Australian banks to negative as household debt is high and rising relative to disposable income making borrowers sensitive to changes in the job market and interest rates. The ratings firm also thinks banks' earnings growth is likely to continue slowing this year amid low rates, competition, higher funding costs and a rise in loan-impairment charges. Fitch, though, keeps its ratings view on banks while being watchful of the previously stated risks. The move comes as Australia's prudential regulator opted against requiring banks to hold more capital, leaving its "counter-cyclical buffer" at zero given no observed change in the level of systemic risk. (robb.stewart@wsj.com; @RobbMStewart)

BSE Exchange Releases Estimated IPO Terms

India's BSE is set to raise as much as INR12.43B ($182.3M) from its IPO next week, with Asia's oldest stock exchange offering 15.4M shares at INR805-806 each. BSE will be the first stock exchange to be listed in India; the National Stock Exchange of India, the country's largest by volume, has also applied to market regulators to go public. Officials last year amended its rules for stock listings in an effort to make it easier for domestic exchanges to go public. (debiprasad.nayak@wsj.com)

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/wells-fargo-reports-decrease-in-earnings-1484312882", Wells Fargo had been one of the most consistent big banks at growing earnings and revenue, but that momentum has fallen off recently. The bank continues to face reputational damage from its sales practices scandal and was dealt another black eye when regulators last month said it failed its so-called living will regulatory test of how it could unwind in a crisis scenario.

The unit responsible for the questionable sales tactics is part of Wells Fargo's community banking division, which reported profit of $2.73 billion, a 15% decrease from the $3.3 billion it earned in the fourth quarter of 2015. Among other factors, the bank cited the hedge ineffectiveness and higher deposit service charges.

Interventions in Markets with Adverse Selection: Implications for Discount Window Stigma

Does taking part in a government program carry a stigma? In a "https://www.richmondfed.org/publications/research/working_papers/2017/wp_17-01", Huberto M. Ennis looks at whether borrowing at the central bank's discount window stigmatizes banks. Discount window stigma, or the reluctance of banks to borrow from the central bank for fear of being regarded as in weak financial conditions, could be considered a sign of weakness, the paper finds, but there is no clear sense in which stigma reduces the efficacy of the discount window.

Improving Financial Reporting Standards

An efficient and stable financial system requires improvements in the quality, scope and accessibility of financial data, "https://www.financialresearch.gov/from-the-director/2017/01/12/promoting-higher-quality-and-lower-cost-in-financial-regulatory-reporting/" The fragmented U.S. regulatory system makes for inefficient data reporting that "can be costly and undermines quality," he writes. The lack of shared data reporting standards among regulators makes for an inefficient system that could be improved by using an identification standard namely the legal entity identifier, a unique ID associated with a single corporate entity.

Toyota marks break from past with fund for tech investments

Kana Inagaki in Tokyo 
521 words

Toyota has invested in 15 technology start-ups worldwide through a $310m "https://www.sparxgroup.com/press/uploads/PressE151104-01.pdf" it launched 14 months ago, marking a break from its typically conservative investment stance in an attempt to ride the new wave of connected vehicles and robotics.

The uncharacteristic focus on tech companies   ranging from car sharing and flat-panel satellite antennas to home robots   reflects a sense of urgency felt by Akio Toyoda, the chief executive of the Japanese company, to keep pace with innovation in autonomous driving and incursions by tech rivals such as Google, according to people close to the company.

Mr Toyoda launchedthe fund in late 2015 with Shuhei Abe, the chief executive of Tokyo-based hedge fund Sparx who is also Mr Toyoda s former MBA classmate at Babson College in the US, and Sumitomo Mitsui Banking Corp.

The 35.1bn ($307m) fund has19 investors, and has poured an average $5m to $10m into companies including San Francisco-based car sharing provider Getaround; UIEvolution, a US company that makes software for connected vehicles; and Groove X, a Japanese start-up launched by the developer of SoftBank s companion robot Pepper.It invests primarily in AI, robotics and hydrogen-related technologies, and last week also announced an investment in FiNC, a Japanese digital health start-up.

Sparx hopes to close the fund soon, once it has raised 50bn, and expand its focus region to include Europe and Israel.

Though dwarfed by the $100bn tech fund SoftBank recently set up, it is part of a broader effort by Mr Toyoda to speed up decision-making at the Japanese car company. It works closely with Toyota s "https://www.ft.com/artificial-intelligence-robotics" and robotics research lab in Silicon Valley headed by Gill Pratt, a robotics expert poached from the US defence department. Toyota has promised a $1bn investment in the new research division.

 The first priority for our investment is to find a company that has a technological edge. The key word is the new services that will emerge when everything becomes connected in the age of  internet of things ,  said Takaki Demichi, executive officer of Sparx Asset Management who oversees the fund with Toyota.

The fund has an investment span of up to 10 years with a target internal rate of return of 15 per cent, according to Mr Demichi.

In the past, Toyota has invested in a range of start-ups on its own including a $50m investment in US electric vehicle maker Tesla, but analysts say the approach has not always borne fruit.

The target areas of Toyota s fund are very crowded and the Japanese carmaker is not alone in recognisingthe potential of those new technologies.

A string of carmakers including General Motors and Volkswagen have also turned to car services, such as on-demand journey-booking or car-sharing schemes, in response to the risk of falling vehicle ownership among young people. Honda is in talks for an alliance with a self-driving car unit that was split out of Google, which is also working with Fiat Chrysler.

Creighton's Take: What Do Trump Nominees' Goldman Ties Actually Mean?

House Votes to Require More Cost Analysis of SEC Rules

Paul Atkins, Trump's Regulatory Adviser, Likely to Remain at Consulting Firm

What Do Trump Nominees' Goldman Ties Actually Mean?

Goldman Sachs Group Inc . has attracted more publicity than it probably cares for in the wake of Donald Trump's election victory. The president-elect's choice of current and former Goldman bankers for key nominations has resurrected the crisis-era pejorative "Government Sachs."

Steve Bannon and Steven Mnuchin, who previously worked for the investment bank, have been tapped to be "http://www.wsj.com/articles/bannon-set-to-reshape-white-house-role-1479089587" and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/donald-trump-to-pick-ex-banker-steven-mnuchin-for-treasury-nominee-1480469049", respectively. Gary Cohn is leaving his post as Goldman's No. 2 to become "http://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-names-goldman-president-gary-cohn-as-director-of-national-economic-council-1481573082", and lawyer Jay Clayton, who was a senior adviser to the bank, has been nominated to become "http://www.wsj.com/articles/president-elect-trump-to-nominate-jay-clayton-securities-and-exchange-commission-chairman-1483545999".

But it's too early, and simplistic, to assume a Trump government will reflect behavior set by "http://blogs.wsj.com/washwire/2013/11/16/u-s-treasury-secretaries-often-find-new-life-on-wall-street/"Robert Rubin and Henry Paulson, who as Treasury secretaries were instrumental in deregulating Wall Street and bailing it out, respectively.

For a start, Trump appointees with Goldman ties might be more not less sensitive to any perception they are changing rules to favor Wall Street or Goldman in particular.

More importantly, though, a Goldman pedigree doesn't necessarily correlate with a pro-Wall Street worldview. Neel Kashkari, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis, is a Goldman alumnus. He has made "http://www.wsj.com/articles/feds-neel-kashkari-rolls-out-blueprint-for-ending-too-big-to-fail-banks-1479300301" the hallmark of his tenure, last year proposing eye-popping "http://www.wsj.com/articles/transcript-feds-neel-kashkari-on-breaking-up-banks-and-monetary-policy-1455747231" that would weigh heavily on Wall Street's leverage-dependent business model.

Gary Gensler, who became a Goldman partner at 30, helped dismantle the Glass-Steagall rules separating commercial and investment banking as a senior Treasury official in the late 1990s. But in the wake of the financial crisis, he became an "http://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052748703766704576009312321035744"as chairman of the Commodity Futures and Trading Commission from 2009 to 2014.

Mr. Bannon "http://fortune.com/2016/08/17/donald-trump-bannon-mnuchin-goldman/" in the 1980s, when it was a very different institution. It was much less dependent on debt, and a partnership rather than a publicly traded limited liability company, meaning top bankers had personal liability for their decisions. In fact, in 2014 he said, "We've never really gone and dug down and sorted through the problems of 2008," calling for "a real clean-up of the banks' balance sheets" including much higher levels of capital and less trading of securities anathema to Goldman's interests.

Mr. Mnuchin left Goldman in 2002, a few years after it went public in 1999 and long before the precrisis "Big Short" excesses emerged.

To be sure, Mr. Mnuchin has said he wants to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-treasury-choice-steven-mnuchin-vows-to-strip-back-dodd-frank-1480513188" parts of the landmark 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul law, saying "it's way too complicated and cuts back lending." But his views on other financial-regulation issues of more concern to larger institutions, such as capital standards, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/schlesingers-take-trumps-treasury-picka-goldman-guy-with-no-clear-agenda-1480509119".

Mr. Trump's high-profile choices have amassed significant personal wealth already, and would seem to have far less personal incentive to help their former Goldman colleagues of decades past than they might have been at the start of their careers. Work histories offer clues, not a guide, on how Trump's government will treat big finance.
