Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

WSJ Pro: Key House Republican Wants Consumer Bureau Shut Down

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTON A key Republican on the House Financial Services Committee said he wants to see the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau closed and its responsibility given back to other regulators, a move that would return to a regulatory framework for consumer protection before the financial crisis.

Rep. Blaine Luetkemeyer (R., Mo.), who was appointed in January as head of the Financial Services panel that oversees financial regulators, said eliminating the CFPB is among options Republicans are considering as they examine ways to change the bureau created by the Obama administration.

"This was a duplicative agency that didn't need to be there," he said in an interview. "It's been a total failure, and in my mind it needs to go away."

A businessman who has worked as a state bank examiner and a community banker, Mr. Luetkemeyer is the new chairman of the Financial Institutions and Consumer Credit Subcommittee, which has oversight of the CFPB.

A CFPB spokesman declined to comment.

The CFPB has emerged as a fresh battleground between the two parties since Republicans swept into power in the November elections. Republicans want to significantly weaken the bureau as part of a campaign to dismantle the Obama-era financial regulations.

The view expressed by Mr. Luetkemeyer is more radical than other ideas discussed to weaken the CFPB. The bureau's opponents are pushing the incoming administration to remove its director, Richard Cordray, before his term ends in mid-2018 and replace him with an official who shares their ideas.

House Republicans also plan to soon reintroduce broad financial deregulation known as the Financial Choice Act bill that includes a significant revamp of the CFPB. Possible changes include turning its single-director leadership into a commission; subjecting its budget to the purview of Congress; narrowing its rule-making and enforcement authority; and revamping its employee pay structure to make it harder to recruit professionals.

Democrats oppose GOP moves to overhaul the CFPB, including Sen. Elizabeth Warren, who helped establish the CFPB.

"We're going to fight back against any efforts to gut financial reform and to allow big banks and shady financial institutions to once again cheat consumers and put our economy at risk," the Massachusetts senator said during a conference call hosted by consumer groups Tuesday.

Mr. Luetkemeyer said if the CFPB is shut down, the responsibility of consumer protection could be shared among existing agencies such as the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp ., the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency and the Federal Reserve.

"I think we have a lot of regulatory folks already in place," he said, adding they were "doing a good job of it."

The CFPB's 1,600 employees "probably will have to go find another job" if the bureau is closed, he said. "They may go back to some of the other regulatory agencies that may have to ramp up their oversight with regards to consumer protection."

House Votes to Require More Cost Analysis of SEC Rules

By Andrew Ackerman

WASHINGTON House lawmakers voted largely along party lines Thursday to approve a bill to require securities regulators to more extensively assess how much their rules cost the financial industry, part of a push to curtail what Republicans say is overly burdensome red tape.

The House voted 243 to 184 to pass the measure, with the support of a handful of Democrats. It would require the Securities and Exchange Commission to analyze all of its existing rules and to conduct "look backs" on a regular basis to ensure new and existing rules are still effective. Critics, including top SEC officials, say the measure is intended to gum up the regulatory process and would further hamstring a commission with limited resources.

Passage of the bill is significant because it is among the first of several deregulatory measures congressional Republicans are expected to advance this year. It faces uncertain prospects in the Senate, where no companion bill is pending and Democrats have more power to block the legislation, but comes at a time when Republicans are about to control Congress and the executive branch.

The House separately voted 239-182 to require the Commodity Futures Trading Commission to conduct more rigorous analysis on the costs and benefits of its rules, as part of legislation setting the contours of the CFTC's regulatory powers for the next five years.

And the House on Wednesday passed related legislation that would require a broader array of federal agencies to adopt the lowest-cost version of a proposed regulation and to limit judges' deference to regulators on legal challenges to regulations.

Under Thursday's SEC bill, sponsored by Rep. Ann Wagner (R., Mo.), the top U.S. markets regulator would need to more carefully examine a problem to see if there is a need for a rule, with analysis conducted by the agency's chief economist. One year after implementation, the commission would have to perform another analysis to see if the rule met its intended goals. It would have to repeat the process again every five years.

SEC Chairman Mary Jo White, through a spokeswoman, declined to comment. In 2013, she told lawmakers that she was "troubled" by a nearly identical version of the bill, sponsored at the time by Rep. Scott Garrett (R., N.J.), saying the agency already takes steps to ensure "robust" analysis of the costs and benefits of its rules. Ms. White said the steps have already won positive feedback from a congressional watchdog and the U.S. Chamber of Commerce .

Ms. Wagner succeeded Mr. Garrett as the bill's sponsor after the New Jersey Republican lost his reelection bid last year.

The SEC is required by law to assess the impact its rules have on competition, efficiency and capital formation. It faced a series of legal challenges to its rules in past years, with plaintiffs arguing successfully that regulations weren't adopted with sufficient economic analysis.

To address those concerns, the SEC in 2012 boosted internal guidelines, which call for the commission's economists to play an early role in drafting new rules. The guidance applies to rules mandated by Congress, such as those in the 2010 Dodd-Frank law, and those done on a discretionary basis, such as a set of restrictions for the asset-management industry. Thursday's bill would effectively replace such guidance if signed into law.

Before Thursday's vote, the Securities Industry and Financial Markets Association , a top Wall Street trade group, said it backed the bill for strengthening the rigor of the SEC's rule-making process. Andy Blocker, the industry group's top lobbyist, said in a letter to congressional leadership the bill would "ensure the continued efficacy of regulation."

The Consumer Federation of America , which opposed the bill, said it "goes far beyond what is reasonable or balanced" and would cause a "serious drain" on the SEC's resources.

"It would tie the SEC in procedural knots, keep its focus on an endless review of existing rules rather than emerging issues, [and] provide endless grounds for legal challenge," said Barbara Roper, CFA's director of investor protection, in a written statement.

Key Reforms From Dodd-Frank Shouldn't Be Rolled Back, Yellen Says

By Kate Davidson

WASHINGTON Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen said Thursday that key regulatory changes implemented by the 2010 Dodd-Frank law including higher capital standards and enhanced supervision for big banks and new tools to help regulators deal with potential bank failures should not be rolled back.

Speaking at a Fed town hall discussion with teachers, Ms. Yellen said, "Dodd-Frank was a very important road map for strengthening the financial system and mitigating the chance of another financial crisis."

While Ms. Yellen said there is room for changes to the law that would help reduce the regulatory burden on small banks, she said Dodd-Frank established many key reforms that made the system "substantially safer and sounder," including new liquidity requirements, derivatives reforms, stress tests, resolution plans and the establishment of the Financial Stability Oversight Council .

Congressional Republicans and President-elect Donald Trump have vowed to dismantle the bulk of the Dodd-Frank law, which they argue will remove regulatory uncertainty for businesses and help promote economic growth.

Ms. Yellen also told teachers she worries "a great deal" about income inequality and its long-term effects on the U.S. economy. She noted that a larger share of income gains in recent years have gone to workers at the top of the income distribution, and that the gap between workers without a high school diploma and those with advanced degrees has grown wider.

And she lamented the underrepresentation of women in the economics profession, as well as among economics majors and in Ph.D. programs, a problem she said begins in college or even earlier.

The disparity may have to do with a lack of role models for young women in economics, as well as an implicit bias, Ms. Yellen said, citing research that shows women economists receive significantly less credit than their male counterparts for coauthoring academic research papers, especially when their co-author is a man.

"It's something that I do hope will change over time and it may even make a difference in the way that policy is conducted," she said, adding that the Fed is working to improve diversity in its ranks.

In remarks prepared for delivery, Ms. Yellen said that improving education is at the top of her list among initiatives she believes could help spur economic growth and raise living standards. She credited educators with helping students build practical life skills, including the ability to make wise financial decisions, that benefit not just the students but all of society.

"Everyone is engaged in and depends on the economy, and nothing is more critical to a healthy and growing economy than the capability, creativity and productiveness of its workforce," she said.

Ms. Yellen also said all teachers, but especially economics teachers, effectively help further the Fed's mission by educating students and supporting their contributions to the economy.

She didn't comment on monetary policy, interest rates or the economic outlook in her prepared remarks.

Ms. Yellen took questions from teachers Thursday evening at the Fed's main building in Washington and via a webcast on its website.

Former Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke held several similar meetings with teachers during his tenure where he spoke about his own background as a college professor and the importance of financial education.

Ms. Yellen has spent much of her career in academia. She is a professor emeritus at the University of California at Berkeley and has been a faculty member since 1980, with various stints in government in between. Before that, she was a faculty member at the London School of Economics and Political Science , and an assistant professor at Harvard University.

Paul Atkins, Trump's Regulatory Adviser, Likely to Remain at Consulting Firm

By Andrew Ackerman and Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON Donald Trump's point man on financial regulation probably won't be joining the incoming administration, at least not initially.

Paul Atkins, widely viewed as a top candidate for a powerful regulatory perch at the Federal Reserve, has told people he intends to stay at his consulting firm, Patomak Global Partners, according to people familiar with the matter.

A former member of the Securities and Exchange Commission , Mr. Atkins has had a key role on Trump's transition team, helping to recommend regulatory policy and personnel at federal financial regulators.

He was seen as a leading candidate for a top post in the president-elect's administration, including the Federal Reserve's point man on banking oversight.

That post is now unlikely to go to Mr. Atkins, these people said, though the situation could change and the Trump administration may tap Mr. Atkins for a post at some point in the future.

The Trump transition team couldn't immediately be reached for comment.

Mr. Atkins has told people for months that he was happy at Patomak and wasn't actively campaigning for a top job, such as the vice chair of supervision at the Fed.

That post was created by Congress in 2010, but it has never been filled. Fed governor Daniel Tarullo, who has been effectively doing the job, has used the role to exert significant influence over the largest U.S. banks. His term as fed governor expires in 2022, though he could step down sooner.

Any nominee to the vice chairman role would need to be confirmed by the Senate.

Other possible candidates for the Fed job, The Wall Street Journal has reported, include: Rep. French Hill (R., Ark.), a former banker; David Nason, chief executive of General Electric Co.'s GE Energy Financial Services and a Treasury Department official in the George W. Bush administration; and John Allison, the former leader of the libertarian Cato Institute think tank and former CEO of regional bank BB&T Corp.

Mr. Trump's team also is considering whether to appoint two Fed officials at once, people familiar with the matter have said. The vice chair job is open, as is another slot on the Fed's seven-member governing board, which currently has only five members.

Mr. Atkins founded his behind-the-scenes consulting business Patomak in the garage of his suburban Washington home in 2009 after he stepped down as a Republican member of the SEC.

Since then, he has prepared private-equity firms for compliance exams, devised strategies for mutual funds to resist government curbs on risk-taking and helped Chinese accounting firms avert a ban on doing business in the U.S. Patomak's other clients have included the U.S. Chamber of Commerce and Fidelity Investments .

Financial Stability Board Releases Guidelines on Asset-Management Industry

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine

In a bid to avoid a future market meltdown, the Financial Stability Board released final policy guidelines Thursday to regulate the growing asset-management industry.

The global standards setter which is based in Basel, Switzerland, and headed by Bank of England Gov. Mark Carney laid out recommendations for regulators to implement over the next several years.

The FSB identified several points of weakness that could wreak havoc on financial stability. At the top of its list is liquidity, the ease with which market participants can buy and sell securities without undue price movements, which could spark a crisis if asset managers seek to exit trades simultaneously. Other concerns the FSB cited were use of leverage and operational risks such as system failures.

The final guidelines include minor changes to the FSB's original proposal from June 2016.

Asset managers expressed some worry about the guidelines. Paul Schott Stevens, president and chief executive of the Investment Company Institute, which represents funds that manage total assets of $18.4 trillion in the U.S., said the report is based on flawed assumptions about liquidity risk management. He also criticized the FSB's plan to consider designating big asset managers as systemically important, which would subject them to stricter rules.

"The FSB has made some helpful changes to its recommendations," said Mr. Stevens. "Nevertheless, we remain troubled."

The FSB's efforts come in response to the increasingly important role of asset managers, which are now responsible for managing 40% of global financial assets, or $76.7 trillion as of 2015. That was up from $53.6 trillion in 2005, according to the FSB paper.

"The growth in asset management activities provides new sources of credit and investment, and adds diversity to our financial system," said Mr. Carney, adding the new guidelines "will enhance the resilience of asset management activities so that this form of market-based finance can help underpin strong, sustainable and balanced economic growth."

The global regulator expressed most concern around liquidity, considered a proxy for market stress.

"As investors have reached for yield in an environment of exceptionally low interest rates, they have sought out asset managers and funds that offer exposures to higher yielding, less actively traded asset classes," the FSB said.

While the FSB said it recognizes that funds and asset managers pose less of a risk than investment banks, as they don't use their balance sheets to finance market transactions, it highlighted the impact the collapse Long-Term Capital Management , a hedge fund, had on markets in 1998.

The International Organization of Securities Commissions , the world's biggest committee of markets regulator, is in charge of developing the relevant solutions and reviewing its existing guidance. The FSB set out 14 recommendations for IOSCO. For instance, to address the systemic risk of leverage, the FSB recommends the market regulator create common measures with which to monitor and assess leverage across funds globally.

The FSB recommendations "are an important step toward addressing potential financial stability issues in the asset management sector," said Ashley Alder, head of the IOSCO board and a member of the FSB steering committee. "IOSCO will take forward the recommendations on liquidity mismatch and leverage so as to enhance the overall soundness of this important financial sector."

ITG Agrees to Pay $24 Million in SEC Settlement

By Dave Michaels

WASHINGTON Brokerage firm Investment Technology Group Inc . agreed to pay $24.4 million to settle claims that it improperly obtained and lent foreign shares to clients, the first penalty in a wide-ranging probe of misconduct in the market.

ITG is a broker that provides execution services and trade analytics to institutional investors. It provided American depositary receipts, which are certificates representing ownership of a foreign stock, to clients without ensuring they would be backed by the actual shares, the Securities and Exchange Commission said. The SEC said that resulted in the issuance of phantom shares, or ADRs that weren't tied to securities issued by a foreign company.

ITG, which didn't admit or deny the claims, agreed to pay $9.3 million in penalties and interest and forfeit $15 million in profits, the commission added.

"ITG's failure to properly supervise its securities lending desk caused ADRs to be issued that were not backed by actual shares, leaving them ripe for potential market abuse,'' said Andrew M. Calamari, director of the SEC's New York regional office.

ITG declined to comment. Frank Troise, its chief executive, said in an earnings call last year that the office accused of wrongdoing was shut down in late 2014.

"We take these matters very seriously, and we recognize that there is heightened sensitivity among some of our clients with respect to regulatory matters," Mr. Troise said in November.

The probe of ITG, which covered activity from 2011 to 2014, was related to the prerelease of ADRs, where banks issue depositary receipts without first having the underlying shares in their custody. The practice was intended to smooth trading by bridging different countries' settlement times.

Created by J.P. Morgan in 1927, ADRs make it easier for American investors to own stocks that are listed outside the U.S. They allow, for instance, U.S. shareholders to avoid receiving dividend payments in foreign currency; each ADR represents a specific number of foreign shares. Brokers who sell or transfer ADRs are typically responsible for ensuring that a matching number of foreign shares has been deposited with a custodian.

ITG's customers included other brokers who used the ADRs to close out short sales, the SEC's order said. Regulators have worried that prereleased depositary receipts could be abused for "naked short selling," according to people familiar with the matter. The activity is an illegal practice where traders bet against a company's stock by selling shares they don't own, without borrowing or locating the shares needed to cover the sale. The agency is also looking at whether such receipts are being used to illegally arbitrage between different tax regimes.

The SEC's press release said its investigation isn't over but didn't elaborate. The Wall Street Journal reported in November that the SEC had sent subpoenas to four depositary banks  Bank of New York Mellon Corp ., Citigroup Inc ., Deutsche Bank AG and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . as it examined whether the banks have broken controls designed to prevent market abuse and tax fraud, people close to the investigation said.

Two years ago, ITG agreed to pay $20.3 million to settle SEC allegations that it ran a secret trading project that relied in part on confidential information from its clients and improper trading in its dark pool, a private trading venue that doesn't disclose investors' orders.

Officials Planned to Prioritize Debt Payments as 2011 Debt-Ceiling Deadline Loomed

By Kate Davidson

Federal Reserve and Treasury Department officials in August 2011 had privately formalized a plan to make on-time payments on Treasury debt and delay paying other government bills if Congress and the White House failed to reach an agreement to raise the federal borrowing limit, according to transcripts of a central bank meeting released Thursday.

The transcripts provide the most detailed description yet from any government agency of what officials were prepared to do in the event of a debt-limit breach.

At the time, Obama administration officials were warning the Treasury would run out of cash to pay all the government's bills if Congress failed to lift the debt ceiling.

Republicans were pressing the Treasury to prioritize payments to bondholders to avoid a default on U.S. government securities, a linchpin of global markets. But Treasury officials said prioritizing principal and interest payments while missing payments on other obligations such as Social Security checks, veteran benefits and other vendor bills was "simply default by another name," and thus an "unacceptable outcome."

The issue was of great concern then to investors around the world, and could be again this year as Congress could face another debt ceiling deadline in the coming months. The federal borrowing limit was suspended in 2015 but will return mid-March, after which the Treasury will have to deploy emergency measures to avoid breaching the limit, measures that can probably last for a few months.

The prospect that the U.S. government might fail to make bond payments had roiled financial markets when Fed officials held an emergency conference call on Aug. 1, 2011, just after congressional leaders announced a deal to raise the debt limit.

Transcripts from the call show the Fed acting as the Treasury's fiscal agent and at its direction was prepared to keep making principal and interest payments on time, and delay other payments as necessary, if the deal fell through and the debt limit wasn't raised in time.

Louise Roseman, the director of the Fed's division of reserve bank operations and payment systems at the time, told Fed officials that the central bank, its regional reserve banks and the Treasury had jointly developed and codified the procedures into a "special operating circular," which it planned to issue to banks if Congress failed to strike a timely agreement. The plan was "approved by the Treasury as reflecting what it would like the Reserve Banks to do as its fiscal agents if it ever came to that," she said.

The Fed released the transcripts from its 2011 meetings on Thursday under its customary five-year lag.

Ms. Roseman said the Treasury planned to ensure it would be able to make the interest payments "by holding back other government payments and accumulating sufficient cash balances in its Fed account to pay upcoming coupon payments."

Janet Yellen, the Fed's vice chairman at the time, said she came away from the debt ceiling episode "quite concerned" about the vulnerability of market infrastructure in the event of an actual default, which she said could have had "catastrophic market consequences."

"Given that we could face a similar situation somewhere down the road, I think it's important for us to think about lessons learned so that we and markets will be better prepared if we face such a situation again," Ms. Yellen said, according to the transcript.

Fed officials also discussed ways they might respond in the event of a default or renewed market turbulence. Those options included valuing defaulted securities at their own market prices, conducting reverse repurchase operations to address strains in money markets, providing emergency liquidity to money market mutual funds and buying defaulted Treasury securities outright.

Some officials worried that emergency lending to money markets could generate moral hazard, and that buying defaulted Treasurys could be seen as financing the public debt. Ms. Yellen said those options "seem to be possible to me" if such a situation were to arise in the future and default seemed to be a possibility, "but whether or not I would endorse them would depend on the actual circumstances were this to arise."

"The implication of this approach would be that the Treasury would be delaying non-[principal and interest] payments even on days when it may have ample balances in its Fed account to have been able to make those payments if it had so chosen," she said. "Instead, the Treasury would be conserving that cash to be able to ensure that it would be able to pay future-dated interest payments."

Ms. Roseman said the Treasury wouldn't submit any payments to the Fed's regional reserve banks unless it was sure it would have sufficient balances to settle the transactions on the settlement dates.

The release of the transcripts could complicate efforts to negotiate the next debt-limit increase ahead of the return of the ceiling in March. Congressional Republicans had repeatedly called on the Treasury to prioritize payments ahead of the 2011 deadline, as well as other categories of federal spending.

In the years since the showdown, administration officials have acknowledged that they conferred with the New York Fed and determined that prioritizing such payments was technologically possible. But they warned such a move "would be entirely experimental and create unacceptable risk to both domestic and global financial markets."

Treasury officials told government watchdogs that they determined the "least harmful option" available in 2011 was to delay all payments until they could all be made on a day-by-day basis.

According to the inspector general's report, the Treasury said that, because Congress has never provided guidance on how to prioritize payments, its systems are designed to make each payment in the order it comes due.

"Treasury officials determined that there is no fair or sensible way to pick and choose among the many bills that come due every day," the report said.

The Fed transcripts released Thursday suggest that, at the end of the day, officials were prepared to prioritize principal and interest payments on bonds, though Ms. Roseman acknowledged the plans were fluid and had only been finalized the prior Friday, July 29.

"That would be a comfort if we find ourselves in another debt ceiling event, although we view that as less likely this time around," said Ian Lyngen, head of U.S. rates strategy at Bank of Montreal , said after the transcripts were released Thursday.

Contingency planning for such a default "had been under way for months," she said, but the plan had changed substantially over the previous week or two, in part because "it was only recently that senior policymakers, here at the board and at the Treasury and elsewhere, paid very serious attention to what the procedures would be, and questioned, perhaps, whether that was the appropriate approach."

She said the plan she was outlining would "provide a higher degree of certainty" to payment recipients.

Then-Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke asked whether the Fed would have been able to prioritize payments for other major categories of federal spending, in addition to payments on Treasury debt, as envisioned under a bill that had been proposed by Sen. Pat Toomey (R., Pa.).

Ms. Roseman said the challenge "would be more on the Treasury side," adding that the department had concerns about its ability to send certain types of payments to the Fed while withholding others.

"But frankly, the Treasury had concerns about some of the approaches we've ultimately adopted &in the past week or so that it has now decided it would be able to do after all," she said. "So I suspect that it would, with sufficient lead time at least in weeks, not days be able to accommodate that."

"But it's something that, until you have developed the procedures and tested the procedures, your comfort level is pretty low," she added.

Ms. Roseman also said the Fed system would have been capable of handling delayed coupon payments or delayed redemption of matured securities if the Treasury changed its mind again and decided not to prioritize payments. But she said it could create "very big operational challenges and probably a lot of disruption" for other market participants, who never anticipated the possibility of the government missing a debt payment.

 Katy Burne contributed to this article.

Corrections & Amplifications:

The federal debt ceiling will be reinstated in March after being suspended in 2015. An earlier version of this article incorrectly stated there is a debt ceiling deadline March 15. (Jan. 12, 2017)

Leslie Caldwell, Head of Justice Department's Criminal Division, Stepping Down

By Aruna Viswanantha

The head of the Justice Department's criminal division will leave her post on Friday, leaving behind efforts to simplify and speed up white-collar investigations that helped pave the way for the indictment of Volkswagen executives and a multi-billion dollar fine against the company this week.

Leslie Caldwell, who has led the unit since 2014, also oversaw the seizure last year of more than $1 billion in assets tied to alleged corruption at 1Malaysia Development Bhd ., or 1MDB, and landmark foreign corruption cases against J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., and Brazilian construction giant Odebrecht SA .

"The key is to keep things relatively simple, you don't need to include every fact," Ms. Caldwell said in an interview, describing her approach to prosecuting cases. "The temptation in white-collar cases is to investigate everything."

Ms. Caldwell said she doesn't yet know what she will do next, other than a trip planned to the Caribbean next week.

A U.S. grand jury on Wednesday indicted six current and former executives of Volkswagen AG for their alleged part in the company's U.S. emissions fraud, as the company agreed to plead guilty to engaging in the fraud and pay $4.3 billion in criminal and civil penalties.

The case focused on several internal Volkswagen meetings and several meetings with regulators the officials allegedly participated in. One of the executives was arrested earlier this week when vacationing in Miami. The others are believed to be in Germany.

The case serves as something of a capstone to a what has been a thorny issue for the Justice Department during the Obama administration   the investigations of major corporations.

The government has extracted record multibillion-dollar fines from banks and other companies for alleged misconduct, but Democrats have charged the Obama administration has failed to hold executives criminally accountable for conduct leading up to the financial meltdown of 2008, and failed to pursue individuals in other corporate cases.

Ms. Caldwell apologized last month after appearing to blame colleagues for problems in some white-collar prosecutions.

Earlier this week a grand jury also returned an indictment of three former currency traders alleging they colluded to move foreign exchange rates, in violation of antitrust laws. Their employers paid billions of dollars to resolve related criminal cases in 2015.

Ms. Caldwell took over the unit in 2014 as it raised its profile as a prosecuting office by handling large, international financial investigations that historically might have been the purview of federal prosecutors in New York, including into the manipulation of Libor and other financial benchmarks.

The division has added dozens to its ranks in the past few years, including increasing its foreign corruption group by 50% with an effort to focus on "bigger" cases and provide companies more information about how they can avoid prosecution if they report potential misconduct and cooperate in any investigation.

Ms. Caldwell said she devoted more people and resources to investigating cyber crimes, and said she expected her successor would need to tackle the growing inability for investigators to access information stored on encrypted devices.

"That will be problem No. 1 to address," she said.

SEC Backs  Virtual-Only' Annual Meeting Option

By Tatyana Shumsky

The growing popularity of virtual-only shareholder meetings isn't sitting well with shareholders.

In the latest round of the debate, the Securities and Exchange Commission backed the right of companies to decide on the format of annual meetings.

The regulator in December told HP Inc . it could drop an investor proposal objecting to virtual-only meetings. The proposal called for a shareholder vote on restoring in-person annual meetings.

A virtual meeting can done over the phone, online or by using a combination of both. Shareholders log into a special website using a code or pin number to cast their votes and ask executives and board members questions.

In granting HP's no action letter request, the SEC reaffirmed its earlier position, that company boards of directors can decide whether the annual meetings should be virtual-only or in-person.

The SEC declined to comment beyond the staff's response in the letter.

HP Inc . declined to comment. However the law firm representing HP, Gibson, Dunn & Crutcher LLP , said it's likely more companies will follow HP's lead.

"Given the potential cost savings and flexibility that can be achieved from holding virtual-only annual meetings, we expect that more companies will choose to hold virtual-only annual meetings in the near future," Lori Zyskowski, Elizabeth Ising and Ronald Mueller from Gibson Dunn said in a blog post. Ms. Zyskowski represented HP in the matter.

Companies can also save money on swag. While some companies, such as Berkshire Hathaway Inc ., are known for lavishing gifts on shareholders at the annual meeting, many companies have been cutting back on these expenses in recent years. An attendee of Alphabet Inc .'s annual meeting in June complained that the company wasn't giving out bags, caps or mugs, The Wall Street Journal reported.

Last year, 187 companies used the services of Broadridge Financial Solutions Inc ., the main provider of virtual shareholder meeting technology, to hold virtual shareholder meetings. This is up from 28 companies in 2010.

Virtual shareholder meetings have grown in popularity despite opposition from groups including the Council of Institutional Investors (CII) and the California Public Employees' Retirement System , as well as individual investors. Critics say that companies could use the technology to limit shareholder participation, allowing executives to cherry pick shareholder questions and avoid addressing critical issues.

The HP proposal was lodged by John Chevedden, an activist investor who earned his corporate gadfly bones by filing hundreds of shareholder proposals aimed at strengthening shareholder rights, corporate governance and transparency.

"A cloud meeting gives a company wide latitude to muzzle shareholders during the mandatory annual meeting," Mr. Chevedden said in a letter to the SEC regarding his HP proposal.

"As shareholders we believe it is important for companies to have in person stockholder meetings where investors can ask questions and speak before management and the Board," Mr. Chevedden said in an email to CFO Journal. "Our request for annual in person meetings should be seen as basic good governance and not a minor administrative detail."

You Know What Banks Need? Slot Machines, Miniskirts and Chocolate

By Eleanor Warnock

OGAKI, Japan Entrepreneur Takashi Tsuchiya is eager to talk of new ventures he has launched under his "OKB" brand. There's the OKB blueberry jam, the OKB chocolates, an OKB art gallery.

He makes loans, too.

Those are OKB loans, short for the institution he heads, the Ogaki Kyoritsu Bank . But the other OKB stuff is as exciting as banking, the 70-year-old Mr. Tsuchiya says, what with Japan's superlow interest rates forcing ever-slimmer margins on loans and turning his day job into drudgery.

"With these interest rates," he says, "you might make more money selling 10 OKB chocolates instead of managing a million yen in deposits" (about US$8,600).

It's tough being a small-bank operator in Japan. Customers are rapidly aging. Companies are stockpiling cash rather than borrowing. Big Japanese banks are seeking growth in the U.S. and elsewhere, but that's far beyond the means of smaller banks such as Mr. Tsuchiya's.

Some other smaller lenders have branched out, too, including one that offers a special interest rate to elderly people who can certify they recently gave up their driver's licenses. Another calls itself Tomato Bank.

Dozens of regional banks sponsor an annual food convention in Tokyo to proffer local delicacies such as lemon-flavored squid chips. The prime minister's famously outspoken wife, Akie Abe, showed up at the most recent event in November. Banks "aren't just for lending," Ms. Abe said. "We're in an era when the role of a bank has changed a little."

It is an economic era that has inspired other Japanese industries to branch out, too, including electronics companies that have moved into growing vegetables. Fujitsu Ltd . grows lettuce in a former semiconductor plant.

To go beyond lending, Mr. Tsuchiya has plastered his OKB brand around this town of about 160,000 people near Nagoya. That includes OKB rice, an OKB farm and, just in time for the new year, the new OKB chocolates at $25 for a box of 10.

He does have to stay on the right side of Japanese laws that say banks can't run other businesses. The store selling the chocolates, which opened in late December, is run by a partner of his bank.

The OKB farm, which grows the rice, is managed by an independent company that OKB paid to put a giant yellow "OKB Farm" sign out front. Chief Farmer Hiromasa Kasai works for the bank's think tank as well as the farm company and consults with Mr. Tsuchiya on new projects.

Mr. Kasai says the farm will likely turn a profit in the fiscal year that ends in March, partly because the think tank pays his salary.

"The Financial Services Agency wants banks to create a new business model," says Mr. Tsuchiya, "so we're using this brand strategy because we don't want to be like other banks."

A spokesman for the agency, Japan's banking regulator, declined to comment.

Mr. Tsuchiya, who giggles as he shows off gadgets such as a secret remote control to call his secretary, does direct some of his innovative instinct into banking. He claims to be the only bank president in Japan who has penned the lyrics to a song available at karaoke boxes nationwide.

The ballad, "To the One and Only Palm in the World," viewable on YouTube , is dedicated to one of the bank's signature services: palm-scanning ATMs. OKB customers can withdraw and deposit cash with only their birth date, personal identification number and palm scan.

To make its ATMs even more fun, OKB gives customers a chance to win cash prizes in slot, roulette, dice and pinball games when they deposit or withdraw money. "We're not a bank, we're a service company," says Mr. Tsuchiya. "If you're going to go to the bank, it should be fun."

Among the bank's other innovations: Women can get loans to finance divorce proceedings, undergo infertility treatments or get plastic surgery.

His branding strategy extends to the menu of one of the town's most expensive restaurants, Yontori, or "Four Birds," featuring $170 courses of traditional kaiseki cuisine and a private room with its own lavatory so VIP customers don't run into each other. The restaurant uses OKB rice and OKB blueberries.

"If a bank can make rice," says head chef Shujiro Tsutani, "maybe it's OK for a restaurant like us to do something different as well. It provides a spark" for the local economy.

He says a challenge is avoiding OKB talk when rival-bank executives come to dine.

The store selling the OKB chocolate says it has mostly run out of stock and is trying to fill 2,300 orders. Next door is an OKB studio from which a local radio station broadcasts a show. A short walk down OKB Street stands the art gallery it recently featured paintings by Mr. Tsuchiya's late father, himself a former president of the bank whose enthusiasms also ranged beyond financial services.

To Japanese customers, the name OKB brings to mind AKB48, a Japanese group featuring more than 100 mostly teenage girls who sing sugar-sweet pop songs.

Mr. Tsuchiya says he came up with the OKB brand first, but he did make a play on the pop group's name with "OKB45" 45 bank employees dressed in miniskirts who advertise for the bank at local events and "OKB3," three professional models who appear in advertisements for financial services.

The bank has expanded its market share over the past decade and increased net profit for the last two years. Mr. Tsuchiya says attaching the OKB name to both banking and nonfinancial ventures will help contribute to the local economy and create jobs.

"It's my dream," he says, "that one day people will say to us,  OKB was originally a bank?' "

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

HUD Nominee Carson Wants More Private Mortgage Entities

When quizzed on mortgage companies Fannie Mae (FNMA) and Freddie Mac (FMCC), HUD nominee Ben Carson tells lawmakers he supports "some kind of backstop" for the home-loan market but favors introducing more private entities into the marketplace. Government-controlled FNMA and FMCC purchase loans from lenders and repackage them as securities that are insured if the loans default. Treasury nominee Steven Mnuchin said after his nomination was announced in November the administration would move quickly to restore the mortgage firms to private ownership. (jeffrey.sparshott@wsj.com; @jeffsparshott)

Former Rep Neugebauer Seen as Candidate for CFPB Job

Randy Neugebauer, a former Republican congressman, visited Donald Trump late Wednesday, fueling speculation that the president-elect may be considering him for a job in the administration. The Texas politician, who has led his party's attack on the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau until his retirement in December, is viewed as one of the top candidates to lead the agency if Trump decides to remove its current director before his term ends in 2018. In December, he discussed various steps for overhauling the CFPB with WSJ, while declining to deny or confirm his interest in its top job. Neugebauer couldn't be reached for comment. For more, see: "http://on.wsj.com/2j4Nsqa". (yuka.hayashi@wsj.com; @TokyoWoods)

Crapo Opens Banking Committee Session With Bipartisan Tone

Senate Banking Committee Chairman Mike Crapo (R., Idaho) gaveled-in his first hearing as chairman today by welcoming new members to the panel and striking a bipartisan tone. "This committee has a long and distinguished history of tackling important and complicated issues. This Congress, we will continue this tradition, and my hope is that we do so in a strong, bipartisan manner," he said in his opening statement. The financial industry is hoping he can succeed in moving significant regulatory relief through the committee with bipartisan support. (Ryan.Tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Nordic Banks Could Copy Santander's Haste in Tier 3

Banks in the Nordic region could follow the example of Banco Santander and start issuing bail-in-able bonds before required to do so by national law, says Swedbank. Santander is planning to issue bonds compliant with total loss-absorbing capacity and minimum requirement for own funds and eligible liabilities requirements, commonly referred to as TLAC and MREL, before Spain transposes relevant EU directives into national law. Non-preferred senior bonds issued by Santander before legislation amendments would include a clause signifying them as senior non-preferred in resolution and insolvency. This contractual solution could become a template for Nordic banks, Swedbank adds. (tasos.vossos@wsj.com, @tasosvos)

Jacob Schlesinger and the reporters and editors of WSJ Pro Financial Regulation welcome your questions. Send an email to AskFinReg@wsj.com.

Italian Banks: How to Get Something for Nothing

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/italian-banks-how-to-get-something-for-nothing-1484243440" right price. For some Italian banks, that price is zero. Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena found this out when it failed to raise fresh equity last month, forcing it into a state-backed rescue. But now a rival has found a way to exploit the problem: UBI Banca is buying three failed Italian lenders for a price of one euro.

The proposed deal announced Thursday will see UBI buy the three lenders, which all collapsed at the end of 2015 from Italy's resolution fund. But the takeover will only happen if the fund injects  450 million ($474 million) of fresh capital into the banks and takes  2.2 billion of bad loans out of them before the sale. Both are likely.

Dodd-Frank's Impact on Financial Stability and Economic Growth

Where has the Dodd-Frank Act been successful, and where has it failed? "http://www.rsfjournal.org/doi/full/10.7758/RSF.2017.3.1.02"by Martin Neil Bail, Aaron Klein and Justin Schardin published in the Russell Sage Foundation Journal of the Social Sciences, the "clear wins" are in areas of higher prudential standards (including for capital), the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau and single-point-of-entry resolution authority. They base their analysis on measures of "long-run sustainable growth and stability."

Barney Frank's 2012 Argument for President's Ability to Fire CFPB Chief

Comments Barney Frank made in a 2012 congressional hearing may have bearing on the possibility that Consumer Financial Protection Bureau Director Richard Cordray could be removed from his post by the president, "http://yalejreg.com/nc/is-barney-frank-right-about-the-presidents-power-to-remove-the-cfpb-director-by-aditya-bamzai-john-f-duffy/" for the Yale Journal on Regulation's blog. The former congressman's views "may be relevant since he is the  Frank' in Dodd-Frank," the 2010 regulatory-overhaul law, the authors write. "No one doubts that if a change in administration comes, and the new president disagrees with the existing director, he or she can be removed," Mr. Frank said at the time. "And proving that you were not inefficient, the burden of proof being on you, would be overwhelming."

Authers  Note: glass half-full

John Authers 
1,382 words
13 January 2017
00:24
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2017 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

There are some things on which we can all agree. "https://www.ft.com/content/e4230062-b1ba-11e6-9c37-5787335499a0?emailid=56cef8b84218200300714481&segmentId=e2b21419-1ad0-96bc-fb17-88413ea973e2", and the US post-crisis expansion has been going on for a long time (albeit very slowly). But that still, according to Goldman Sachs' private wealth management group, means that US stocks are a buy. The glass is half-full, not half-empty.

I am nervous about this logic, but must concede that the US has been the most expensive stock market for years and has continued to rise. I interviewed Sharmin Mossavar-Rahmani, chief investment officer of Goldman Sachs  private wealth management group, today on Facebook live. You can visit the video on "https://www.facebook.com/financialtimes/videos/10154864462430750/":

Here is her argument, using the graphs from the report. First, there is no sign of euphoria, even after the month of Trump-led excitement that followed the election. Instead the talk is still of decline, and Charmin even drew a parallel between a famous BusinessWeek cover from 1979 and a more recent illustration in the Financial Times:

That we are publishing such images could be viewed as a classic contrarian buying signal. Next comes the acknowledgment that US equity valuations are now in the tenth decile (meaning that they are more expensive than they have been 90 per cent of the time). This normally means that it is a terrible time to buy:

The problem is that expensive assets can always grow more expensive. And since the US market entered the ninth decile, by Goldman s metrics, it has gained another 38 per cent. Further, it is reasonable to pay a higher multiple when yields and inflation are low:

And the low bond yields of the present, combined with the low disparity between equity and bond returns of recent years, create a reasonable expectation that equities can move forward:

Not only this, but while rates are low, stocks do well until bonds reach an inflection point, apparently. (As there have not been all that many inflection points in the bond market this is a hard one to gauge, but here is Goldman's version of the effect). Rises in bond yields only cease to be good for stocks once they have passed 3.9 per cent. We are still only at 2.4 per cent:

Then there is an argument from the Trump effect. Any cut in the effective tax rate will be good for shareholders of US companies. Naturally, much devil is in the detail. Different companies pay different effective rates at present, and an attempt to level the terms of trade by imposing a destination tax would create some major losers among the bigger importers. But the logic that lower tax, all else being equal, is good for stocks, and that lower tax is very likely, is unassailable.

Finally, there are arguments from economics. Unlike other recoveries, this one has been accompanied by continuous household deleveraging. Easing lending standards, and signs that wages are beginning to grow, suggest that there is plenty of room now for credit to expand.

Many will suspect data-mining, so it is worth taking a look. There is also a broader argument to be made about the dangers of being out of the market. Long-term studies show that market-timing is very difficult. Lest anyone think that this is typical over-the-top bullishness from a notoriously bullish house, note that the outlook still characterises risks as high, particularly from China, and expects equity returns to be well below historic averages, thanks to multiple compression. "http://www.goldmansachs.com/what-we-do/investment-management/private-wealth-management/intellectual-capital/isg-outlook-2017.pdf", and it is worth taking a read.

Two other points: first, we have to wait for the new president, and see how his first 100 days play out. Bond yields are back where they were early in December. US stocks have gone nowhere for a month. There is no great retracement of the Trump rally, and I think "http://www.marketwatch.com/story/beware-of-the-trump-dump-in-stocks-as-rally-peters-out-2017-01-11"But for another move forward, "https://www.bloomberg.com/view/articles/2017-01-12/trump-rally-needs-more-policy-fuel", it will need some more policy fuel.

Second, Goldman also subscribes to worries about Chinese credit. Yesterday I offered a chart putting Chinese credit in the context of previous great credit crises. Goldman offers a chart that is strikingly similar.

Chinese credit is still nagging away at influential investors  minds. It would not take much bad news on that front to start a new  risk-off  wave.

Thinking fast and slow

Last night I was lucky enough to have dinner withDaniel Kahneman. Almost certainly the only person ever awarded the Nobel Prize for economics even though he does not describe himself as an economist, "https://www.ft.com/content/a33e54d0-eefd-11dd-bbb5-0000779fd2ac?emailid=56cef8b84218200300714481&segmentId=e2b21419-1ad0-96bc-fb17-88413ea973e2"nowadays best known for his book "https://www.amazon.com/Thinking-Fast-Slow-Daniel-Kahneman/dp/0374533555".

His greatest work was carried out with Amos Tversky, now no longer with us. Between them, working at a time when Israel s conflicts were at their peak, the two psychologists discovered and documented a raft of "https://www.ft.com/content/d2b32b2c-e034-11e3-9534-00144feabdc0?emailid=56cef8b84218200300714481&segmentId=e2b21419-1ad0-96bc-fb17-88413ea973e2"that lead to "https://www.amazon.com/Predictably-Irrational-Revised-Expanded-Decisions/dp/0061353248"behaviour. Although neither had much interest in finance or investing, they became the fathers of behavioural finance.The Undoing Project, anew book by Michael Lewis, tells the story of their great collaboration.

Kahneman is now inhis 80s, but does not look it. He appeared in New York at a networking dinner, held in the unlikely confines of the LGBT Center in Greenwich Village, that had been organised by Zach Liberman and Saumitra Thakur of The College-100network. He is as fascinatingas he is generous with his time. Here are some of the thoughts he shared that struck me as most interesting:

* The most exciting field in psychology now is no longer decision-making theory but artificial intelligence   which is an area where investors are now taking an intense interest, and where the last 18 months have seen vital breakthroughs. Computers can now mimic an individual's voice so that it is indistinguishable from the original. It is not long before they can do this for video.

* He enjoys changing his mind, and has no  sunk costs  when he works. Changing his mind means that he has learnt something.

* Sunk costs   or in a psychological context, a commitment to a belief or a theory that we have already nurtured   are a great problem, and cause many to get stuck. This applies either to projects or to investments.

* The use of the words  rational  and  irrational  in common usage is a recent development. Their definitions are not clear-cut, and when someone accuses someone else of being  irrational    as often happens when coastal elitists criticise Trump voters   what they really mean is that they have a polite way to call them "evil".

* There is already great acceptance of behavioural theories in microeconomics. Even though he does not in any way consider himself an economist, his ideas have been widely taken on board. What is not clear is whether behavioural science can be applied to macroeconomics. It hasn't happened yet.

* Collaboration is a matter of luck. His collaboration with Tversky worked because they found each other funny.

*  The world is not kind to collaborators    a quote from Tversky himself. There is an urge to give credit to an individual and to assume that one person did something that makes collaborations very difficult. This was true even though this work with Tversky was a true collaboration. They were two brilliant men, but the work they did together was  clearly superior to what either of us could have done on our own .

*

As for Michael Lewis  book, he found it uncomfortable reading but hopes that it will inspire more collaborations. His response to the election of Donald Trump was to take a Valium and go to bed early.

Separately, "https://www.ft.com/content/ec89a34a-bc6f-11e6-8b45-b8b81dd5d080?emailid=56cef8b84218200300714481&segmentId=e2b21419-1ad0-96bc-fb17-88413ea973e2" that the Trump victory owes much to the flaws in reasoning and perception that Kahneman and Tversky discovered, which he shared with Gary Silverman, is also worth reading.

Tired of waiting for Dow 20,000

The Dow fell today. The process of waiting for the 20,000 landmark has grown tedious. So we might as well take the cue to listen toThe Kinks. Here are Muswell Hill s finest:

Markets
Citadel to Pay $22.6 Million for Misleading Clients; Firm agreed to settle claims that it didn't always obtain the best price for orders despite giving assurances

By Dave Michaels and Alexander Osipovich 
775 words
13 January 2017
21:50
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Corrections & Amplifications:

The Securities and Exchange Commission found that Citadel Securities LLC didn't use algorithms that would have found clients the best prices. A previous version of this article incorrectly stated that Citadel did use the algorithms and referred to the SEC as the "Security and Exchange Commission." Also, the name of the earlier article's contributing reporter is Imani Moise. The first name was misspelled as "Imami." (Jan. 13, 2017)

U.S. regulators made Citadel Securities LLC pay $22.6 million for failing to fill customer orders at the best price, nabbing their first offender in the lucrative side market for executing retail stock orders.

Citadel Securities, which handles more retail trades than any other brokerage, agreed to settle claims that it didn't always obtain the best price for orders despite giving assurances that it would. The trading firm, owned by billionaire investor Kenneth Griffin, handles more than one in three retail orders that once would have gone straight to stock exchanges, promising better prices and speedier executions.

The practice in which Citadel and other high-tech "wholesalers" pay retail brokers to execute mom-and-pop orders has drawn increased scrutiny in recent years. At a Senate hearing in June 2014, Sen. Carl Levin (D., Mich.) questioned TD Ameritrade Holding Corp. about whether such deals simply reap windfalls for retail brokers instead of ensuring the best price and trade execution for their clients.

Joe Saluzzi, a co-founder of brokerage Themis Trading LLC, said Friday that "this is all about a system that's been rigged and broken for many, many years."

Firms such as Citadel, which handles 35% of the average daily volume for retail shares, noted the SEC has approved the practice, known as payment for order flow. It results in better prices for retail clients than trading on exchanges, these firms say.

Zia Ahmed, a Citadel spokesman, said the firm takes "very seriously our obligations to comply fully with all laws and regulations" and is "committed to providing superior service and execution quality to our clients."

The Securities and Exchange Commission's civil probe targeted two specific algorithms that Citadel used to execute about 2.6% of retail orders from 2008 to 2010. The algorithms decided how Citadel would fill an order when prices from a slower feed differed from prices on a richer and faster view that is sold by exchanges. When the two sources diverged, an algorithm known as FastFill used the price shown on the slower feed, which resulted in a worse price for "a substantial number of smaller orders," the SEC's order said.

Because of the way FastFill worked, some orders for larger numbers of shares actually received a better price, the SEC said.

Another algorithm, called SmartProvide, delayed the immediate execution of retail orders when the slower feed showed a better price than the faster one, the SEC said. In those cases, Citadel sent the order to the broader market, which sometimes netted clients a better price. In other cases, some orders that came back to Citadel got a worse price than if they had been immediately filled at the quote shown on the slow tape, the SEC's order said.

The Securities and Exchange Commission's action faulted Citadel because its agreements with retail broker-dealers didn't fully explain how the algorithms worked, and only said Citadel would get the best available price for clients. Under the agreement, Citadel will pay a $16 million penalty and forfeit $5.2 million in profits and $1.4 million in interest. Citadel didn't admit or deny the allegations.

"The penalty reflects that it's a substantial amount for serious conduct," Robert Cohen, co-chief of the SEC's market abuse unit, said on a conference call with reporters. "It's a difficult area to penetrate if you are an investor or even a regulator, and it affected a lot of orders."

Retail brokers who supply Citadel with orders include Charles Schwab, Ameritrade, Scottrade Financial Services and E*Trade Financial Corp. Retail brokers shouldn't be faulted because, on average, their customers benefit from trading with market makers such as Citadel, said David Weisberger, head of trading analytics at IHS Markit Ltd.

"The size of the fine has nothing to do with the impact on investors and everything to do with disclosure," Mr. Weisberger said.

Critics such as IEX Group Inc. and T. Rowe Price Group Inc. have complained that speedy trading firms could exploit arbitrage opportunities created by the gap between the slower feed, known as the consolidated tape, and faster feeds sold by the stock exchanges. That gap has narrowed in recent years as exchanges, under pressure from regulators and money managers, committed to upgrade the slower tape's technology.

Write to Dave Michaels at "mailto:dave.michaels@wsj.com" and Alexander Osipovich at "mailto:alexander.osipovich@dowjones.com"

His greatest work was carried out with Amos Tversky, now no longer with us. Between them, working at a time when Israel s conflicts were at their peak, the two psychologists discovered and documented a raft of"https://www.ft.com/content/d2b32b2c-e034-11e3-9534-00144feabdc0?emailid=56cef8b84218200300714481&segmentId=e2b21419-1ad0-96bc-fb17-88413ea973e2"that lead to"https://www.amazon.com/Predictably-Irrational-Revised-Expanded-Decisions/dp/0061353248"behaviour. Although neither had much interest in finance or investing, they became the fathers of behavioural finance.The Undoing Project, anew book by Michael Lewis, tells the story of their great collaboration.

Kahneman is now inhis 80s, but does not look it. He appeared in New York at a networking dinner, held in the unlikely confines of the LGBT Center in Greenwich Village, that had been organised by Zach Liberman and Saumitra Thakur of The College-100network. He is as fascinatingas he is generous with his time. Here are some of the thoughts he shared that struck me as most interesting:

* The most exciting field in psychology now is no longer decision-making theory but artificial intelligence   which is an area where investors are now taking an intense interest, and where the last 18 months have seen vital breakthroughs. Computers can now mimic an individual's voice so that it is indistinguishable from the original. It is not long before they can do this for video.

* He enjoys changing his mind, and has no  sunk costs  when he works. Changing his mind means that he has learnt something.

* Sunk costs   or in a psychological context, a commitment to a belief or a theory that we have already nurtured   are a great problem, and cause many to get stuck. This applies either to projects or to investments.

* The use of the words  rational  and  irrational  in common usage is a recent development. Their definitions are not clear-cut, and when someone accuses someone else of being  irrational    as often happens when coastal elitists criticise Trump voters   what they really mean is that they have a polite way to call them "evil".

* There is already great acceptance of behavioural theories in microeconomics. Even though he does not in any way consider himself an economist, his ideas have been widely taken on board. What is not clear is whether behavioural science can be applied to macroeconomics. It hasn't happened yet.

* Collaboration is a matter of luck. His collaboration with Tversky worked because they found each other funny.

*  The world is not kind to collaborators    a quote from Tversky himself. There is an urge to give credit to an individual and to assume that one person did something that makes collaborations very difficult. This was true even though this work with Tversky was a true collaboration. They were two brilliant men, but the work they did together was  clearly superior to what either of us could have done on our own .

*

As for Michael Lewis  book, he found it uncomfortable reading but hopes that it will inspire more collaborations. His response to the election of Donald Trump was to take a Valium and go to bed early.

Separately,"https://www.ft.com/content/ec89a34a-bc6f-11e6-8b45-b8b81dd5d080?emailid=56cef8b84218200300714481&segmentId=e2b21419-1ad0-96bc-fb17-88413ea973e2" that the Trump victory owes much to the flaws in reasoning and perception that Kahneman and Tversky discovered, which he shared with Gary Silverman, is also worth reading.

Tired of waiting for Dow 20,000

The Dow fell today. The process of waiting for the 20,000 landmark has grown tedious. So we might as well take the cue to listen toThe Kinks. Here are Muswell Hill s finest:

Heard on the Street
How Banks Flatter Themselves

By Michael Rapoport 
431 words
13 January 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B12
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

[Financial Analysis and Commentary]

Investors are looking for higher returns from big banks, but one measure lenders are embracing can be deceiving.

Some big banks now highlight "return on tangible common equity" in earnings reports, which start landing in investors' inboxes on Friday. This measure typically makes banks look stronger than the more straightforward "return on equity."

In J.P. Morgan Chase's third-quarter earnings press release last year, for instance, it touted a 13% return on tangible common equity. That looked better than its 10% return on common equity.

Banks say the tangible-equity return number helps investors gauge success in putting core capital to work. Investors should be wary.

Return on tangible equity is a non-GAAP measure that doesn't follow generally accepted accounting principles, something J.P. Morgan didn't make clear in its release. The Securities and Exchange Commission has been warning companies in recent months about placing too much emphasis on such ersatz measures, although it hasn't addressed banks' use of return on tangible equity during its latest push.

Tangible equity excludes intangible assets such as goodwill created from acquisitions. When those assets are stripped out, earnings are measured against a smaller base, boosting the return figure.

At least 28 banks and other financial companies disclosed tangible-equity measures in SEC filings detailing their third-quarter results, according to consulting firm Audit Analytics.

In some cases the shift to return on tangible equity gave banks a more favorable metric to tout just when it was most useful to them. At J.P. Morgan, return on tangible equity was just another line item in its financial tables back in 2011. From then on, the bank has moved it higher in releases; it has been featured in the headline section since late 2013. The change happened as banks were struggling to post returns much above their theoretical cost of capital.

Bank of America had a return on average tangible common equity of 10.3% in the third quarter versus a return on average common equity of just 7.3%. It has included the non-GAAP number on the first page of its earnings releases since the second quarter of 2015. BofA identifies it as non-GAAP and features it alongside the comparable GAAP number, although the non-GAAP disclosure is in footnotes deep in its releases.

Although investors have been starved for decent returns in recent years, they should pass when banks offer them junk food.

MYLAN

EpiPen Coverage Dropped by Cigna

A top U.S. health insurer has dropped its coverage of Mylan NV's brand-name EpiPen and switched to the half-priced version Mylan launched in response to public outrage over its sharp price increases on the lifesaving drug.

Cigna Corp. swapped its coverage of the $600 EpiPen for Mylan's $300 version, according to a document on Cigna's website outlining its prescription-drug coverage changes for 2017. Cigna will also cover a competing drug device made by Impax Laboratories Inc., and allow physicians to choose which product to prescribe, Cigna spokeswoman Karen Eldred said.

Mylan launched the cheaper version, which it refers to as a generic, in December. The product is identical to the original -- which has been on the market for nearly 30 years but carries the generic name epinephrine instead of the brand name.

"We have revised our covered drug list to encourage use of the generic version as it will deliver more overall value to customers and clients," Cigna spokeswoman Karen Eldred said.

Mylan took heat last summer for its price increases on EpiPen the list price since 2007 had climbed nearly 550% from about $94, according to data from Truven Health Analytics.

In August, Mylan announced it would launch a cheaper generic in an attempt to quell the furor over its pricing practices. The move also better positioned the company to defend its market share in anticipation of greater market competition, some analysts said.

Anne Steele and Joseph Walker

HESS

Exploratory Budget To Grow This Year

Hess Corp. said its capital and exploratory budget will grow by 18% in 2017 compared with last year as it dives deeper into the oil patch, following similar moves from other shale drillers that have made investors wary.

The company also announced it will take more than $4.6 billion in charges in the fourth quarter.

Some producers, such as Devon Energy Corp. and Pioneer Natural Resources Co., have "http://www.wsj.com/articles/some-shale-drillers-return-to-oil-patch-1471799516"as the price of crude oil has risen above $50-a-barrel territory, but investors have been wary that more drilling activity may be premature. Hess said Thursday it plans to spend $700 million this year on unconventional shale with plans to bring about 75 new wells online in the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/shale-drillers-key-to-survival-efficiency-1463514347".

Over all, the company said its 2017 capital and exploration budget will be $2.25 billion compared with "http://www.wsj.com/articles/hess-pares-2016-spending-production-plans-after-poor-quarter-1446036967".

Stephen Wilmot

DELTA AIR LINES

Carrier Cites Costs Of Pilots Contract

Delta Air Lines Inc. which reported a 37% slide in fourth-quarter net profit on a small drop in revenue, said Thursday that it thinks the worst is behind it as absorbs higher pilot costs and holds down its capacity.

The Atlanta-based company said it believes the current quarter will represent a trough in operating margins, and that things will improve in the second half in spite of the continued strengthening of the U.S. dollar, which makes it more costly for foreigners to fly on Delta. In the fourth quarter, Delta's operating margin was 10.7%, compared with 17.1% a year ago. Its long-term goal is 17% to 19%.

In the December quarter, Delta's net income fell to $622 million, a decline it blamed almost entirely on the cost of a new pilot labor contract. A year ago, Delta earned $980 million.

Revenue fell by about 0.5% to $9.46 billion. The company beat Wall Street expectations on its fourth-quarter revenue and earnings per share excluding items.

Susan Carey

Reshuffle Workers

Lowe's Cos. is cutting thousands of workers and shuffling the jobs of thousands more, according to people familiar with the matter, as the home-improvement retailer tries to adapt to shifting shopping habits.

Economy
Bank of Korea Keeps Rates Unchanged, Lowers Growth Forecast; Central bank expects South Korea's gross domestic product to grow 2.5% in 2017

By Kwanwoo Jun 
566 words
13 January 2017
05:51
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SEOUL The Bank of Korea left interest rates unchanged for a seventh straight month Friday despite cutting its economic growth forecasts for the year.

The decision to keep the main policy rate at 1.25% was widely expected as the central bank balances concerns over continued softness in the economy with the need to keep a lid on high household debt and possible capital outflows. Uncertainties at home and abroad also favored a continued cautious stance.

The central bank now expects South Korea's gross domestic product to grow 2.5% in 2017 slower than its October estimate of 2.8%--Bank of Korea Governor Lee Ju-yeol said shortly after it unanimously decided to stand pat on interest rates.

The bank also expects consumer prices will rise 1.8% this year, compared with its previous estimate for a 1.9% gain, Mr. Lee said.

"There were big adjustments in private consumption," Mr. Lee said, noting the likely weaker-than-expected consumer spending was a primary reason for slashing the forecasts. The slump in exports has eased but the recovery in domestic demand has weakened, the bank said in a separate policy statement.

All 16 market analysts polled by The Wall Street Journal had forecast the central bank would hold the base rate steady at its current record low. The bank "http://www.wsj.com/articles/bank-of-korea-surprises-with-a-rate-cut-1465434720".

Policy makers see downside risks outweighing those on the upside, with corporate restructuring and a cooling housing market clouding prospects for growth. However, uncertainties over "http://www.wsj.com/articles/fresh-arrests-in-south-korean-political-scandal-1483350679", who is awaiting a Constitutional Court decision on the matter, and the incoming U.S. administration's policies are keeping the central bank in wait-and-see mode.

The potential for capital flight spurred by the U.S. Federal Reserve's policy tightening also likely kept the bank from easing further.

Barclays economist Angela Hsieh said the central bank seemed comfortable with keeping the policy rate on hold for now but cutting rates still remained a viable option. "Factoring in weak domestic demand and a slowdown in construction, we believe further easing still cannot be completely ruled out," Ms. Hsieh said. She also said the uncertainty around U.S. President-elect Donald Trump's proposed protectionist policies weighed on South Korea's trade outlook.

As the scope for further rate cuts has narrowed, the likelihood of a larger fiscal stimulus has increased.

The government said last month it would work out a fresh fiscal-stimulus package of more than 20 trillion won ($17 billion) with additional tax revenue and state funds for financing small companies, creating jobs and expanding public services. It also plans to front-load budget spending in the first quarter.

In December, the government lowered its 2017 growth forecast to 2.6% from 3%. The nation's economy expanded 2.7% last year, following 2.6% growth in 2015.

"High private debt remains a problem for the central bank, while rising U.S. interest rates will put further pressure on capital outflows," said Emily Dabbs of Moody's Analytics. She said she expects interest rates in South Korea to stay on hold through this year.

Finance & Markets: Deal Aims At U.K. Student Housing

English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

U.S. real-estate investment firm Starwood Capital Group has made its first foray into Britain's student housing market, where demand has been booming as investors hunt for yield.

Starwood together with Round Hill Capital LLC, a U.K. real-estate investment and management firm, said Thursday that they bought six student housing assets and one development for about GBP 120 million ($145.6 million).

Demand from investors for alternative property assets, from student housing to hotels, has been surging. Shrinking returns on traditional commercial real estate such as offices and shopping malls have sent investors to niche sectors.

Alternative property assets captured 15% of all property transactions in Europe in 2016, a record share, according to data provider Real Capital Analytics.

Student housing has been one of the hot spots, and the U.K. is Europe's most active market. In 2016, the GBP 3.1 billion invested in the sector was the second highest on record, according to real-estate broker Cushman & Wakefield.

Starwood Capital made its first U.S. student housing investments in 2015. The decision to expand into the U.K. was partly because the country has attracted a large population of international students, said Zsolt Kohalmi, head of European acquisitions at Starwood Capital.

One out of five U.K. college students is from overseas, Cushman & Wakefield said.

Analysts have warned that Britain's exit from the European Union could weaken the U.K.'s appeal for foreign students.

"We believe that the U.K. is, and will continue to be, one of the top educational destinations for international students," Mr. Kohalmi said.
