Deng's Take: Behind China's Tightened Monetary Stance

Wall Street Lawyer Jay Clayton Emerges as Top Candidate for SEC Chairman

Democrats Gather Ammo Against Treasury Pick Steven Mnuchin

U.K. Regulator Raises Competition Concerns Over Mastercard 's VocaLink Buy

U.S., Europe Spat Over Bank Risk Set to Drag On

Behind China's Tightened Monetary Stance

China's top leadership has hinted at a slightly tightened monetary stance in 2017. But bubbling below that seemingly unified goal are long-simmering tensions among various financial regulators, each with its own thoughts on what policy measures are appropriate.

A divergence between the People's Bank of China and the China Banking Regulatory Commission came into focus in a recent speech by an official at the banking regulator.

Yu Xuejun said China should cut the amount of money that banks are required to hold in reserves at the central bank, adding that other financing tools could be used to adjust liquidity after a cut, according to a report last week by state-owned Shanghai Securities News. Reducing the required reserve ratio, or RRR, would let banks lend more.

Tools like medium-term lending facilities, where the central bank agrees to lend to banks at a set rate for a few months, shouldn't be overused before a cut, Mr. Yu said.

But senior party officials at the recent "http://www.wsj.com/articles/china-makes-stability-of-housing-financial-sectors-its-2017-priorities-1481894124" have called for "prudent and neutral" policy this year. That language, shifting from "accommodative" in their previous statements, implies that the central bank will refrain from cutting RRR for banks, much less lower interest rates, according to analysts.

The dissonance in rhetoric lies in different regulators' priorities. The banking commission looks out for banks, which in China are buckling under a surge of bad loans coupled with slowing economic growth. The central bank looks out for the broader financial system and attempts to address risk wherever it may arise, from trying to deleverage the local bond market to curbing declines in the yuan and arresting capital flight. Increasingly, it is also taking on a coordinator role between China's various lumbering regulatory groups.

Cutting banks' reserve requirement ratios risks adding to the cash flowing out of China the exact situation the leadership wants to prevent. The last time the central bank cut RRR was in March 2016, when it took the ratio down 0.5 percentage point to 17% for the largest banks. Since then, it has stuck with more flexible money-market instruments to adjust the supply of funds in the financial system, even though such measures are more expensive for banks.

Zhu Chaoping, an economist at UOB Kay Hian in Shanghai, said to "expect more fine-tune measures" this year, but not an RRR cut. The use medium- and short-term lending facilities started in 2015 and authorities are increasingly formalizing them as tools to adjust borrowing costs, he explained.

Given Mr. Yu's relatively narrow role at the banking regulator of overseeing state-owned financial institutions, his opinion might not carry much sway.

But his comments illustrate the need for more coordination between Chinese regulators. Similar policy tussles "http://blogs.wsj.com/chinarealtime/2016/08/03/in-a-mysterious-redaction-a-glimpse-of-chinas-policy-tussle/" between the central bank and National Development Reform Commission, the country's economic planner.

Unfortunately, analysts say last year's talk of "http://www.wsj.com/articles/china-takes-aim-at-regulatory-overhaul-1468507534" has fizzled out. China's undeniable message right now is stability at all costs.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Wall Street Lawyer Jay Clayton Emerges as Top Candidate for SEC Chairman

By Dave Michaels and Michael C. Bender

WASHINGTON Wall Street lawyer Jay Clayton has emerged as the leading candidate to be chairman of the Securities and Exchange Commission and could be announced as the nominee as soon as Wednesday, according to an official working with the transition team of President-elect Donald Trump .

Mr. Clayton, whose clients have included Goldman Sachs Group Inc . and Barclays Capital Inc ., would succeed SEC Chairman Mary Jo White , another lawyer with a history of representing Wall Street banks before becoming a regulator. Mr. Clayton, who met with Mr. Trump on Dec. 22, is a partner at Sullivan & Cromwell LLP , where he also worked on the 2014 initial public offering of Alibaba Group Holding Ltd ., according to Sullivan's website.

Mr. Trump has also considered Debra Wong Yang, who served as a U.S. attorney during the administration of former President George W. Bush , for the role of chairman. Mr. Clayton appears to have surpassed Ms. Yang, a partner at Gibson Dunn & Crutcher LLP based in Los Angeles, as the most likely candidate to run the SEC, which oversees the $27 trillion U.S. equity market. Mr. Clayton has spent his career working on the kinds of securities deals that the SEC has a hand in regulating.

Reached on his cellphone Tuesday evening, Mr. Clayton said he couldn't talk and hung up.

Mr. Clayton would become the latest Trump appointee with longstanding Wall Street ties, joining former Goldman executive Steven Mnuchin , Mr. Trump's choice for Treasury secretary; former Goldman President Gary Cohn , who will run the National Economic Council; and private-equity investor Wilbur Ross, the pick to head the Commerce Department.

Mr. Clayton represented Goldman when it received a $5 billion investment from billionaire Warren Buffett's company during the peak of the credit crisis in September 2008, according to his bio on Sullivan's website. He's also represented Goldman in connection with other investments and acquisitions, according to the law firm. Sullivan is a key outside legal adviser for Goldman and is more closely associated with Wall Street than perhaps any other law firm.

Mr. Clayton has a wide-ranging corporate practice spanning mergers and acquisitions, IPOs, corporate governance, and investment advice for high-net-worth families. Other matters that Mr. Clayton has worked on include advising Morgan Stanley on the sale of its physical oil-trading division and Bear Stearns on its sale to J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . two deals shaped heavily by the financial crisis and its aftermath and the 2014 IPO of Moelis & Co ., a boutique advisory firm. He's also represented an ownership group for the Atlanta Hawks and British Airways in its 2010 merger with Iberia.

Mr. Clayton would take over the SEC at a time when congressional Republicans are pressuring the agency to loosen fundraising rules for smaller public companies, lighten its oversight of private-equity firms, and repeal executive-compensation rules opposed by corporations.

He would be one of at least three new members to join the SEC this year. The agency has two unfilled commissioner posts in addition to the chairman's role, which Ms. White will give up on Jan. 20.

Two other securities lawyers who have advised the Trump transition team, Paul Atkins and Ralph Ferrara, also have been viewed as possible candidates to run the SEC. Mr. Atkins, the chief executive officer of Patomak Global Partners, met with Mr. Trump in late November and is also regarded as a candidate to become vice chairman of the Federal Reserve.

 Sara Randazzo and Liz Hoffman contributed to this article.

Democrats Gather Ammo Against Treasury Pick Steven Mnuchin

By Gabriel T. Rubin

Liberal groups and Democratic senators are zeroing in on the record of Steven Mnuchin , President-elect Donald Trump 's pick for Treasury secretary, aiming to pressure Republicans into voting against his nomination. The campaign is part of a larger effort by Democrats to derail some of Mr. Trump's nominees.

The senators and groups are painting Mr. Mnuchin as an executive who oversaw thousands of foreclosures during the financial crisis while at the helm of OneWest Bank . Buying the bank, formerly known as IndyMac, eventually earned Mr. Mnuchin hundreds of millions of dollars. It is now part of CIT Group Inc .

The liberal group Allied Progress is launching a six-figure television ad campaign in Arizona and Nevada to call on Sens. Jeff Flake (R., Ariz.) and Dean Heller (R., Nev.) to vote against Mr. Mnuchin's nomination.

"Mnuchin made millions after his bank foreclosed on homeowners," the ads say. They also accuse the bank of discriminating against prospective Latino home buyers, citing a complaint filed in November with the Department of Housing and Urban Development by two California-based housing organizations.

Both Sens. Heller and Flake criticized Mr. Trump for some of his conduct during the presidential campaign, and both face re-election campaigns in 2018 in states with rapidly growing Latino populations. Both states were hit particularly hard with foreclosures during the financial crisis.

Representatives for Mr. Flake and Mr. Heller didn't respond to requests for comment.

Tara Bradshaw, a spokeswoman for Mr. Mnuchin, said that he had "tirelessly worked to help families stay in their homes" during his time at OneWest. The bank was a "modification machine," not a "foreclosure machine," as Democrats allege, she said.

"The complaints filed by two California housing groups are grossly misleading since they rely on lending figures for time periods after OneWest sold its primary mortgage business," Ms. Bradshaw said. "During the time period the groups focus on, OneWest originated only a very small number of mortgages to anyone, because during that time period OneWest had sold its primary mortgage platform."

Opponents also are poring over records related to OneWest's activities while Mr. Mnuchin was there, particularly with regard to the legality of its foreclosure practices.

OneWest is under civil investigation by HUD for loan-servicing practices. CIT , OneWest's parent company, has said it is "committed to fair-lending and works hard to meet the credit needs of all communities and neighborhoods we serve."

The campaign against Mr. Mnuchin comes as Senate Democrats plan out how to proceed with Mr. Trump's cabinet nominees and try to exact political damage on the new administration, even if they can't block any of the nominations.

"Looking at the cabinet, which is stacked with billionaires, corporate executives, titans of Wall Street, and those deeply embedded in Washington's corridors of power, it seems that many of his campaign themes are quickly being abandoned," Senate Minority Leader Chuck Schumer (D., N.Y.) said Tuesday.

Late last year, Senate Democrats led by Sens. Elizabeth Warren of Massachusetts, Jeff Merkley of Oregon and Bernie Sanders of Vermont launched a website to solicit the stories of homeowners who were foreclosed on by OneWest during Mr. Mnuchin's time at the bank.

They have drawn attention to particular examples of aggressive foreclosure practices by the bank, including a case in which a Minneapolis homeowner was locked out of her home during a blizzard.

Ms. Warren has been particularly vocal in her criticism of Mr. Mnuchin, writing in a statement last month that "Mnuchin swooped in after the crash to take a second bite out of families by aggressively and sometimes illegally foreclosing on their homes," after "peddling the kind of dangerous mortgage-backed securities that eventually blew up the economy."

Additionally, the top Democrat on the Senate Banking Committee, Sherrod Brown of Ohio, wrote to Mr. Mnuchin asking him questions on a variety of banking and housing policies, including several related to his time in the financial services industry.

Mr. Mnuchin won't be providing written answers to those questions, Ms. Bradshaw said.

"The American public deserves to know where Mr. Mnuchin stands on these basic, straightforward questions about important housing and finance issues that he'll oversee," a spokesman for Sen. Brown said.

Mr. Brown will have an opportunity to question Mr. Mnuchin as a member of the Finance Committee, which has jurisdiction over the Treasury secretary's nomination.

CFTC Chairman Timothy Massad to Step Down Jan. 20

By Dave Michaels and Andrew Ackerman

WASHINGTON Timothy Massad plans to step down as chairman of the Commodity Futures Trading Commission on Jan. 20, after nearly three years at the helm of the top U.S. derivatives regulator, the agency said Tuesday.

The departure, which was expected, is set to coincide with the end of the Obama administration. Mr. Massad, a Democrat, has sought to complete stricter rules for the multitrillion-dollar swaps market. His departure opens the door for a Republican successor who could ease or unwind some of his efforts.

President-elect Donald Trump will appoint the CFTC's next leader, adding to the bounty of financial appointees who could loosen the reins on Wall Street after years of tighter supervision under Democrats. Mr. Trump also is expected to appoint a new SEC chairman, a vice chairman for supervision at the Federal Reserve as well as a new comptroller of the currency.

J. Christopher Giancarlo, the CFTC's sole Republican member, is expected to succeed Mr. Massad, at least on an interim basis. Mr. Giancarlo is also under consideration to fill the top CFTC role on a permanent basis, according to people who have spoken with the Trump transition team.

Mr. Massad's resignation plans mirror the exit of Securities and Exchange Commission Chairman Mary Jo White , who announced in November that she would leave the top U.S. markets regulator at the close of the Obama administration.

Mr. Massad has said the CFTC is underfunded given the scope of its oversight. His departure will leave the agency with just two commissioners: Mr. Giancarlo and Sharon Bowen, a Democrat. That will make it even harder for the undermanned agency to get anything done, given government sunshine laws that prevent commissioners from making decisions outside of public meetings.

The remaining commissioners will at least have a neighbor for a bit longer. While Mr. Massad plans to step down as chairman, he will stick around for a "few weeks in order to close out his office and handle administrative matters," the agency said in a news release.

In the press release Mr. Massad laid out a number of policy priorities he spearheaded, including the regulatory framework for trading and reporting swaps, products that accelerated the damage from the 2008 financial crisis. "I came to the CFTC with a number of priorities, and I am proud we have made significant progress in every area," he said.

Mr. Giancarlo may seek to ease some of the CFTC's postcrisis rules particularly swaps-trading requirements that he says have sent trading business overseas. But he has backed broad mandates for overhauling the swaps market, including the requirement that many be processed through clearinghouses, entities that guarantee trades in case one side defaults. He also could push for the introduction of examinations for swaps traders and brokers.

Mr. Massad took over the CFTC in June 2014, succeeding Gary Gensler, a former Goldman Sachs Group Inc . executive who gained a reputation as an aggressive regulator. European policy makers repeatedly chafed at Mr. Gensler's efforts to quickly and aggressively impose U.S. rules for derivatives overseas. Mr. Massad made smoothing those relations a priority after he took over.

In one of his biggest accomplishments, Mr. Massad succeeded last year in ending a lengthy impasse between the U.S. and Europe over the regulation of clearinghouses.

But in other areas he fell short of his own expectations. Last month, for instance, he failed to complete controversial curbs on commodities trading. Instead of finishing the position-limit rules, Mr. Massad opted to rewrite the proposal for the third time since 2011. That will allow the Trump administration, which has said it would dismantle the Dodd-Frank law calling for the rules, to finish them.

Mr. Massad told reporters he didn't want to complete rules that a commission under Republican leadership would be unlikely to implement or enforce.

Republican lawmakers were cordial with the CFTC chairman but refused to boost the agency's budget above $250 million for the past three years. Mr. Massad has said the agency lacks the resources to advance priorities such as testing the cybersecurity controls of firms it oversees.

He also clashed with members of his own party over bank-safety rules completed a year ago. The measure eased proposed requirements that banks set aside cash to offset the risk of losses on certain swaps trades, bucking objections from Sen. Elizabeth Warren and other Democrats who wanted a stricter approach.

Mr. Massad defended the rules, which covered collateral known as margin, as "very strong," and said they were nearly identical to restrictions imposed by fellow U.S. regulators and similar to those imposed overseas.

Fidelity International Gets Nod to Sell Investment Products in China

By Rachel Rosenthal

Chinese financial regulators have given Fidelity International the green light to offer local investment products to wealthy mainland investors, making it the first major foreign fund manager to get such a permission.

The company's Chinese operation, FIL Investment Management (Shanghai) Co., has this week registered with the Asset Management Association of China as a private fund-management company. That step allows the firm to create investment products to sell to Chinese institutional and high-net-worth investors, the firm said Wednesday.

The move marks China's latest effort to open up its financial markets to foreigners, and could herald further opportunities for global money managers to set up shop without an onshore partner.

"This is a very significant milestone in our expansion into the world's second-biggest economy," said Daisy Ho, managing director, Asia Pacific ex-Japan, at Fidelity International . "The potential for the onshore market is huge."

To date, only a handful of global investment firms have gained approval to operate wholly-owned investment companies in China, allowing them to operate as stand-alone money managers. The list includes J.P. Morgan Asset Management , U.S. hedge fund Bridgewater Associates and U.K.-based Aberdeen Asset Management . Fidelity 's registration with AMAC, an industry body that is supervised by the country's securities regulator, will allow it to actually sell products in China, the next step in the process.

Since 2001, most asset managers looking to do business in China have set up joint-venture arrangements with partners onshore. Roughly 40 fund managers have established such relationships.

The benefit of going solo is that "you finally get to control your own destiny," said Peter Alexander, founder and managing director of Shanghai consultancy Z-Ben Advisors. "You can hire who you want to hire, and won't have to spend time on negotiating politics. What you can do in your businesses in Boston, New York or Hong Kong, you can now do in Shanghai."

Fidelity International has a six-month window to launch its first product onshore. The platform will be available only to institutional clients and high-net-worth investors, which includes individuals with financial assets of no less than 3 million yuan ($430,000) or personal annual average income of no less than 500,000 yuan for the past three years.

The firm's registration is the latest development in China's recent effort to welcome foreign investors to its markets. In December, China launched a trading link between the Hong Kong and Shenzhen stock markets, and last February officials lifted restrictions on foreign investors in China's main bond market.

Still, investors have met these efforts with caution. China's yuan has weakened sharply against a resurgent U.S. dollar falling nearly 7% against the greenback in 2016, and is approaching the psychologically important level of 7 yuan per U.S. dollar. China's bond market has been rattled by regulators' recent campaign to deleverage and tighten monetary conditions, and its stock market was one of Asia's worst-performing last year.

"We understand there will continue to be volatility in China's markets," said Ms. Ho. "But because of our nature, our vision is for long-term growth."

Fidelity International will have to compete with Chinese peers, who have a well-established presence on the ground. Still, the firm has more than a decade of experience operating in China, with a staff of 400 employees there.

The firm has mainly offered domestic institutional clients and retail investors the ability to invest offshore. The firm also has a quota of $1.2 billion under the Qualified Foreign Institutional Investor Scheme, a program that allows international investors to buy Chinese stocks and bonds with yuan raised outside China. That is one of the largest amounts held by any fund manager globally.

Established in 1969 as the international arm of Fidelity Investments , Fidelity International split from the U.S. organization in 1980, and is today owned mainly by management and members of the original founding family.

WSJ Pro: U.K. Regulator Raises Competition Concerns Over Mastercard 's VocaLink Buy

By Ian Walker

LONDON The U.K. Competition and Markets Authority said Wednesday Mastercard Inc .'s acquisition of VocaLink gives rise to competition concerns, and it is seeking undertakings from the companies to address these issues.

They have until Jan. 11 to offer a solution or the regulator will refer the deal for an in-depth investigation, it said.

Last July Mastercard said it was buying 92.4% of VocaLink Holdings Ltd. in a deal valued around $920 million. If the deal is completed, a majority of VocaLink's current owners will retain a 7.6% stake for at least three years.

Mastercard U.K. Holdco Ltd., a unit of Mastercard , already owns and operates credit- and debit-card schemes Mastercard , Maestro and Cirrus, and has bid to supply infrastructure services to U.K. interbank payment systems.

VocaLink's technology provides the backbone for non-card transactions such as employer-payroll deposits and consumer-bill payments, processing over 90% of salaries, more than 70% of household bills and almost all state benefits in the U.K.

It also unites the infrastructure of Britain's automated-teller-machine network among its participating banks via the LINK network. VocaLink reported $240 million in revenue last year and processed more than 11 billion transactions.

The regulator said its main concern is the LINK ATM network as VocaLink and Mastercard are two of the three most credible providers of infrastructure services to the network across the U.K. As such, it believes the merger would reduce the number of bidders and limit the ability of the LINK scheme to obtain good value when tendering for an infrastructure provider.

"The LINK ATM network provides an essential service for millions of customers. It's important that LINK has a good choice of providers when it comes to supplying the necessary infrastructure so it can take advantage of the opening up of payment systems to competition," Andrea Coscelli, acting chief executive of the CMA, and decision maker in this case, said.

"These concerns warrant a closer investigation in the event that Mastercard cannot address them at this stage," she said.

Mastercard said it would address this concern within the time frame provided.

VocaLink is owned by a consortium of 18 banks and building societies, with the four biggest being Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC , Barclays PLC , HSBC Holdings PLC , and Lloyds Banking Group PLC .

Last July, after the deal was announced, the U.K. Payment Systems Regulator said the four largest shareholders of VocaLink should sell all, or part of their holdings in the infrastructure provider if its acquisition by Mastercard doesn't go ahead, to open up the market and boost competition.

At the time of the deal, RBS confirmed it would sell its 21.4% shareholding in VocaLink and book a 150 million ($183.6 million) pretax gain in its accounts. Barclays also said it sold 13.68% of its shareholding in VocaLink for 104 million, leaving it with a 1.5% stake.

U.S., Europe Spat Over Bank Risk Set to Drag On

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine and Max Colchester

BRUSSELS A fight between U.S. and European authorities over how banks should calculate the riskiness of their assets will drag out into 2017, extending lingering uncertainty over the health of lenders' balance sheets.

After months of intense lobbying by European banks, the international standards setter, the Basel Committee on Banking Supervision , Tuesday delayed a key meeting.

European authorities complained that new proposed rules would force their banks to thicken capital cushions and stymie lending.

"The committee came to recognize the initial set of proposals was an overshoot," said a person familiar with the discussions.

Indonesia's Message to J.P. Morgan : Accentuate the Positive

By Carolyn Cui, Emily Glazer, Ben Otto and Rachel Rosenthal

Indonesia's decision to cut business ties with J.P. Morgan Chase & Co . after a downgrade highlights the fine line banks and ratings companies walk.

In the banking world, securities analysts are supposed to provide independent, unvarnished investment advice to their commission-paying clients, but a negative word can alienate clients on the other side the stock and bond issuers that pay banks large fees to help them raise capital. That can cost bankers' access to corporate executives or government officials, and lose them business from the offended issuers.

Emerging-country governments often react strongly to sovereign-ratings downgrades, which can raise the cost of borrowing in international capital markets. The offense in this case, though, was a downgrade of Indonesia's stocks, partly occasioned by Donald Trump 's U.S. election win a ratings change that the country's finance ministry said could destabilize the financial system.

The ministry responded by ending all partnerships with J.P. Morgan as of Jan. 1, including the bank's role as a government-appointed primary dealer in certain bond auctions. For a nation to completely cut off a bank that way appears rare. It was a first for Indonesia.

The decision was the product of several run-ins between Southeast Asia's biggest country and the largest U.S. bank by assets, the ministry's director of debt portfolio and strategy, Schneider Siahaan, told The Wall Street Journal.

It was the third time a J.P. Morgan ratings call threatened to exacerbate selloffs, Mr. Siahaan said in 2008 and 2015 Indonesia issued warnings to the bank after its research division downgraded the government's rupiah bonds and the government believes J.P. Morgan has a conflict of interest.

"As a bonds dealer, J.P. Morgan was tasked to find buyers, but at the same time they issued a recommendation to sell," Mr. Siahaan said. "We see they're no longer effective in carrying out their obligations."

J.P. Morgan reiterated that its Indonesia business is operating as usual and that it is working for a resolution with the finance ministry. It declined to comment on possible conflicts of interest.

The bank was working toward improving its relationship with the country, according to a person familiar with the matter, when U.S. voters elected Mr. Trump. Then, in a Nov. 13 report about the global repercussions of Mr. Trump's victory, J.P. Morgan downgraded Indonesian equities to underweight from overweight, saying that a rise in bond-market volatility "increases emerging-market risk premiums."

The report, authored by six analysts led by veteran Asian strategist Adrian Mowat, also made smaller downgrades to Brazilian and Turkish equities.

Mr. Mowat wasn't available for comment.

The next day, the yield on Indonesia's 10-year government bond shot up 0.466 percentage point, its biggest intraday jump since January 2011, to 7.895%. Yields rise as prices fall.

While Indonesia's debt leverage is relatively low for Asia, its external debt is among the highest when measured against foreign-exchange reserves, according to Standard Chartered . That essentially makes Indonesia more vulnerable than other Asian countries to a stronger dollar and higher U.S. interest rates, as does the relatively high proportion of government debt held by foreigners nearly 40% of rupiah bonds and almost 90% of U.S. dollar bonds.

In some cases, banks under fire for negative opinions have sought to placate issuers or make amends for analysis that could be seen as unfair by firing employees or rewriting reports after publication.

Ratings firms can face similar heat.

The U.S. Treasury called Standard & Poor's decision to strip the country of its top credit rating in 2011 "flawed." When Standard & Poor's cut Poland's credit rating to BBB-plus from A-minus a year ago, citing its new government's radical policies, the finance minister at the time, Pawel Szalamacha, called the decision "incomprehensible &from economic and financial-analysis point of view."

 It is nonsense that Japan's national strength is lower than African countries to which Japan is providing assistance.'

 Japanese Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi in 2002

When Moody's Investors Service downgraded its outlook for China's sovereign rating last March, citing a slowing economy and shrinking foreign reserves, state-run news agency Xinhua answered with an editorial accusing it of "practices that are damaging its own reputation" namely, holding emerging and developed economies to different standards. Moody's declined to comment.

In the early 2000s the government of Japan blasted all three of the major credit-rating companies for downgrades, including one from Moody's that rated the country's yen debt riskier than Botswana bonds. "It is nonsense that Japan's national strength is lower than African countries to which Japan is providing assistance," said then-Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi at the time.

A government outcry over an equities call, like Indonesia's response to J.P. Morgan , is much rarer.

"There's instances of sovereigns railing against rating agencies where formal ratings are applied, which do have more real financial or economic consequences," said Mark Baker, emerging-markets portfolio manager at Standard Life Investments in Hong Kong. "I don't ever recall seeing an instance where a change of outlook from a bank elicited such a response."

 I Made Sentana contributed to this article.

Equifax , TransUnion Settle CFPB Claims of Deceptive Marketing

By Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON Top credit-reporting companies Equifax Inc . and TransUnion have agreed to pay more than $23 million over federal claims that they deceptively marketed and sold credit scores to consumers.

The Consumer Financial Protection Bureau said Tuesday that Atlanta-based Equifax and Chicago-based TransUnion  two of the three largest credit-reporting firms falsely advertised how lenders use credit scores and deceptively charged consumers for subscriptions to check their own score.

Credit reporting firms are required by law to provide one free credit report to consumers every year. Consumers typically end up seeing their credit score or report when applying for a loan, and some banks now offer the score free as part of a bank account.

Equifax and TransUnion collectively have agreed to pay more than $17.6 million in restitution to consumers and $5.5 million in fines, without admitting or denying the CFPB's findings.

"TransUnion and Equifax deceived consumers about the usefulness of the credit scores they marketed, and lured consumers into expensive recurring payments with false promises," CFPB Director Richard Cordray said in written statement.

Equifax and TransUnion in separate statements said they already have fixed many of the issues raised by the CFPB. The cited activities occurred as early as July 2011 for both companies, and for Equifax continued through March 2014, according to the CFPB.

The CFPB said both companies and their subsidiaries falsely advertised a credit score to the consumer as the same score lenders use when underwriting a loan. A lender typically uses several different scores that can sometimes include those provided by Equifax and TransUnion to determine the interest rate and eligibility for a consumer credit.

The CFPB also accused Equifax of offering a free credit-score trial and TransUnion of offering a similar trial for $1 after which the companies would automatically enroll the consumer into a subscription once the trial ended. The result was that consumers who didn't cancel their subscription during the trial period were then charged a recurring fee, typically $16 or more a month, the CFPB said.

Equifax agreed to pay $3.8 million in consumer restitution and $2.5 million in civil penalties. TransUnion has agreed to pay more than $13.9 million in consumer restitution and a $3 million penalty.

"The CFPB's investigation into these matters has been ongoing for nearly three years, and Equifax implemented changes addressing the CFPB's concerns shortly after the investigation began," Equifax said. "While Equifax does not believe it has violated any laws and has not admitted any liability, Equifax determined it was in its best interest to resolve the matter."

"We continue to believe that our consumer marketing has been clear and has complied with the law and other government guidance," TransUnion said. "However, we are committed to making improvements to our consumer experience, and over the past several months we have worked cooperatively with the CFPB to be the industry leader."

The CFPB estimates about 700,000 consumers had enrolled in TransUnion 's trial subscription then canceled it within two billing cycles but didn't get a refund.

"We have agreed to fund a generous program to fully compensate any consumer who may not have understood that his or her trial program would convert to a subscription," TransUnion said.

WSJ Pro: Senate Republicans Name Three More Lawmakers to Banking Panel

WASHINGTON Republican leaders named three more lawmakers to the Senate Banking Committee as the new Congress convened on Tuesday.

Jesse Litvak, A Symbol of the Government Crackdown on Wall Street, Goes on Trial Again

By Sara Randazzo

A securities-fraud case that triggered widespread changes to sales tactics on Wall Street is once again in the spotlight as a former Jefferies Group LLC bond trader goes back on trial Wednesday in New Haven, Conn.

A jury in 2014 found the former trader, Jesse Litvak, guilty of fraud for misleading customers about the price he paid for residential mortgage-backed bonds. A federal appellate court cast doubt on the conviction a year ago, throwing out parts of the case and ordering the securities-fraud charges to go before a new jury.

The case, first brought by the Connecticut U.S. attorney's office in January 2013, accused Mr. Litvak of swindling customers out of $2 million by inflating the prices he said he paid for securities. Those misstatements violated securities laws, prosecutors claim, and caused hedge funds and professional investment managers to overpay for bonds.

The case caused many banks to re-evaluate their policies and cast a harsher eye toward negotiating tactics often considered commonplace sales puffery. Federal prosecutors have since brought civil and criminal charges against several other bond traders for similar sales tactics. A second conviction of Mr. Litvak could further bolster those efforts.

Jury selection is scheduled to begin Wednesday in the three-week retrial. A spokesman for the U.S. attorney's office and Mr. Litvak's lawyers at Williams & Connolly LLP declined to comment.

The Second U.S. Court of Appeals ruled in December 2015 that a judge unfairly blocked Mr. Litvak from introducing testimony that could have assisted his defense. That testimony, Mr. Litvak contends, would have shown that bond buyers don't rely on the price a broker paid for a security when making deals. Unlike stocks or other securities sold on open exchanges, bonds are sold in an opaque market, with prices negotiated through brokers.

"It's certainly fairer to defendants to put this expert testimony in," securities-law expert John Coffee of Columbia Law School said. "On the other hand, I think juries may give more weight to an investor saying,  We really want to know this [pricing information] for the following reasons.'"

The jury will hear details of trades Mr. Litvak made between 2009 and December 2011 while employed in the Stamford, Conn., office of brokerage firm Jefferies. Prosecutors say that, on average, he made $100,000 more in profits for Jefferies on each transaction than his customers were led to believe.

Mr. Litvak was sentenced to two years in prison and fined $1.75 million. He has been out on bail since his arrest, and he relocated to Florida in 2015, court filings show.

The appellate court, while ordering the retrial, didn't discount the underlying legal theory of the case: that misleading customers on bond prices could be considered fraud. "Without knowledge of Litvak's actions, the financial consequences of negotiations colored by false representations were virtually undiscoverable in the opaque RMBS market," the court wrote, referring to residential mortgage-backed securities. Defaults on mortgages that underpinned some of these securities were a major driver of the 2008 financial crisis.

Unlike in the first trial, the jury won't be asked to find that Mr. Litvak defrauded the government, charges that were dismissed by the Second Circuit. Because of that, Mr. Litvak has argued that the jury shouldn't be able to hear that many of his customers were funds backed by the U.S. government 's banking bailout, called the Troubled Asset Relief Program, which authorized private money managers to use taxpayer funds to buy troubled securities. U.S. District Judge Janet Hall disagreed, ruling that prosecutors can say government money was used.

At the first trial, one prosecution witness was Michael Canter, a public-private investment fund manager who used Treasury money to buy bonds. According to court filings, Mr. Canter testified that, when he found out Mr. Litvak misled him, he called Mr. Litvak and said, "[A]re you freaking crazy doing this to the United States Treasury Department . Because of this, I'm going to have to report this."

While the jury will be able to hear some broader context on the bond market, the judge ruled that Mr. Litvak can't present testimony that broker-dealers at other firms similarly inflated bond prices, or show evidence of changes to compliance programs on Wall Street after his indictment. Mr. Litvak has argued that showing standards were tightened only after his indictment supports his point that the kinds of misrepresentations he made weren't previously viewed as a problem in the industry.

"At the end of the day, this case is about Jesse Litvak," Judge Hall wrote in a June pretrial ruling. "It is not a trial of the entire RMBS industry."

Analysis: Making Banks Safer Waits for Another Day

By Paul J. Davies

U.S. and European banks have long had their differences, nowhere more so than over how much capital they need to stay safe.

In American eyes, Europeans have long looked both undercapitalized and overly reliant on complex models that minimize requirements. The negotiations over changes to global rule changes meant to narrow those differences are proving harder than expected. The final agreement was due to be signed off by the Basel Committee this coming Sunday but has been postponed, perhaps until March.

This is a shame because the outline deal that emerged in December looked set to narrow the capital gap substantially while giving European banks plenty of time to adjust, which is the only practical way to ensure the availability of bank credit in Europe was preserved. A slow transition would also benefit European bank investors by protecting dividends and coupons on junior bonds.

The rule changes, known among bankers as "Basel IV," are meant to limit how much banks can minimize capital requirements by using sophisticated risk models.

The simple way to see the potential effects is to look at risk density, which shows how much banks rely on modeling. The measure is expressed by giving the proportion of assets that attract a capital charge.

The best measure of risk density uses the leverage exposure measure of balance sheet size, because that irons out most of the differences in U.S. and international accounting over how to measure derivatives exposure. Done this way, European banks' average density is 33%, compared with the U.S. average of 49%.

Now, look at the potential Basel IV impact. The changes outlined in December were expected to be less painful than was originally feared after noisy protests from Germany and other countries. However, they would still make a difference. Morgan Stanley analysts estimate average European risk-weighted assets will increase 21%, although the inflation will be much greater in some countries than others.

The average risk density for big European banks could rise to more than 40%, based on Morgan Stanley forecasts for individual countries. That is much closer to the U.S. average. Deutsche Bank 's density would be almost 35% compared with 48% for J.P. Morgan Chase .

The capital gap would still exist; it always will because European banks don't sell their prime mortgages. But those Basel IV changes would have made it much smaller and European banks better capitalized.

Analysis: How China's Insurance Crackdown Spawns More Risks

By Anjani Trivedi

It was only a matter of time before Beijing realized that giving its insurers a free rein was going to be a plan steeped in risk.

Following a year in which Chinese insurers aggressively built risky and illiquid portfolios, acquired real estate, companies and stakes in companies at home and across the world, under loose regulation, China's insurance regulator has taken to severe tightening measures in recent weeks. Regulators have effectively barred insurers from making risky investments and banned certain insurers and insurance products with high cash value and low protection.

The repercussions could cause trouble far and wide. Almost 60% of China's wealth-management products are invested in bond and money-market funds and a growing portion of these products are backed by bonds. And, they account for almost 20% of banking-system deposits. Chinese insurers' nonstandard investments a bundle of debt plans, wealth-management products and the like rose to more than a third of their assets toward the end of last year. Tightening too fast could turn some insurers into forced sellers and expose a larger-than-expected heap of credit risk on insurers' balance sheets. Shares of Chinese insurers are down 9% since December, after ending last year down 15%, on average.

China's unlisted insurers infamous among them, Anbang will now see their wealth-management, product-backed financing platforms squeezed under new rules that bar investing cash raised from investment products. With policyholder deposits accounting for more than 70% of written premiums, more asset-selling may be on the cards.

Taming its financial giants, Beijing will find, is bound to spawn more chaos in China's intertwined financial system.

Senate Republicans Name Three Additional Lawmakers to Banking Panel; Sens. John Kennedy, David Perdue and Thom Tillis join Senate Banking Committee

By Rachel Witkowski 
369 words
4 January 2017
01:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON Republican leaders named three more lawmakers to the Senate Banking Committee as the new Congress convened on Tuesday.

Sens. John Kennedy (R., La.), David Perdue (R., Ga.) and Thom Tillis (R., N.C.) will join the panel, which will play a key role in deciding on potential changes to financial regulation considered in Congress this year.

Mr. Perdue, a former chief executive of Dollar General Corp. and Reebok International Ltd., has called for a repeal of the 2010 Dodd-Frank law, saying it is "one of the most onerous, overreaching pieces of legislation in my lifetime." He also has served on the board of Cardlytics Inc ., a banking data analytics company.

Mr. Tillis and Mr. Kennedy also have advocated for changing Dodd-Frank, according to past interviews.

The incoming Republicans on the committee join "http://www.wsj.com/articles/democrats-add-fresh-faces-to-senate-banking-committee-1482441066": Sens. Brian Schatz of Hawaii, Catherine Cortez Masto of Nevada and Chris Van Hollen of Maryland.

Republicans have a majority on the committee, one of the primary congressional committees with oversight of the financial industry. They are likely to face aggressive opposition from Ranking Member Sen. Sherrod Brown of Ohio and Sen. Elizabeth Warren of Massachusetts, who are staunchly opposed to Republican attempts to change the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul.

Incoming Senate Banking Committee Chairman Mike Crapo (R., Idaho) replaces Sen. Richard Shelby (R., Ala.), who will remain on the committee in a senior role. Mr. Shelby has advocated for changes to Dodd-Frank, including making the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau 's budget subject to congressional approval.

Mr. Brown has said he expects to have a better working relationship with Mr. Crapo than he did with Mr. Shelby.

"It will be a better day with Sen. Crapo," Mr. Brown "http://www.wsj.com/articles/sen-brown-hopes-sen-crapo-helps-to-set-fannie-and-freddie-free-1480620048" in Washington. "Sen. Crapo is an honorable guy, way more conservative than I am. That's fine. The point is that he will work with us. I am optimistic that we can do things together."

Write to Rachel Witkowski at "mailto:rachel.witkowski@wsj.com"

Predictive analytics startup EverString has promoted co-founder and president J.J. Kardwell to be its chief executive.

Former CEO Vincent Yang was named executive chairman, and Mr. Kardwell has also become a member of EverString's board.

Business News: SoftBanks Fund Hits Its $100 Billion Goal

By Liz Hoffman and Ted Greenwald 
443 words
4 January 2017
The Wall Street Journal
J
B3
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SoftBank Group Corp. has lined up chip maker Qualcomm Inc. as an investor in the Japanese firm's new technology fund, helping the vast portfolio reach its $100 billion goal, according to people familiar with the matter.

San Diego-based Qualcomm has moved toward completing its commitment in recent days, the people said. It joins Apple Inc., which previously agreed to invest in the fund, the people said. The amounts to be invested couldn't be determined.

Together, the pair brings a Silicon Valley imprimatur to what would be one of the world's largest pools of private capital.

SoftBank -- the conglomerate whose chief executive, Masayoshi Son, has spoken of a coming technology revolution -- plans to invest in next-generation technologies such as artificial intelligence.

The SoftBank Vision Fund was announced in October and thrust back in the public eye last month, when Mr. Son met with President-elect Donald Trump in New York and pledged to invest $50 billion in the U.S. and create thousands of jobs. That money is expected to come from the new fund.

The telecom company's fund has gathered the $100 billion in commitments it was seeking and is set to formally launch in the coming weeks, according to people familiar with the matter.

SoftBank said when it announced the fund that it will contribute $25 billion of its own cash over the next five years. Saudi Arabia's government fund has agreed to invest up to $45 billion over the period.

The Wall Street Journal reported Dec. 12 that Apple and the sovereign-wealth fund of Abu Dhabi have also discussed investing. Those negotiations have since advanced and are near being completed, people familiar with the matter said.

The Qatar Investment Authority, the wealth fund of another oil-rich nation, is also in discussions to invest, the people said.

Apple and the Qatar Investment Authority declined to comment. A representative of the Abu Dhabi wealth fund couldn't be reached.

SoftBank is the third-largest mobile carrier in Japan, but Mr. Son has been expanding its reach through deal making. SoftBank in 2013 bought majority control of U.S.-based Sprint Corp. and in 2016 spent $32 billion to acquire ARM Holding PLC, which designs microchips.

That takeover, SoftBank's largest ever, is a bet on the so-called Internet of Things, in which millions of everyday devices are connected online. Mr. Son has predicted that in the next 20 years, one trillion chips will go into gadgets from thermostats to watches.

Pro Private Markets
The Roundup

5,966 words
3 January 2017
15:06
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

U.S. & CANADA

Buyouts

CenterGate Capital, Austin, Texas

CenterGate Capital wrapped up its first fund at its hard cap with $350 million in investor commitments. Founded in 2014, the firm completed fundraising for CenterGate Capital Partners I LP, which had a $300 million target, after about a year of fundraising, said a person familiar with the matter. Combined with general-partner commitments, the fund will have $365 million to invest, people familiar with the fund said. CenterGate targets businesses with $8 million to $15 million in annual earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization, one of the people said.

Deerfield Management Co., New York

Deerfield Management Co. collected at least $2.39 billion toward a $2.5 billion goal for Deerfield Private Design Fund IV LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The fund's predecessor raised about $1.6 billion in 2014. Fund III invests in health-care sectors including biotechnology, pharmaceuticals, medical devices, health-care services and animal health. Among the investments from Deerfield's third Private Design fund is Recovery Centers of America, a behavioral health operator in which the firm initially injected $231.5 million in late 2015 and invested an additional $100 million in 2016.

KarpReilly, Greenwich, Conn.

KarpReilly collected at least $200 million for KarpReilly Capital Partners III LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. It isn't clear whether the fund has officially closed. KarpReilly, named after co-founders Allan Karp and Chris Reilly, makes equity investments of $3 million to $75 million in sectors including the retail, e-commerce, direct-to-consumer, restaurant, branded consumer product, apparel, natural product and food and beverage markets. The firm in 2013 said it held a final closing of its second fund at $210 million and of a co-investment vehicle at $150 million. KarpReilly in 2016 invested in Lifeaid Beverage Co., a Santa Cruz, Calif., maker of nutritional beverages; San Francisco juice producer Zola; and BoxyCharm Inc., a Miami Gardens, Fla., business that sends subscribers boxes containing beauty products each month.

KKR & Co., New York

KKR & Co. closed its first dedicated fund for taking stakes in technology, media and telecommunications companies. The firm said KKR Next Generation Technology Growth Fund LP collected $711 million and exceeded its original target, which KKR declined to disclose. The vehicle is expected to make equity investments of less than $100 million in sectors including software, information technology, security, digital media and the Internet of Things. The firm will make investments from the fund averaging $40 million to $50 million, said David Welsh, a KKR managing director who heads the firm's growth-equity program for TMT. Its growth-capital deals will be done within a group led by Herald Chen, Richard Sarnoff and Philipp Freise.

L Catterton, Greenwich, Conn.

L Catterton closed its latest flagship fund at $2.75 billion. The amount raised for L Catterton VIII LP surpassed the $1.68 billion the firm gathered for its previous fund, Catterton Partners VII LP, which held a final closing in 2013. Through the latest pool, L Catterton will invest $60 million to $500 million at a time in midmarket consumer companies throughout North America and Europe. The initial hard cap for the newest fund was $2.5 billion, said a person familiar with the fund, adding that the vehicle has a broad group of investors ranging from sovereign-wealth funds to strategic investors. No single investor accounts for more than 10% of the fund's total commitments, the person said. L Catterton was formed through the merger of consumer-focused Catterton with the private-equity arm of LVMH Mot Hennessy Louis Vuitton SE and Groupe Arnault in 2016. The firm last year closed L Catterton Growth Partners III LP at $615 million to invest in consumer-facing companies.

Pamlico Capital, Charlotte, N.C.

Pamlico Capital closed Pamlico Capital IV LP with $910 million to invest in lower midmarket companies within the business and technology services, communications and health-care sectors. The capital raised exceeded an original $750 million target as well as the $650 million Pamlico raised for the fund's predecessor in 2013. Pamlico expects to begin investing the new fund in the second or third quarter of 2017 after it finishes deploying Fund III, which is about 80% invested, said Partner Eric Eubank. Although the new fund is larger than the firm's previous pool of capital, Pamlico expects to keep its investments in the same range, about $20 million to $100 million, and make about 15 or 16 investments, a few more than the previous fund, Mr. Eubank said. The vehicle will target companies with enterprise values of $50 million to $250 million.

Providence Equity Partners, Providence, R.I.

Providence Equity Partners, through growth-focused affiliate Providence Strategic Growth Capital Partners, closed Providence Strategic Growth II LP at the fund's $640 million hard cap. An April filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission indicated the vehicle had an offering amount of $600 million. Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement System committed up to $75 million to the fund, according to an October news release from the pension. Fund II so far has invested in six companies, including payment and business services software company PaySimple Inc.; LogicMonitor Inc., which manages and automates information-technology infrastructure for businesses; and life-sciences software platform Anju Software Inc. The fund's predecessor closed at $315 million in 2015, surpassing its $250 million target. Providence's growth-equity arm invests in lower midmarket software and technology-enabled services companies, primarily in North America.

Rock Hill Capital, Houston

Rock Hill Capital raised at least $128.5 million so far for Rock Hill Capital III LP, which is aiming to collect $175 million, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm, which closed its previous vehicle in 2013 with $87.7 million, invests in industrial companies in the south and southeast U.S. with enterprise values of up to $60 million and earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization of less than $10 million. Rock Hill in 2016 sold liquid waste management company SouthWaste Services Holdings LLC and Tideland Signal Corp., a maker of marine-navigation products. In December 2015, it acquired Big City Access Inc., which makes scaffolding equipment.

Wind Point Partners, Chicago

Wind Point Partners is more than halfway to the amount it is seeking for its eighth fund, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. A pair of related Wind Point Partners VIII vehicles have gathered a total of at least $434.7 million and have a $750 million offering amount, according to the filings. The State of Wisconsin Investment Board committed $50 million to Fund VIII, pension documents show. Wind Point wrapped up the fund's predecessor at $915 million in 2009. The firm invests $30 million to $150 million in companies that have at least $10 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. The firm in 2016 acquired companies including plastics manufacturer Aurora Plastics Inc., warehouse-logistics services provider St. George Logistics and pork-rind maker Evans Food Group Ltd. The firm also in November announced plans to sell packaging company Novolex to Carlyle Group LP.

Distressed/Turnaround

Solace Capital Partners, Los Angeles

Solace Capital Partners wrapped up fundraising for its first vehicle, Solace Capital Special Situations Fund LP, at $576 million, above a $500 million target. The firm, which typically invests $10 million to $75 million per deal, has invested or committed more than $120 million from the fund across five portfolio companies. Solace targets distressed debt and special situations private-equity deals, seeking U.S. and Canadian companies that generate annual revenue of $50 million to $1 billion. Solace, whose formation Private Equity Analyst initially reported in 2013, formally launched in 2014, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm began raising its inaugural vehicle in late 2014, Private Equity Analyst previously reported, citing people familiar with the situation. The vehicle had collected at least $388.9 million as of late April, according to a separate SEC filing.

Z Capital Group, New York

Z Capital Group plans to raise at least $1 billion for a fund to prop up smaller companies and businesses in need of cash. The firm could round up as much as $1.25 billion for Z Capital Partners III LP, according to a November letter to investors viewed by Private Equity Analyst. Z Capital expects the pool to surpass the $750 million collected for its last private-equity fund in 2014. The firm has drawn demand of close to $1 billion so far, said a person familiar with the matter. Z Capital officially will launch marketing efforts in 2017 and plans to wrap up fundraising by the second quarter. As with its previous pool, Z Capital expects to continue targeting equity investments of roughly $100 million in total size and focusing on companies with enterprise values of less than $1 billion. The firm's private-equity team takes control stakes in companies in a variety of ways, from snapping up assets in bankruptcy auctions to spinning them off from corporations, participating in balance-sheet restructurings and taking them private. The firm in 2016 launched fundraising for Z Capital Credit Tactical Fund LP, a new offering to pursue noncontrol investments and provide senior secured debt. Z Capital is seeking up to $500 million for the vehicle and closed on roughly $100 million as of the beginning of December, said a person familiar with the matter.

Funds of Funds

HarbourVest Partners, Boston

HarbourVest Partners collected at least $245.1 million for its latest real-assets vehicle, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund-of-funds manager didn't indicate a total offering amount for HarbourVest Real Assets Fund III LP and a related pool, HarbourVest Real Assets III Feeder Fund LP, in the filings. HarbourVest, through its real-assets program, focuses on investments in sectors such as energy, power, infrastructure and natural resources. The firm in 2015 hired Kevin Warn-Schindel as a managing director on its secondary investment team to lead the program. In addition to its real-assets fundraising efforts, HarbourVest in November said it held a final close for its latest secondary fund, Dover Street IX LP, at $4.77 billion. The vehicle exceeded a $3.6 billion target. In April, the firm said it collected 375 million Canadian dollars for HarbourVest Canada Growth Fund, a venture-capital fund of funds that invests in vehicles from Canadian managers as well as U.S. managers that invest in Canadian businesses.

Mezzanine & Other Debt

Angelo Gordon & Co., New York

Angelo Gordon & Co. gathered at least $1.08 billion for its latest direct-lending vehicle, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. It isn't clear from the filings whether the firm has held a final closing for AG Direct Lending Fund II LP and four affiliated vehicles. The firm's direct-lending group finances leveraged buyouts, recapitalizations, add-on acquisitions and other private-equity transactions, generally lending to companies with $3 million to $50 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. Angelo Gordon in 2014 hired a pair of senior executives from Madison Capital Funding, a subsidiary of New York Life Investment Management LLC, to establish its direct-lending business. The group began raising its first direct-lending fund in 2015, though it is unclear from regulatory filings how much the vehicle ultimately gathered.

CRG, Houston

CRG is approaching a final close on its third credit fund, said two people familiar with the matter. The health care-focused firm collected $1.1 billion of commitments for the fund, above its target of $1 billion, one of the people said. The vehicle has a $1.25 billion hard cap, the person added. One of the people said the firm's third fund will invest globally as it continues to identify opportunities throughout North America, Asia and Europe. CRG raised $805 million of equity for Capital Royalty Partners II LP in 2013 and said it expected total investible capital for the vehicle to exceed $1 billion with debt. The firm primarily provides structured debt investments, underwrites senior-secured loans and invests in companies that reduce health-care costs or provide health-care services that cater to personalized medicine and shifting demographics. It targets investments of $20 million to $300 million.

Lone Star Funds, Dallas

Lone Star Funds completed the final closing for debt funds Lone Star Fund X (U.S.) LP and Lone Star Fund X (Bermuda) LP with a total of about $5.6 billion. Commitments to the pool exceeded the Dallas firm's original target of $5 billion. Lone Star in April said it closed Lone Star Real Estate Fund V (U.S.) LP and Lone Star Real Estate Fund V (Bermuda) LP, its fifth dedicated commercial real-estate vehicle, at about $5.8 billion, exceeding a $5 billion target and reaching its $5.5 billion hard cap for third-party commitments. The firm in 2014 collected about $7.4 billion including internal commitments, for its ninth fund, Lone Star Fund IX LP, Private Equity Analyst previously reported, citing a person familiar with the matter. Since the establishment of its first fund in 1995, Lone Star has managed 17 private-equity funds with aggregate capital commitments exceeding $70 billion.

Neuberger Berman Group, New York

Neuberger Berman Group gathered at least $235.4 million for NB-Athyrium Opportunities Fund III LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Neuberger Berman's alternatives business in 2009 formed a relationship with health-care investor Athyrium Capital Management to pursue life-sciences investments and later expanded the relationship to include additional accounts and funds. Athyrium and Neuberger Berman closed on more than $1.2 billion for Athyrium Opportunities Fund II LP in 2015. Athyrium makes structured-capital investments in the health-care sector, committing $25 million to $150 million per transaction.

Venture Capital

Alberta Enterprise Corp., Edmonton, Alberta

Alberta Enterprise Corp. launched its second early-stage angel co-investment fund, Accelerate Fund II, with a $10 million investment. The fund will be managed by venture-capital firm Yaletown Partners and A100, a group of technology founders and executives in Alberta. The vehicle will invest $500,000 in companies within sectors including energy technology, information technology, communications, life sciences, agriculture and food. Accelerate Fund I was launched with a $10 million investment from Alberta Enterprise in 2012.

Alpha Venture Partners, New York

Alpha Venture Partners closed on $25 million toward Alpha Venture Partners Fund II, a $100 million vehicle, said Steve Brotman, founder and managing partner of Alpha. The firm enables early-stage venture firms to reinvest in their growing portfolio companies during later rounds by transferring capital into a special purpose vehicle set up by the early-stage investor. The special purpose vehicle provides the early-stage investors with the means to participate in rounds that would otherwise require capital beyond their investment capabilities. In addition to the $25 million the firm has already closed on for the fund, Alpha has received soft commitments for $25 million more, Mr. Brotman said. Pritzker Group is an anchor limited partner in both of Alpha's funds, he said.

ARCH Venture Partners, Chicago

ARCH Venture Partners, a life sciences and technology investor, has raised $408.4 million toward a new fund that passed the initial amount it was seeking, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The offering amount for ARCH Venture Fund IX LP was increased to $550 million from a $400 million amount recorded in a May SEC filing. The firm also filed with the SEC for ARCH Venture Fund IX Overage LP, which has a $450 million offering amount. No capital commitments were recorded in the filing.

Ascension Ventures, Clayton, Mo.

Ascension Ventures, a subsidiary of nonprofit health system Ascension Health Alliance, closed its fourth fund, CHV IV LP, at $255 million. The firm invests in health-care companies that provide services and software that help health systems in sectors such as health-care information technology, health-care services and medical devices. Its latest fund includes 12 health systems besides Ascension as limited partners, including Adventist Health System, Children's Medical Center of Dallas and Inova Health System.

Biomatics Capital Partners, Seattle

Biomatics Capital Partners collected at least $200 million for Biomatics Capital Partners LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Fund managers include Boris Nikolic and Julie Sunderland, according to the filing.

Draper Nexus Venture Partners, San Mateo, Calif.

Draper Nexus Venture Partners, backed by billionaire investor Tim Draper, has loaded up with $175 million in cash for two new funds. The vehicles, Draper Nexus Technology Partners II LP and Draper Nexus Technology Partners Japan II, are expected to unleash about two-thirds of the capital into U.S.-based startups and the other third into those based in Japan, said Managing Director Q Motiwala. About 80% to 85% of the limited partners from its previous Draper Nexus fund have returned to the new vehicles, said Mr. Motiwala. The firm's limited partners include Panasonic Corp., Fujitsu Ltd. and Kyocera International Inc.

Epic Ventures, Salt Lake City

Epic Ventures collected at least $51.6 million for its fifth fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The filing didn't specify a total offering amount for Epic Ventures V LP. Epic was more than halfway toward the $150 million target of its fourth fund in May 2009, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. The firm's recent investments include Recursion Pharmaceuticals LLC, which develops therapies for rare diseases from drugs that other pharmaceutical companies decided not to pursue, and Signal Digital Inc., whose technology enables advertisers to track people across devices.

ff Venture Capital, New York

Ff Venture Capital is aiming to raise $150 million for ff Graphite (V) Venture Capital Fund LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Ff, which invests in seed- and early-stage rounds in the U.S. as well as countries including Canada and Israel, has placed bets in businesses including wealth management startup Addepar Inc. and crowdfunding company Indiegogo Inc.

Founder Collective, Cambridge, Mass.

Founder Collective closed a third fund, Founder Collective III LP, with $75 million to invest at the seed stage. The vehicle is roughly the same size as the firm's previous fund, which closed in 2012. Founder Collective invests $200,000 to $2 million at the seed stage and also writes checks for Series A investments. The firm has backed highly valued startups including ride-sharing business Uber Technologies Inc., media company BuzzFeed Inc. and Cruise Automation Inc., which was bought by General Motors Co. in 2015.

Four Rivers Group, San Francisco

Four Rivers Group, which invests in enterprise software, raised at least $87 million for Four Rivers Partners IV LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm's investments include publicly traded digital security firm FireEye Inc., backup storage company Carbonite Inc. and information-technology analytics company Numerify Inc. The firm raised at least $67 million for its third fund in 2014, according to an SEC filing.

Information Venture Partners, Toronto

Information Venture Partners has closed on $106 million for Information Venture Partners Fund II LP, surpassing a $100 million target. The firm, which focuses on fintech and enterprise software startups in North America, looks to make Series A investments. In October 2014, Information Venture said it launched its first fund, at which time it also said it was fundraising for its second fund. It isn't clear on what amount the firm closed on for its first fund. Manulife Financial Corp., which operates as John Hancock in the U.S., joined the fund as a limited partner. Previously announced limited partners include BDC Capital, Export Development Bank of Canada, Royal Bank of Canada and Power Financial Corp. Information Venture Partners was spun out of RBC Venture Partners in 2014.

Microsoft Corp., Redmond, Wash.

Microsoft Ventures, the venture unit of Microsoft Corp., launched a new fund to invest in artificial-intelligence startups. The unit also said the new fund had made its first investment, in Element AI, a Montreal incubator that helps organizations embrace an AI-first strategy. Microsoft Ventures, which launched in 2013, didn't say how much it would deploy through the new fund. Nagraj Kashyap, corporate vice president of Microsoft Ventures, in a blog post described the AI fund as an expansion of the venture unit's mission to fund startups that assist enterprises. Its investments will be around the same size as those made in the past by Microsoft Ventures, according to the post.

Partners Innovation Fund, Cambridge, Mass.

Partners Innovation Fund, which focuses on technologies emerging from the Partners HealthCare health system research community, collected at least $38.5 million for Partners Innovation Fund II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. Partners Innovation Fund was established in 2008 with a $35 million commitment from Massachusetts General Hospital and Brigham and Women's Hospital. Partners HealthCare in January 2016 said the two investors provided commitments to Fund II that would bring total assets under management to $100 million.

Playground Global, Palo Alto, Calif.

Andy Rubin, the creator of Google Inc. parent Alphabet Inc.'s Android mobile operating system, is looking to raise up to $500 million for Playground Ventures II LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Mr. Rubin in 2015 co-founded hardware incubator Playground Global and raised its first fund, which closed with $300 million. Mr. Rubin that year told The Wall Street Journal the incubator had backing from investors including Hon Hai Precision Industry Co., Google, Hewlett-Packard Co. and Tencent Holdings Ltd. Playground's investments have included startups working on drones, augmented reality and online software focused on traffic.

Red Cedar Ventures, Lansing, Mich.

Michigan State University Foundation, a nonprofit organization that supports Michigan State University initiatives, formed Red Cedar Ventures, a venture investing unit that seeks to support startups coming out of the university. The arm is comprised of a preseed fund and an opportunity fund. Red Cedar's preseed fund will invest $750,000 annually altogether, said a spokesman for the vehicle. The university foundation also has committed $5 million to Red Cedar Opportunity Fund, which will make follow-on investments over the next three to five years, he said. The funds will be overseen by the foundation's board, and a management team will oversee daily operations with advice from venture-capital investors and student venture analysts from Michigan State University's Eli Broad College of Business.

RiverVest Venture Partners, St. Louis

RiverVest Venture Partners is looking to raise $125 million for a new fund called 3X5 RiverVest Fund II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't disclose how much it raised so far for the pool. The fund recently invested in an $86 million Series A round for Tioma Therapeutics Inc., a RiverVest portfolio company developing cancer therapies. RiverVest closed its third main fund at $80.2 million in 2015. The firm is an early-stage life sciences investor but also backs later-stage companies.

Slow Ventures, San Francisco

Slow Ventures, a seed-stage investor that can also invest smaller amounts for initial capital, gathered $145 million for its third fund. The firm is maintaining more than half of its new fund for follow-on funding, according to blog post from the firm. In addition to traditional endowments and foundations, Slow Ventures' limited partners include more than 100 technology executives and investors. Founded in 2011, the firm has nearly $250 million under management.

Spark Capital, Boston

Spark Capital has raised $1 billion for two new venture funds: a $400 million early-stage vehicle named Spark Capital V LP and a $600 million growth fund, Spark Capital Growth Fund II LP. The firm previously raised a $450 million early-stage fund and a $375 million growth fund in 2014. Spark has five funds with $3 billion under management. The firm saw a quick exit from autonomous driving startup Cruise Automation after investing in the company in 2015 as part of a $12.5 million funding round. Cruise Automation was acquired by General Motors Co. about six months later for around $1 billion.

SV Angel, San Francisco

SV Angel raised at least $53.1 million for a new fund, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm initially listed a fundraising target of $46 million for SV Angel VI LP, according to a March SEC filing. SV Angel has invested in companies including Airbnb Inc., which enables people to rent dwellings such as rooms or entire apartments; cloud-storage company Dropbox Inc.; and social website Pinterest Inc.

Telescope Partners, San Francisco

Mickey Arabelovic, a former Sequoia Capital partner and a founder of Telescope Partners, is forming his own fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Mr. Arabelovic aims to raise $75 million for Telescope Partners I LP, according to the filing. No capital commitments were recorded for the vehicle.

Tola Capital, Seattle

Tola Capital, a firm that invests primarily in enterprise-software businesses, raised $295 million for Tola Ventures LP, said Managing Director Sheila Gulati. Ms. Gulati said the firm invests in companies anywhere but its proximity to Microsoft Corp. and Amazon.com Inc. makes it an ideal investor in cloud startups. Tola Capital plans to continue to invest in cloud-based enterprise software companies, but also is looking at the influence of artificial intelligence on marketing, the supply chain and manufacturing, she said.

Ulu Ventures, Redwood City, Calif.

Ulu Ventures, an early-stage investor that backs internet-enabled consumer and business services companies, is seeking $50 million for its Ulu Ventures Fund II LP, according to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing. The firm didn't record any capital commitments for the vehicle in the filing. Among the firm's portfolio companies are Bridge US, a startup developing software to manage immigration paperwork, and Palantir Technologies Inc., a heavily funded data-analytics company.

Wing Venture Capital, Menlo Park, Calif.

Wing Venture Capital raised $250 million for a second fund, Wing Two, which will address early-stage enterprise businesses. The firm's first fund invested in 13 companies and ran a seed-investment program. Peter Wagner, a Wing Venture founder, said the new fund would invest in 20 to 25 companies, deploying about $10 million to $15 million per company. The firm's limited partners include university endowments and private foundations.

EUROPE

Buyouts

Access Capital Partners, Paris

Access Capital Partners raised  226 million ($239.9 million) for its latest European co-investment fund. The vehicle, Access Co-Investment Fund Buy-out Europe, closed above a  150 million target. The firm, which has invested almost half the fund, aims to co-invest in 15 business in total across Europe within the business services, consumer services, manufacturing, equipment rental and food sectors. Access, which has European private-equity, private-debt and infrastructure strategies, makes primary and secondary investments as well as participates in co-investments.
