Charterhouse Capital Partners held a final closing for Charterhouse Capital Partners X at  2.3 billion ($2.44 billion), according to a statement from law firm Simpson Thacher & Bartlett LLP, which represented Charterhouse in its latest fundraising effort. The firm was targeting  3 billion for the fund, Private Equity Analyst sister publication Private Equity News previously reported, citing two people familiar with the matter. Charterhouse wrapped up fundraising for the vehicle's predecessor in 2009 with  4 billion in commitments.

Clessidra SGR, Milan

Clessidra SGR completed fundraising for its third private-equity fund, Clessidra Capital Partners 3. In a letter sent to investors on Dec. 5 that was viewed by Private Equity Analyst sister publication Private Equity News, the firm's chairman Carlo Pesenti said it had collected  607.3 million ($652.1 million) for its latest vehicle about 40% below Clessidra's original fund target. The firm initially set out to collect  1 billion, but reduced this target to  800 million after its founder Claudio Sposito died in January. Italmobiliare Group, an investment holding of Italy's Pesenti family, subsequently bought 99% of the firm from Mr. Sposito's widow in May. The group pledged  90.3 million to Clessidra's third fund, Mr. Pesenti said, adding the fund size would enable the team to focus on companies with enterprise values of  150 million to  1 billion. He added that the current fund size means that the firm will be able to provide investors with more co-investment opportunities. According to the leaked letter, the firm has already offered investors  185 million of co-investments alongside its third fund.

GHO Capital Partners, London

GHO Capital Partners raised  660 million ($699.6 million) for its debut fund, GHO Capital Fund I LP. The vehicle, which targets health-care deals, closed above a  500 million target. The firm collected capital from investors world-wide, including pension funds, sovereign-wealth funds, insurance companies, funds of funds, family offices and high-net-worth individuals. Dennis Gillings and Mireille Gillings, co-founders of the firm, made a cornerstone investment in the fund, said Executive Partner and founder Alan MacKay. GHO will invest in nine to 12 European health-care businesses with enterprise values of  100 million to  400 million. More than 40% of the capital came from investors in North America, while 31% came from Europe and 28% from the rest of the world.

Idinvest Partners, Paris

Idinvest Partners held a  250 million ($264.9 million) first close on its second growth-capital fund, Idinvest Growth Fund II. The fund is targeting  350 million to  400 million and aims to hold a final closing in the first quarter of 2017. The vehicle will invest in small to midsize European businesses within the digital, health and energy sectors as well as those that produce technologies contributing to the development of smart cities. It has attracted support from investors which backed previous Idinvest funds as well as new limited partners in the Middle East. The fund will invest  10 million to  20 million of equity for minority stakes, according to a spokeswoman for the firm.

IK Investment Partners, London

IK Investment Partners held a final closing for its eighth midcap buyout fund, IK VIII Fund, at  1.85 billion ($1.96 billion). The fund, which received commitments from institutional backers in North America, Europe, the Middle East and Asia, exceeded its  1.6 billion target. Although the fund is larger than its  1.4 billion predecessor, the firm's investment strategy has remained largely unchanged. IK VIII will seek majority stakes in companies worth up to  500 million across IK's core markets: Scandinavia, France, Germany, Austria, Switzerland and the Benelux region. The vehicle, which held a first close on  1.3 billion in August, has made three investments to date. One of these was an undisclosed investment in kitchen sink manufacturer Granite Holding that the firm announced in November. Pro Private Markets
VC Profile: Space Could Be the Next Frontier for Venture Investors

By Tomio Geron 
712 words
3 January 2017
15:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Starburst Venture, a new firm focused on aerospace and aviation startups, has raised $200 million for its first fund, joining an increasing number of investors launching into the sector.

The fund, which will be based in San Francisco, is affiliated with Starburst Accelerator, the aerospace and aviation accelerator with locations in Los Angeles, Paris, Munich and Singapore. The accelerator doesn't take equity stakes in its startups.

Francois Chopard, the founder of Starburst, will be one of the general partners, along with another GP yet to be named and an expected staff of 15 people, Mr. Chopard said. The fund expects to start investing in the first quarter of 2017.

"We're just at the beginning of a new trend," Mr. Chopard said. "Aerospace is in a phase of maturity like biotech was 15 years ago."

Space investing has drawn increased interest recently from investors, from existing technology investors to others focusing more specifically on space tech or frontier tech more generally. Seraphim Capital is one firm focusing on space, while other established firms such as Founders Fund and Draper Fisher Jurvetson have invested in Elon Musk's Space Exploration Technologies Corp. also known as SpaceX. Lux Capital has made investments in startups Planet Labs Inc., Kymeta Corp. and Orbital Insight Inc. Y Combinator has also backed space startups.

Starburst Venture

The Firm

San Francisco-based Starburst Venture is affiliated with Starburst Accelerator, a space-focused accelerator active in Los Angeles, Paris, Munich and Singapore.

The Strategy

The firm seeks to invest in a range of aerospace- and aviation-related startups, which could range from components or software related to flight or actual space companies.

The Fund

The firm raised $200 million for its debut fund.

More generally, tech executives have shown massive interest in space. Amazon.com Inc. Chief Executive Jeff Bezos has Blue Origin, a rocket startup that in October had a successful rocket launch test. Richard Branson's Virgin Galactic aims to offer space tourism.

In the past, investors have been wary of the high capital costs and risks of space startups. But costs are dropping and an ecosystem of commercial space technology is forming to help startups develop, said Jude Gomila, an angel investor who has invested in four space startups. Google's acquisition of Skybox Imaging for $500 million in 2014 also showed that large exits are possible in the sector. "Now it's more on the mind of someone who comes to Silicon Valley," Mr. Gomila said. "There is a changing mindset with entrepreneurs. Also the web is hitting maturity in some capacities. People are looking for new frontiers to do companies."

Leonie Hill Capital, a Singapore-based private-equity firm, is the anchor investor in Starburst Venture, said Mr. Chopard, who declined to disclose the amount invested. Leonie Hill has invested as a fund of funds in other firms such as 500 Startups, Mr. Chopard said.

Starburst Venture, which will have its fund registered in Singapore, will invest in Series A and B deals in anything space- or aviation-related as well as supporting technologies such as artificial intelligence or big data. Starburst will invest in companies beyond those in its accelerator.

Starburst is seeking additional limited partners, mainly in high-net-worth family funds and sovereign-wealth funds, as well as potentially some of its existing corporate partners.

At an accelerator demo day this past June at the NASA Ames Conference Center in Moffett Field, Calif., startups pitched companies in areas such as deep space mining, supersonic aircraft, vertical landing hybrid helicopter-jets, rocket-launching technology, new satellite communications systems, drone anticollision technology and even a new type of airline seat to give travelers more "elbow room."

Originally from Paris, Starburst Accelerator, which launched in October 2015, holds events for its companies to pitch in Munich, Paris, Los Angeles and Singapore. When startups pitch at these events, the experts in attendance score the companies, and Starburst uses this data to determine which companies it accepts into its program. Attendees included executives from corporations such as Lockheed Martin and Raytheon Co., venture-capital firms and public research agencies.

Pro Private Markets
VC Outlook: IPO Outlook Might Not Be All Rainbows and Unicorns; Will Wall Street ride Sand Hill's unicorns to the end of the rainbow? Venture-capital investors will certainly be watching how bankers price the likes of Spotify and Dropbox for IPOs.

1,561 words
3 January 2017
15:38
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2017 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Here's the billion-dollar question: Will Wall Street discount Sand Hill's once-hot startups in initial public offerings?

For investors in billion-dollar "unicorn" startups companies facing cash burns and liquidity demands from all directions the question looms as pressure for some to go public runs high.

See also

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/outlook-pe-fundraising-expected-to-brush-off-challenges-in-2017-1483455024"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/secondary-outlook-secondary-buyers-predict-gps-will-drive-more-deals-in-2017-1483455222"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/lp-outlook-investor-capital-keeps-private-equity-party-going-for-now-1483454937"

Spotify AB raised convertible debt in March, agreeing to terms that set the clock ticking on an IPO in return for the $1 billion in financing that enabled the $8.5 billion-valued company to take money without a valuation reset.

"It's not only huge, but it had some terms that they had to go public," said Matt Murphy, a managing director at Menlo Ventures.

About 151 companies are valued at a billion dollars or more, according to The Wall Street Journal's Billion Dollar Startup Club, and many that raised late-stage funding in a frothy investing environment over the past several years now face cash concerns and potential lowered valuations.

Investors will be closely watching Spotify and other highly valued startups to move forward with IPOs for whether their share pricings come in below their last private financing prices. Many face this splash of cold water as a hangover sets in following recent venture investor euphoria.

"There's a disconnect between the private venture capital-type markets and the public markets," said Martin Wellington, a managing partner at law firm Sidley Austin LLP in Palo Alto, Calif.

Dropbox Inc., the 10-year old cloud storage startup, is another company with a heady valuation, raising $500 million in debt financing in 2014. Dropbox was valued at $10 billion in its last funding round, but publicly traded private-equity investor BlackRock Inc. has since written it down 45%.

Amid renewed market skepticism, flat and down private financing rounds are more in vogue these days, reflecting the desire by venture investors to come to grips with valuation compression.

Still, the 2017 IPO market is expected to see offerings from highly valued startups including Snap Inc., Blue Apron Inc. and Bloom Energy Corp. Many more likely have filed confidentially with the Securities and Exchange Commission under a provision of the Jumpstart Our Business Startups Act and remain under the radar.

But the IPO market remains a concern. The 37 U.S. venture-backed IPOs through Dec. 14 put 2016 on pace for the lowest level of public offerings since the end of the financial crisis in 2009, according to data from VentureSource, a unit of Private Equity Analyst publisher Dow Jones & Co.

Many investors remain unworried about how heated valuations play out in public markets, saying that those who invested earlier made money. But the late-stage investors who helped fuel the valuations frenzy for startups in 2014 and 2015 are the ones positioned for less stellar gains.

"If the unicorn valuation is disconnected from what the market valuation will be and it's too high, then somebody is gonna get hurt," said Mr. Wellington.

Mutual funds and other institutions who bought in at rich valuations might see their shares underwater on the public markets, triggering ratchet protections to be made whole if written into the investing terms. Venture investors say the institutional set of investors has become more selective on backing companies recently in the wake of valuation concerns.

That factor already has been borne out on investments in startups. The median premoney valuation for late-stage rounds fell to $148.6 million in the first half of 2016 from $269.3 million in 2015, according to VentureSource. The median premoney valuation for Series D or higher rounds dropped to less than $200 million in the second quarter of 2016 from a peak of about $400 million in the third quarter of 2015, according to law firm Cooley LLP.

Megavaluation startups have fallen out of favor in IPO markets. The U.S. median postmoney valuation of startups at IPO plummeted to $139.6 million in 2016 as of Dec. 14, 69.3% lower than the 10-year high of $455.2 million in 2011, according to VentureSource.

Indeed, venture-capital investors have throttled back on late-stage funding. In the third quarter of last year, the median size of later-stage rounds declined to $8.45 million, the lowest since the third quarter of 2010, according to VentureSource. In the same period in 2015, the median amount invested was pushed up to $18 million in the clamor to give cash to companies with valuations of $1 billion or more.

Crossover investors aren't the only ones chasing unicorns. Venture-capital firm Sequoia Capital, whose megahits include the likes of Airbnb Inc. and Dropbox, holds the title for the largest stable of unicorns, according to data from the Journal's Billion Dollar Startup Club. Sequoia is trailed by the likes of Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, Accel Partners and Andreessen Horowitz in billion-dollar startup stakes.

To be sure, many investors see the megavaluations of startups such as Uber Technologies Inc., Snap and Airbnb and plenty of others as the next big ticket. Some of the late-stage investors who picked up golden lottery tickets in these companies could expect to see rewards at the end of the rainbow.

The question is whether 2017 will be more receptive for tech IPOs. A handful of IPOs went out in recent months that have done well, offering hopes for the return of the public markets. Shares of Crispr Therapeutics AG, Coupa Software Inc. and Nutanix Inc. have stayed aloft in post-IPO trading, up 39.4%, 46.6% and 79.6%, respectively, as of Dec. 16, according to data from the WSJ Pro Venture-Backed Post-IPO Index.

"Hopefully, your private valuation is not completely out of line with how the public markets might view your company," said Eric Paley, a managing partner at Founder Collective.

Private markets are showing some signs of IPO-related interest in shares, too. On the secondary market for private shares, demand has soared for companies thought to be planning an IPO. "The companies that are being discussed as potential near-term IPO companies are seeing increased interest on our platform," said Greg Brogger, chief executive of private securities broker SharesPost Inc.

But even when unicorns see a strong public pop, gains don't always hold. The 21 U.S. unicorns that went public between 2011 and 2015 saw their shares close an average of 45% above the offer price on the first day of trading. Six months after the IPO, their shares traded only 33% above the offer price on average, according to data provider Dealogic Ltd.

"The question is what happens when a private investor wants to get out," said Kathleen Smith, co-founder of IPO researcher Renaissance Capital.

Venture firms, often mandated to get liquidity soon after a company goes public, hope those gains will hold through the six-month lock-up period after an IPO or at least long enough for them to cash out in an authorized secondary public offering before the lock-up ends. Some recent highly valued companies have seen a big drop when they announce that venture-capital firms plan to sell.

When Twilio Inc. hit the market in May as the first large tech IPO in months, its stock surged in part because it attracted interest from retail investors. In October, the company announced plans to let private investors sell shares in a secondary offering, and its share price fell 12% in one day, then continued to slide. Four weeks later, the stock traded 45% below its price before it announced the secondary offering.

Who can make it to an IPO is the question. IPO veterans expect only those unicorns with the strongest business fundamentals will take chances in the first few months of 2017.

"It's much harder to market a growth-at-any-cost kind of deal [since 2016] than it would have been in 2015 or 2014," said Mike Tuchen, chief executive of Talend Inc., a big-data integration company that went public in July with positive cash flow.

Two unicorns known to be waiting in the wings Snap and Blue Apron are expected to have numbers in line for Wall Street. Snap told investors last year it expected revenue between $250 million and $350 million in 2016. Blue Apron says it ships 8 million meal kits per month. At $10 each, that's nearly $1 billion in annual revenue.

Some unicorns are dropping IPO hints, saying their business is sound. In June, Dropbox Chief Executive Drew Houston announced the company was cash-flow positive. With that news, Dropbox signaled to the markets that its business was working, said Menlo Ventures' Mr. Murphy, who is not an investor in the company.

"There's going to be more pressure for them to go public. The question is can they get comfortable with going out with a down value?" he said.

Pro Private Markets
In Their Own Words: Limited Partners/Placement Agents; In this final installment of a three-part series, four members of the limited-partner and fundraising communities share their insights on the past year and projections for the year ahead.

827 words
30 December 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

LEE GARDELLA, managing director and head of risk management, Adveq

What surprised you the most in 2016?

How conventional wisdom lacks wisdom.

What do you think will be the biggest challenge your corner of the industry will face in 2017?

GPs staying disciplined on valuations in this market, given the need to deploy capital.

How do you see the abundance of dry powder affecting LP commitments to the asset class?

Will have no effect on LP commitments. Allocations to private equity are increasing and more capital will be coming into the market. Dry powder levels will continue to increase in 2017.

JENNIFER RINEHART, partner and chief executive, Acalyx Advisors Inc.

What surprised you the most in 2016?

More institutional investors have become strikingly quick to decide which GPs they will seriously pursue. In a congested market where everyone has a sense of urgency, investors have been compelled to take a more binary early view, which they subsequently confirm or refute by dedicating resources to an extensive diligence process. Triage often weighs portfolio fit, runway for diligence and potential allocation as key factors in an initial decision. This diverges from the traditional approach wherein limited partners reviewed a broad set of facts and required longer lead time with a GP before committing to intensive diligence.

See also

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/in-their-own-words-corporate-finance-part-1-1482872319"

* "http://www.wsj.com/articles/in-their-own-words-corporate-finance-part-2-1483014604"

What do you think will be the biggest challenge your corner of the industry will face in 2017?

The general commoditization of PE ownership today is concerning a GP can outsource everything from the IT in your office, the data analytics to India, the search firms that find executives and the consultants who draft 100-day plans. Finding GPs who have their own point of view, approach and execution but who can also demonstrate potential to repeat, evolve and institutionalize is the challenge for 2017 and beyond.

How will the fundraising environment look different in 2017?

While I believe [2017] is likely to be similar [to 2016], the fundraising environment will change for the worse at some point in the future. I believe GPs who are actively building a better practice, being good partners to their constituents in all parts of the cycle and thinking about how to continually improve their platform will prevail.

GREGORY STENTO, managing director, HarbourVest Partners

What surprised you the most in 2016?

The resilience in private-equity fundraising. Despite market events and volatility, we're headed for a third straight year of more than $200 billion raised globally, levels we haven't seen consistently since 2008. The low-return environment is attracting more institutional and individual investors to the asset class.

What do you think will be the biggest challenge your corner of the industry will face in 2017?

Capital flowing into the industry could put pressure on returns and lead eager or inexperienced investors to overpay for deals. The best response is to focus on strong secular trends, build diverse portfolios and select high-quality managers who are price-disciplined.

What will be the biggest point of contention between limited partners and general partners going forward?

As both a GP and an LP, we have a unique view into this relationship. The real point of contention may actually be among LPs and the growing number of new investors trying to gain access to the best-performing GPs. This two-tiered market where some gain access and some don't is reflected in the rapid fundraising by certain managers who are able to raise larger funds and can find additional capital in the form of co-investment.

ASHBEL WILLIAMS, executive director and chief investment officer, State Board of Administration of Florida

What surprised you the most in 2016?

General partner discipline! The last few years have been characterized by record amounts of capital distributed back to limited partners and very successful fundraisings by GPs. Yet, in general, GPs have shown discipline when making new investments, evidenced by lower than expected deal volume and increasing amounts of dry powder in the industry.

What do you think will be the biggest challenge private-equity limited partners will face in 2017?

Can private equity maintain its discipline? Do GPs eventually succumb to the "lower for longer" (low growth, low interest rates) train of thinking and begin to rationalize paying record purchase price multiples for new investments? Does this come at the exact wrong time?

What will be the biggest point of contention between limited partners and general partners going forward?

Probably not anything new, but the alignment of interests will always be an area of concern and focus for LPs. Improved transparency will go a long way toward alleviating this concern.

Pro Private Markets
Lux Capital's Zavain Dar Discusses AI and the Future; Each year, WSJ Pro and its sister publication The Private Equity Analyst ask members of the private equity community to share their reflections on the past year and projections for the year ahead.

357 words
30 December 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

ZAVAIN DAR, principal, Lux Capital

What surprised you the most in 2016?

We started making artificial intelligence-related investments in 2014. 2016 surprised even the most bullish AI evangelists with exponential growth in [the] pace of commercial adoption, Fortune 500 recognition, entrepreneurial activity and venture investment in the emerging technology. If software is eating the world, artificial intelligence is serving it up on a silver platter indeed!

What do you think will be the biggest challenge your corner of the industry will face in 2017?

Mobile, social, on-demand, cloud and big data have proven to be the winning investment theses of the last 10 years. We're hitting diminishing returns in these saturated investment trends, and 2016 proved to be the inflection point as investor appetite and interest turned increasingly so to hardware, artificial intelligence and deep tech more broadly. With record levels of investment capital raised this last year, there's an increasing urgency and challenge on managers and the community at large to find this economic cycle's emerging opportunities to deploy capital with an eye for outsized potential.

A couple of science-focused startups were knocked off their pedestals in the past few months, including Theranos Inc. How did that influence your investing or the market for science-based tech overall?

Science-based investing lies in the DNA of our firm, arguably more so than any other existing active generalist venture firm. We didn't go chasing social when Instagram sold or Snapchat emerged, and we won't shy from true technology and science breakthrough when our peers and entrepreneurs misstep or falter. Regardless of where Theranos and others go and independent of where their historical legacies fall, the current situation is proving to be a healthy reminder for entrepreneurs and investors alike that true technology and science breakthrough is hard and requires thoughtful, diligent and oft difficult work.

MANSION --- Disaster Recovery Plan --- Catastrophic events can wreak havoc on a home, but owners with the resources and the fortitude to rebuild can end up with an even better house than before

By Nancy Keates 
1,441 words
30 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
M1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

When a 6.0-magnitude earthquake struck Napa, Calif., on Aug. 24, 2014, it pushed Jill and Steve Matthiasson's 110-year-old farmhouse off its foundation. The couple was unhurt, but their home -- set on 5 acres of vineyards for their winery -- suffered significant damage.

"We thought it was the end of our house and the end of our business," says Ms. Matthiasson, who is 54 years old.

Instead, friends, neighbors and a cousin who is a contractor all stepped up to help them rebuild their home even better than before. This one is bolted to its foundation with steel structural beams that can withstand seismic shaking and has an electrical system upgraded to meet code and new plumbing with flexible pipes instead of rigid pipes that could snap off in an earthquake.

The home also got a nicer kitchen, designed for Mr. Matthiasson as the "ultimate kitchen for cooking." In all, the project took about six months and cost $350,000.

"We are ready for the next earthquake," she says.

Hurricanes, wildfires, earthquakes, floods. An average of 120 major disasters have struck somewhere in the U.S. every year since 1996, according to the Federal Emergency Management Agency. Catastrophic events can wipe out entire neighborhoods and cost millions of dollars in property damage. But those with the resources and the fortitude to rebuild can end up with an even better house, one that has stronger defenses against acts of nature and is more suited to families' changed needs.

The Memorial Day Flood of 2015 dumped more than a foot of water into Jennifer and Ben Deneen's one-story home in the Meyerland neighborhood of Houston. The couple and their two young children spent eight hours atop the kitchen counters until floodwaters receded. They used their swimming-pool raft to float their kids to a friend's house around the corner. Meanwhile, their furniture, carpets and anything else on the floor were ruined.

The Deneens considered repairs to the damaged floors and cabinets, but quickly realized they wanted a house that could withstand future floods. They also felt it was the right time to tear down the house and build a new one with the extra space they'd long wanted. The next week, work started on a house with two stories, five bedrooms and a price tag of around $1 million. After a week in a hotel and a year and a half in a two-bedroom, one-bathroom rental apartment, the family is back home.

"We decided if we were going to do it, we were going to do it right," says Ms. Deneen, 39, a hospital administrator.

The flood inundated 800 of the 2,400 homes in Meyerland and led to the construction of 150 new homes, according to Ed Wolff, president of Beth Wolff Realtors who lives around the corner from Ms. Deneen. A study his firm did of the effects of the 2001 tropical storm Allison in Southeast Texas found that the values of homes in the affected neighborhoods dipped sharply initially, but after two years were substantially higher than before the flooding, with the square footage of the homes up significantly.

Based on that, Mr. Wolff decided to renovate his damaged house, a three-bedroom, 3,600-square-foot, ranch he and his wife bought for $389,000 in 2005. Removal of the damaged sheetrock and cabinets allowed them access to update electrical and plumbing systems. The newly configured kitchen got new cabinetry and flooring, as well as a pantry. They added drainage around the exterior of the home (about $10,000) to help guard against future water penetration in the home.

Initial improvements cost about $30,000. Then, with the $175,000 they got from insurance, plus another $70,000 of their own money, they undertook larger projects. Today, Mr. Wolff estimates his home is worth $700,000.

"We took advantage of the opportunity," he says.

Emergency assistance and insurance payouts typically require extensive paperwork that can delay reconstruction. And new building codes implemented after a natural disaster can further complicate rebuilding efforts. FEMA assistance is generally no more than a few thousand dollars and is just for initial help, not to "make people whole again," says Rafael Lemaitre, the agency's head of public affairs. "We're not going to redo a rec room," he adds. As a result, homeowners planning sweeping renovations know they'll need to personally finance many of the upgrades.

FEMA's goal is to transfer the risk of natural disasters away from taxpayers and toward the private sector, so it urges everyone to get insurance. That's not always so easy, says Tom Larsen, chief product architect for CoreLogic, a data-analytics company. While fires and tornadoes are usually covered under standard homeowner's policies, earthquakes, mudslides and flooding from extreme weather are generally not. These separate policies are often exorbitant and provide minimal payouts, Mr. Larsen says.

Stephanie Lofgren, 53, a retired college professor, and her husband, Craig, a management consultant, had to wait 18 months before they could start construction on their new vacation home on New Jersey's Long Beach Island after superstorm Sandy struck in 2012. At the time of the storm, they had just razed the $1.1 million home there they purchased in 2010, and construction was about to begin on their new home. The storm left them with what Ms. Lofgren calls "an expensive sandbox."

New structural and flood-level requirements enacted after the storm meant starting from scratch with the design. Instead of what architect Scott Specht calls a "pristine white modern" home, the second design of the house, though still decidedly modern, has more beach-durable cedar cladding and incorporates boat-building materials like a fiberglass roof. Exterior materials include stainless steel, stucco and concrete. What were three levels of living space became two, the media room shrunk and the swimming pool got scratched. "It forces you to reflect and prioritize," says Ms. Lofgren. The final square footage was 2,400 square feet instead of 2,900, and the cost went down about two-thirds, despite having to pay about $40,000 for redesigning and re-permitting.

Superstorm Sandy also drastically changed Bill Meyer's 2,000-square-foot ranch home in Monmouth Beach, N.J., when it split the foundation in two. It was declared beyond rehabilitation by FEMA. So Mr. Meyer, a 73-year-old marketing entrepreneur, hired architect Michael Graves to design something completely different: a 6,000-square-foot contemporary home with two master bedrooms and several decks. The design made it handicapped accessible in case the need arose in the future, Mr. Meyer says.

Architect Bernice Chen of Michael Graves Architecture, who took over the project after Mr. Graves died in 2015, says the need to protect against future storms, which required researching new materials, and the different zoning and environmental clearances made the house a challenge. Mr. Meyer collected various forms of assistance, including federal and local grants that totaled more than $150,000. The new house cost about $1 million to build.

Lynn and John Stasz had only minutes to evacuate the home they'd lived in for almost 30 years in the foothills of Boulder, Colo., during the Fourmile Canyon Fire of September 2010. The fire destroyed 70 homes, including their 1970s-era, 3,000-square-foot traditional home. The new house they built two years later, which cost about $1 million, is completely different. Architect Mark Alan Gerwing created a single-level, modern home with lots of floor-to-ceiling glass and patios to take advantage of the expansive mountain and city views that resulted after the fire wiped out the trees on their 35-acre lot. There is no wood on the exterior (it's all stone, stucco and metal) and no fireplaces; instead there's a courtyard with a water feature at the center.

The fire changed more than just the architecture of their house: It transformed the dynamic of the neighborhood, says Ms. Stasz, a 70 year-old business consultant. Before, the couple barely knew the names of their neighbors because the trees kept them isolated. After the fire, nearby families bonded over recovery efforts, going to community meetings about filing insurance claims and building codes.

Now they have lifelong friends. "We lost our home but we discovered community," says Ms. Stasz.
